Refutation of Objections to the Distinctness of Will
Refutation of objections that will is reducible to knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to objections that will might be reducible to knowledge, and why the distinctness of will is necessary.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not cause will; will is a distinct state, eternally additional to God's essence.

Some opponents argue that God's will is merely His knowledge of what is beneficial or His knowledge of when things will occur. This lesson examines and refutes these objections, concluding that will is a distinct, eternal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that knowledge of the benefit or knowledge of the time of occurrence is prior to will, implying will is not needed.
The phrase 'أو بأنه سيوجد في وقت كذا' introduces the alternative explanation.
2
Understand the response
The author rebuts by noting that the opponent may not concede the priority of such knowledge over will, and that knowledge of occurrence is not temporally prior.
3
Interpret 'knowledge follows occurrence'
This phrase means that knowledge corresponds to the known, not that it is temporally later. The known is the pattern for knowledge.
The author clarifies: 'ليس بمعنى تأخره عنه في الخارج البتة.'
4
Confirm the conclusion
The distinctness of will is necessary (daruriyya), and its eternity and addition to the essence are established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك أصحابنا بأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل، لا بد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص، وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالإرادة، وهو معنى واضح عند العقل، مغاير للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات شأنه التخصيص والترجيح لأحد طرفي المقدور من الفعل والترك على الآخر، وينبه على مغايرتها للقدرة أن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء بخلافها وللعلم أن مطلق العلم نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، والعلم بما فيه من المصلحة. أو بأنه سيوجد في وقت كذا سابق على الإرادة والعلم بوقوعه تابع للوقوع المتأخر عنها وفيه نظر. إذ قد لا يسلم الخصم سبق العلم بأنه يوجده في وقت كذا على (۳) إرادته ذلك، ولا تأخر علمه بوقوعه حالاً عن إرادته الوقوع حالاً. وما يقال أن العلم تابع للوقوع ، فمعناه أنه يعلم الشيء كما يقع ، وأن المعلوم هو الأصل في التطابق لأنه مثال وصورة له، لا بمعنى تأخره عنه في الخارج البتة. والحق أن مغايرة الحالة التي نسميها بالإرادة للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات ضرورية، ثم لقد تبين قدمها، وزيادتها على الذات
Our companions adhere to the fact that the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others, and in some times rather than others, while the relation of the essence to all is equal, must be due to an attribute whose function is specification, because specification without a specifier is impossible, and the impossibility of the Necessary needing in His agency a separate matter. And that attribute is called 'will'. It is a meaning clear to the intellect, distinct from knowledge, power, and other attributes. Its function is specification and weighting of one of the two sides of the possible—act or omission—over the other. It indicates its distinctness from power: that the relation of power to both sides is equal, unlike it; and from knowledge: that absolute knowledge's relation to all is equal, and knowledge of the benefit in it. Or that it will exist at a certain time is prior to the will, and knowledge of its occurrence follows the occurrence that is later than the will. But there is a problem: the opponent may not concede the priority of knowledge that He will bring it into existence at a certain time over His willing that, nor the posteriority of His knowledge of its occurrence at present to His willing its occurrence at present. And what is said that knowledge follows occurrence means that He knows the thing as it occurs, and that the known is the origin in correspondence because it is an example and image of it, not meaning its posteriority in external existence at all. The truth is that the distinctness of the state we call 'will' from knowledge, power, and other attributes is necessary. Then its eternity and its being additional to the essence are established.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof that God's will is a distinct eternal attribute. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) The specification of some opposites (e.g., creating the world at one time rather than another, or creating one thing rather than its opposite) cannot be due to the essence alone, because the essence's relation to both opposites is equal. A specifier is needed. (2) That specifier cannot be an external cause, because the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything external in His agency. Therefore, it must be an attribute inherent in Him, which is called 'will'. (3) This attribute is distinct from power because power is equally related to both opposites, while will specifies one. It is distinct from knowledge because knowledge is equally related to all things, while will involves a particular inclination. The author then addresses an objection: some might claim that will is nothing but knowledge of the benefit or knowledge of the time of occurrence. The author argues that knowledge of the benefit is not the same as will, because will involves a specific inclination. Regarding the timing, if someone says God knows He will bring something into existence at time t, and that knowledge is prior to will, the opponent may not concede that. The proper understanding of 'knowledge follows occurrence' is that knowledge corresponds to the known, not that it is temporally posterior. The conclusion is that the distinctness of will from other attributes is self-evident, and its eternity and addition to the essence are proven.

Key Terms
إرادة
علم
صفة
قدرة
تخصيص
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the Ash'ari author mean by saying 'knowledge follows occurrence'?
A) Knowledge is temporally after the event.
B) Knowledge corresponds to the known.
C) Knowledge causes the event.
D) Knowledge is the same as will.

Key Takeaway
The distinctness of will from all other attributes is rationally necessary, and its eternity is established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's will as merely His foreknowledge, thereby reducing its reality? How can you cultivate a deeper reverence for His will as a distinct, active attribute that truly brings things into existence?
?
The passage insists that God's knowledge corresponds to the object without temporal sequence. When you face trials, do you trust that every detail is specifically willed by Allah, or do you fall into thinking it is just 'known' but not intended? Will you commit to saying 'I believe in His will' with full conviction?
?
The author says the distinctness of will is rationally necessary. How does this truth strengthen your reliance on Allah’s decisions, especially when you do not see the wisdom behind them? What step will you take today to surrender more fully to His will?

Definitions of Divine Will Across Schools
Lists definitions of irada from various schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the variety of definitions of divine will (irada) proposed by different Islamic theological and philosophical schools.

Core Idea
Many schools defined irada differently—each reflecting their broader theological commitments.

Before delving into the Ash'ari proof, it is essential to survey the landscape of definitions of divine will. This lesson presents the views of various groups, from the self-subsistent attribute to knowledge of benefit, setting the stage for the Ash'ari alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation.
This is a list of definitions.
2
Identify each school's definition
Note the school or figure associated with each definition: Dirar, al-Najjar, philosophers, al-Kulabi, Mu'tazila.
3
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why different schools defined irada so differently, e.g., to avoid anthropomorphism or to align with their system.
This diversity shows the centrality of irada in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار نفس الذات، وعند النجار صفة سلبية هي الفاعل ليس بمكره ولا ساء، وعند الفلاسفة العلم بالنظام الأكمل، وعند الكلبي إرادته لفعله تعالى العلم به ولفعل غيره الأمر به، وعند المحققين من المعتزلة هي العلم بما في الفعل من المصلحة تمسك أصحابنا بأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل، لا بد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص، وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالإرادة، وهو معنى واضح عند العقل، مغاير للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات شأنه التخصيص والترجيح لأحد طرفي المقدور من الفعل والترك على الآخر، وينبه على مغايرتها للقدرة أن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء بخلافها وللعلم أن مطلق العلم نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، والعلم بما فيه من المصلحة. أو بأنه سيوجد في وقت كذا سابق على الإرادة والعلم بوقوعه تابع للوقوع المتأخر عنها وفيه نظر. إذ قد لا يسلم الخصم سبق العلم بأنه يوجده في وقت كذا على إرادته ذلك، ولا تأخر علمه بوقوعه حالاً عن إرادته الوقوع حالاً. وما يقال أن العلم تابع للوقوع ، فمعناه أنه يعلم الشيء كما يقع ، وأن المعلوم هو الأصل في التطابق لأنه مثال وصورة له، لا بمعنى تأخره عنه في الخارج البتة. والحق أن مغايرة الحالة التي نسميها بالإرادة للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات ضرورية، ثم لقد تبين قدمها، وزيادتها على الذات بمثل ما مرَّ في العلم والقدرة
It is a self-subsistent attribute, and according to Dirar it is the essence itself, and according to al-Najjar it is a negative attribute, that the agent is not coerced nor evil, and according to the philosophers it is knowledge of the most excellent order, and according to al-Kulabi His will for His own act is knowledge of it and for the act of another it is command thereof, and according to the verifiers of the Mu'tazila it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. Our colleagues hold that the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others and at some times rather than others, while the essence is equally related to all, necessarily requires an attribute whose function is specification, because specification without a specifier is impossible, and the Necessary Being cannot need an external matter in His agency. That attribute is called irada. It is a meaning clear to reason, distinct from knowledge, power, and all attributes; its function is specification and preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible, action and inaction, over the other. And it points to its distinctness from power: the relation of power to both sides is equal, unlike it. And from knowledge: absolute knowledge is equally related to all, and knowledge of the benefit therein. Or that it will exist at a certain time prior to irada, and knowledge of its occurrence follows the occurrence that is after it; but there is consideration. For the opponent may not concede the priority of knowledge that He will bring it into existence at a certain time over His willing that, nor the posteriority of His knowledge of its occurrence at the moment from His willing its occurrence at the moment. And what is said that knowledge follows occurrence means that He knows the thing as it occurs, and the known is the origin of correspondence because it is a likeness and form of it, not in the sense of its being posterior to it in external existence at all. The truth is that the distinctness of the state we call irada from knowledge, power, and all attributes is necessary. Then it has been shown to be pre-eternal and additional to the essence, just like what was said concerning knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof section. It begins by listing various definitions of divine will (irada) from different theological and philosophical schools: some say it is a self-subsistent attribute, Dirar says it is the essence itself, al-Najjar says it is a negative attribute, the philosophers say it is knowledge of the most excellent order, al-Kulabi says it is knowledge of His own act and command for others' acts, and the Mu'tazila say it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. Then the author states that the Ash'aris (أصحابنا) argue for a different understanding. They argue that the fact that one of two opposites occurs rather than the other, and at a specific time rather than another, while the essence is equally related to all, necessarily requires an attribute whose function is specification. Without a specifier, specification is impossible, and the Necessary Being cannot depend on an external factor in His agency. That attribute is called irada (will). This meaning is clear to reason and is distinct from knowledge, power, and other attributes. Its function is specification and preponderance of one side of the possible (action or inaction) over the other. The author then emphasizes its distinction from power: power is equally related to both sides, but irada is not. Similarly, knowledge is equally related to all, while irada specifies. Then he discusses a potential confusion about the relationship between knowledge and will: if knowledge precedes will, then will is not truly specifying; but the author notes that the opponent may not accept the priority of knowledge. He concludes by stating that the distinctness of the state called irada from knowledge, power, and other attributes is necessary, and it has been shown to be pre-eternal and additional to the essence, just as was established for knowledge and power.

Key Terms
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims define divine will in the same way.
Correction: In fact, there is significant diversity among schools, ranging from identifying will with essence to reducing it to knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is divine will?
A) A self-subsistent attribute
B) Knowledge of the most excellent order
C) The essence itself
D) A negative attribute

Key Takeaway
Divine will (irada) has been defined in multiple ways by different schools, from an essential attribute to mere knowledge of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will, which of these definitions resonates most with your own understanding? Are you willing to let go of a definition that has become comfortable if it does not align with revelation?
?
The philosophers defined irada as 'knowledge of the most excellent order'. Do you sometimes trust your own sense of order more than God's will, especially when His plans seem disorderly to you? What would it mean to surrender that?
?
Al-Najjar defined irada as a negative attribute: that God is not coerced nor evil. Have you ever blamed God or felt that He has wronged you? How does this negative definition challenge your heart's accusations against Him?

The Ash'ari Proof of Irada as a Distinct Attribute
Presents the Ash'ari argument from specification to prove irada as a distinct attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that divine will is a distinct attribute necessary for explaining why God creates specific things at specific times.

Core Idea
Specification of opposites requires a specifier—irada.

The Ash'aris argue that the existence of particular things and times, rather than others, cannot be explained by power or knowledge alone; it requires an attribute whose function is specification. This lesson presents that argument, which establishes irada as a distinct attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the logical flow.
2
Identify the premise
The premise is that the divine essence is equally related to all opposites, yet some occur and others do not.
Equal relation means no inherent preference.
3
Understand the need for a specifier
Since specification cannot happen without a specifier, and the specifier cannot be external, it must be an attribute of God.
This attribute is called irada.
4
Recognize its function
The function of irada is to preponderate one side over the other, making it distinct from power and knowledge.
Power relates equally, knowledge is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار نفس الذات، وعند النجار صفة سلبية هي الفاعل ليس بمكره ولا ساء، وعند الفلاسفة العلم بالنظام الأكمل، وعند الكلبي إرادته لفعله تعالى العلم به ولفعل غيره الأمر به، وعند المحققين من المعتزلة هي العلم بما في الفعل من المصلحة تمسك أصحابنا بأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل، لا بد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص، وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالإرادة، وهو معنى واضح عند العقل، مغاير للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات شأنه التخصيص والترجيح لأحد طرفي المقدور من الفعل والترك على الآخر، وينبه على مغايرتها للقدرة أن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء بخلافها وللعلم أن مطلق العلم نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، والعلم بما فيه من المصلحة. أو بأنه سيوجد في وقت كذا سابق على الإرادة والعلم بوقوعه تابع للوقوع المتأخر عنها وفيه نظر. إذ قد لا يسلم الخصم سبق العلم بأنه يوجده في وقت كذا على إرادته ذلك، ولا تأخر علمه بوقوعه حالاً عن إرادته الوقوع حالاً. وما يقال أن العلم تابع للوقوع ، فمعناه أنه يعلم الشيء كما يقع ، وأن المعلوم هو الأصل في التطابق لأنه مثال وصورة له، لا بمعنى تأخره عنه في الخارج البتة. والحق أن مغايرة الحالة التي نسميها بالإرادة للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات ضرورية، ثم لقد تبين قدمها، وزيادتها على الذات بمثل ما مرَّ في العلم والقدرة
It is a self-subsistent attribute, and according to Dirar it is the essence itself, and according to al-Najjar it is a negative attribute, that the agent is not coerced nor evil, and according to the philosophers it is knowledge of the most excellent order, and according to al-Kulabi His will for His own act is knowledge of it and for the act of another it is command thereof, and according to the verifiers of the Mu'tazila it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. Our colleagues hold that the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others and at some times rather than others, while the essence is equally related to all, necessarily requires an attribute whose function is specification, because specification without a specifier is impossible, and the Necessary Being cannot need an external matter in His agency. That attribute is called irada. It is a meaning clear to reason, distinct from knowledge, power, and all attributes; its function is specification and preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible, action and inaction, over the other. And it points to its distinctness from power: the relation of power to both sides is equal, unlike it. And from knowledge: absolute knowledge is equally related to all, and knowledge of the benefit therein. Or that it will exist at a certain time prior to irada, and knowledge of its occurrence follows the occurrence that is after it; but there is consideration. For the opponent may not concede the priority of knowledge that He will bring it into existence at a certain time over His willing that, nor the posteriority of His knowledge of its occurrence at the moment from His willing its occurrence at the moment. And what is said that knowledge follows occurrence means that He knows the thing as it occurs, and the known is the origin of correspondence because it is a likeness and form of it, not in the sense of its being posterior to it in external existence at all. The truth is that the distinctness of the state we call irada from knowledge, power, and all attributes is necessary. Then it has been shown to be pre-eternal and additional to the essence, just like what was said concerning knowledge and power.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof section. It begins by listing various definitions of divine will (irada) from different theological and philosophical schools: some say it is a self-subsistent attribute, Dirar says it is the essence itself, al-Najjar says it is a negative attribute, the philosophers say it is knowledge of the most excellent order, al-Kulabi says it is knowledge of His own act and command for others' acts, and the Mu'tazila say it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. Then the author states that the Ash'aris (أصحابنا) argue for a different understanding. They argue that the fact that one of two opposites occurs rather than the other, and at a specific time rather than another, while the essence is equally related to all, necessarily requires an attribute whose function is specification. Without a specifier, specification is impossible, and the Necessary Being cannot depend on an external factor in His agency. That attribute is called irada (will). This meaning is clear to reason and is distinct from knowledge, power, and other attributes. Its function is specification and preponderance of one side of the possible (action or inaction) over the other. The author then emphasizes its distinction from power: power is equally related to both sides, but irada is not. Similarly, knowledge is equally related to all, while irada specifies. Then he discusses a potential confusion about the relationship between knowledge and will: if knowledge precedes will, then will is not truly specifying; but the author notes that the opponent may not accept the priority of knowledge. He concludes by stating that the distinctness of the state called irada from knowledge, power, and other attributes is necessary, and it has been shown to be pre-eternal and additional to the essence, just as was established for knowledge and power.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power alone is sufficient to explain why God creates what He does.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that power is equally related to action and inaction, so it cannot by itself account for why one is chosen over the other; a specifying attribute (irada) is needed.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the Ash'ari proof for irada?
A) Knowledge necessitates action.
B) The divine essence is equally related to all opposites.
C) Power is limited.
D) Creation requires an external cause.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof for irada rests on the logical necessity of a specifying principle distinct from power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something you deeply hoped for does not happen, do you truly believe that God's will specified that outcome with wisdom, or do you feel that God was indifferent? How does this teaching of irada as an active attribute of specification reshape your trust in His decree?
?
If you believe that God's will is distinct and actively specifies each event, how does that change the way you pray? Are you asking God to change His will, or are you aligning your will with His? Examine your dua habits.
?
Consider a time you attributed something good to your own effort alone. Does this lesson challenge that assumption? How can you acknowledge God's specification (irada) in your daily life—not just in big events but in small choices?

Distinguishing Irada from Power and Knowledge
Argues for the distinctness of irada from power and knowledge and handles an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris further distinguish irada from power and knowledge, and how they respond to the objection that knowledge alone suffices for specification.

Core Idea
Irada is distinct from power and knowledge because it preponderates, while power and knowledge are universal.

Having established irada as a distinct attribute, the Ash'aris must show that it is not reducible to power or knowledge. This lesson presents the specific distinctions and addresses a key objection about the priority of knowledge, reaffirming the necessity of irada's distinctness.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinctions
Read how irada differs from power (equal relation) and from knowledge (universal vs. specific).
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that if God knows He will create at t, then knowledge seems to precede will, making will redundant.
3
Consider the response
The Ash'ari response clarifies that knowledge follows the known, not the other way around; thus knowledge does not negate the need for will.
The distinctness of irada is deemed necessary.
4
Conclude with the status of irada
The passage concludes that irada is pre-eternal and additional to the essence, like knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار نفس الذات، وعند النجار صفة سلبية هي الفاعل ليس بمكره ولا ساء، وعند الفلاسفة العلم بالنظام الأكمل، وعند الكلبي إرادته لفعله تعالى العلم به ولفعل غيره الأمر به، وعند المحققين من المعتزلة هي العلم بما في الفعل من المصلحة تمسك أصحابنا بأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل، لا بد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص، وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالإرادة، وهو معنى واضح عند العقل، مغاير للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات شأنه التخصيص والترجيح لأحد طرفي المقدور من الفعل والترك على الآخر، وينبه على مغايرتها للقدرة أن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء بخلافها وللعلم أن مطلق العلم نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، والعلم بما فيه من المصلحة. أو بأنه سيوجد في وقت كذا سابق على الإرادة والعلم بوقوعه تابع للوقوع المتأخر عنها وفيه نظر. إذ قد لا يسلم الخصم سبق العلم بأنه يوجده في وقت كذا على إرادته ذلك، ولا تأخر علمه بوقوعه حالاً عن إرادته الوقوع حالاً. وما يقال أن العلم تابع للوقوع ، فمعناه أنه يعلم الشيء كما يقع ، وأن المعلوم هو الأصل في التطابق لأنه مثال وصورة له، لا بمعنى تأخره عنه في الخارج البتة. والحق أن مغايرة الحالة التي نسميها بالإرادة للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات ضرورية، ثم لقد تبين قدمها، وزيادتها على الذات بمثل ما مرَّ في العلم والقدرة
It is a self-subsistent attribute, and according to Dirar it is the essence itself, and according to al-Najjar it is a negative attribute, that the agent is not coerced nor evil, and according to the philosophers it is knowledge of the most excellent order, and according to al-Kulabi His will for His own act is knowledge of it and for the act of another it is command thereof, and according to the verifiers of the Mu'tazila it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. Our colleagues hold that the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others and at some times rather than others, while the essence is equally related to all, necessarily requires an attribute whose function is specification, because specification without a specifier is impossible, and the Necessary Being cannot need an external matter in His agency. That attribute is called irada. It is a meaning clear to reason, distinct from knowledge, power, and all attributes; its function is specification and preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible, action and inaction, over the other. And it points to its distinctness from power: the relation of power to both sides is equal, unlike it. And from knowledge: absolute knowledge is equally related to all, and knowledge of the benefit therein. Or that it will exist at a certain time prior to irada, and knowledge of its occurrence follows the occurrence that is after it; but there is consideration. For the opponent may not concede the priority of knowledge that He will bring it into existence at a certain time over His willing that, nor the posteriority of His knowledge of its occurrence at the moment from His willing its occurrence at the moment. And what is said that knowledge follows occurrence means that He knows the thing as it occurs, and the known is the origin of correspondence because it is a likeness and form of it, not in the sense of its being posterior to it in external existence at all. The truth is that the distinctness of the state we call irada from knowledge, power, and all attributes is necessary. Then it has been shown to be pre-eternal and additional to the essence, just like what was said concerning knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof section. It begins by listing various definitions of divine will (irada) from different theological and philosophical schools: some say it is a self-subsistent attribute, Dirar says it is the essence itself, al-Najjar says it is a negative attribute, the philosophers say it is knowledge of the most excellent order, al-Kulabi says it is knowledge of His own act and command for others' acts, and the Mu'tazila say it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. Then the author states that the Ash'aris (أصحابنا) argue for a different understanding. They argue that the fact that one of two opposites occurs rather than the other, and at a specific time rather than another, while the essence is equally related to all, necessarily requires an attribute whose function is specification. Without a specifier, specification is impossible, and the Necessary Being cannot depend on an external factor in His agency. That attribute is called irada (will). This meaning is clear to reason and is distinct from knowledge, power, and other attributes. Its function is specification and preponderance of one side of the possible (action or inaction) over the other. The author then emphasizes its distinction from power: power is equally related to both sides, but irada is not. Similarly, knowledge is equally related to all, while irada specifies. Then he discusses a potential confusion about the relationship between knowledge and will: if knowledge precedes will, then will is not truly specifying; but the author notes that the opponent may not accept the priority of knowledge. He concludes by stating that the distinctness of the state called irada from knowledge, power, and other attributes is necessary, and it has been shown to be pre-eternal and additional to the essence, just as was established for knowledge and power.

Key Terms
إرادة
علم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is just another name for knowledge, since God knows what is best.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that knowledge is universal and does not by itself cause volition; will is the attribute that weighs one possibility over another.

Checkpoint

A) Power is eternal, irada is temporal.
B) Power relates equally to both sides, irada preponderates.
C) Power creates, irada commands.
D) Power is essential, irada is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Irada is distinct from power and knowledge because it specifies, and its distinctness is necessary; it is pre-eternal and additional to the essence.

The First Objection: Does God's Will Need an External Specifier?
Objection, answer, subsidiary objection, and reply about divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'aris answer the objection that God's will (irada) itself would require a specifier, and why they argue that irada specifies by its essence.

Core Idea
God's will specifies by its very nature, not by an external cause.

This passage presents the first major objection against the Ash'ari view of divine will: if irada is equally related to all opposites, why does it specify one? The objector claims this would lead to an infinite regress. The Ash'ari response is that irada is an attribute whose very function is specification, so it does not need another specifier. We'll also see the follow-up concern about human choice and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, answer, subsidiary objection, reply.
The passage uses technical terms like إرادة, مرجح, مخصص.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objector's reasoning: irada is equally related to action/inaction and all times, so its specification needs a preponderator, leading to infinite regress.
This mirrors the earlier objection about the world needing a creator.
3
Understand the Ash'ari answer
Note that the answer says irada specifies by its essence (لذاتها). It is the nature of irada to specify, even the equal or the preponderated.
This is a key theological distinction from other causal chains.
4
Understand the subsidiary objection and reply
The objector counters that if irada attaches, there is no ability to choose otherwise. The reply reaffirms the principle 'necessity from choice is pure choice' (الوجوب بالاختيار محض الاختيار).
This is a standard Ash'ari response to the problem of determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن نسبة الإرادة أيضاً إلى الفعل والترك وإلى جميع الأوقات على السواء، إذ لو لم يجز تعلقها بالطرف الآخر، وفي الوقت الآخر لزم نفس القدرة والاختيار، وإذا كانت على السواء فتعلقها بالفعل دون الترك، وفي هذا الوقت، دون غيره، يفتقر إلى مرجح ومخصص لامتناع وقوع الممكن بلا مرجح كما ذكرتم ، ويلزم تسلسل الإرادات . والجواب : أنها إنما تتعلق بالمراد لذاتها من غير افتقار إلى مرجح آخر لأنها صفة شأنها التخصيص والترجيح، ولو للمساوي، بل المرجوح، وليس هذا من وجود الممكن بلا موجد وترجيحه بلا مرجح في شيء.
فإن قيل : مع تعلق الإرادة لا يبقى التمكن من الترك وينتفي الاختيار.
قلنا : قد مرّه غير مرة أن الوجوب بالاختيار محض الاختيار.
The first [objection]: that the relation of irada also to action and inaction and to all times is equal, for if it were not possible for it to attach to the other side and at the other time, it would entail power and choice itself, and if it is equal, then its attaching to action rather than inaction, and at this time rather than another, needs a preponderator and a specifier, because it is impossible for a possible to occur without a preponderator as you mentioned, and an infinite regress of wills would follow. The answer: it attaches to the intended object by its very essence without needing another preponderator, because it is an attribute whose function is specification and preponderance, even of the equal, rather the preponderated, and this is not like the existence of a possible without an existentiator nor its being preponderated without a preponderator at all. If it is said: with the attachment of irada, the ability to choose inaction does not remain and choice is negated. We say: it has been stated not once that necessity arising from choice is pure choice.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (الإشكال الأول) to the Ash'ari position and its answer. The objection runs: irada itself is equally related to action and inaction and to all times. If it could not attach to the opposite side or another time, it would be like power and choice. If it is equally related, then its attaching to one action rather than another and one time rather than another would require another preponderator and specifier, leading to an infinite regress of wills. The answer: irada attaches to its object by its very essence, without needing another preponderator, because it is an attribute whose function is specification and preponderance, even of the equal or the preponderated. This is not like the existence of a possible without an existentiator or its being preponderated without a preponderator. Then a subsidiary objection is raised: if irada attaches, there is no ability to do otherwise, negating choice. The answer: it has been stated repeatedly that necessity arising from choice is pure choice (الوجوب بالاختيار محض الاختيار).

The Passing Away of Pre-Eternal Irada: Objection and Reply
Second objection and reply regarding pre-eternal irada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the pre-eternality of divine will and the Ash'ari response that distinguishes between the eternal attribute and its temporal relation.

Core Idea
Divine will remains pre-eternal even as its specific relation to creation passes away.

This lesson addresses a powerful objection: if God's will is pre-eternal, then its object (the world) must also be pre-eternal. The Ash'ari response distinguishes between the eternal attribute of will and its temporal relation to created events. The passage also engages with further subtleties about the nature of time and the preponderating role of will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first line of the passage: irada does not remain after bringing into existence, so the pre-eternal would cease, which is impossible. This is the core challenge.
The objection assumes irada is an attribute that perishes with its act.
2
Learn the Ash'ari response
The answer: irada relates to action or inaction; when the intended object occurs, only the temporal relation (talluq) ceases, not the attribute itself. This preserves pre-eternality.
The attribute is eternal; its relation to the created act is temporal.
3
Examine the sub-objection
Consider the further objection: if the intended object is not a necessary concomitant of irada, then irada does not give preponderance. The answer: the object is possible in itself, but with irada's attachment to existence it becomes necessary.
The text ends here, leaving the argument incomplete.
4
Identify the key distinction
Notice the crucial distinction between the eternal attribute (irada) and its temporal relation (talluq hadith). This distinction resolves the objection without compromising divine pre-eternality.
This is a hallmark of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن الإرادة لا تبقى بعد الإيجاد ضرورة ، فيلزم زوال القديم وهو محال . والجواب : أنها صفة قد تتعلق بالفعل، وقد تتعلق بالترك، فيخصص ما تعلقت به ويرجحه وعند وقوع المراد يزول تعلقها الحادث، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال : إنها لا تكون بدون المراد فيلزم من قدمها قدم المراد فيلزم قدم العالم على أن قدم المراد لا يوجب قدم العالم، لأن معناه : أن يريد الله تعالى في الأزل إيجاد العالم وإحداثه في وقته، ويشكل بإيجاد الزمان، إلا أن يجعل أمراً مقدوراً لا تحقق له في الأعيان. فإن قيل : نحن نردد في الأثر الذي هو المراد كالعالم مثلاً، بأنه إما لازم للإرادة فيلزم قدمه أو لا فيكون مع الإرادة جائز الوجود والعدم، فلا تكون الإرادة مرجحة . قلنا : هو جايز الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى نفس الإرادة، وأما مع تعلقها بالوجود،
The second [objection]: that irada does not remain after bringing into existence necessarily, so the cessation of the pre-eternal follows, and that is impossible. The answer: it is an attribute that sometimes relates to action and sometimes to inaction, so it specifies what it attaches to and gives it preponderance, and at the occurrence of the intended object, its temporal relation ceases. By this is repelled what is said: that it does not exist without the intended object, so from its pre-eternality follows the pre-eternality of the intended object, and thus the pre-eternality of the world. However, the pre-eternality of the intended object does not necessitate the pre-eternality of the world, because its meaning is that God, exalted, wills in eternity the bringing into existence of the world and its creation in its time. And there is difficulty concerning the bringing into existence of time, unless it is considered as something possible lacking actual existence in external things. If it is said: we are uncertain about the effect that is the intended object, like the world, whether it is a necessary concomitant of irada, so its pre-eternality follows, or not, then with irada it is possible of existence and non-existence, so irada would not be preponderating. We say: it is possible of existence and non-existence with respect to irada itself, but as for its attaching to existence,
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (الإشكال الثاني) and its answer. The objection: irada does not persist after bringing its object into existence, so if it is pre-eternal, its cessation would mean the passing away of something pre-eternal, which is impossible. The answer: irada is an attribute that sometimes relates to action and sometimes to inaction. It specifies whatever it attaches to and gives it preponderance. When the intended object occurs, its temporal relation ceases. This refutes the claim that irada cannot exist without its object, which would imply that if irada is pre-eternal, its object (the world) must also be pre-eternal. The answer clarifies that irada's pre-eternality does not necessitate the pre-eternality of the world, because it means that God willed in eternity the creation of the world at its time. A difficulty is raised regarding the creation of time itself, but it can be considered a possible thing without actual existence. Then there is a further objection: the effect (al-athar) that is the intended object (e.g., the world) is either a necessary concomitant of irada, implying its pre-eternality, or not, in which case irada does not give preponderance. The answer: it is possible in itself both to exist and not to exist with respect to irada itself, but when irada attaches to existence, it becomes necessary. The text ends here, incomplete.

قدم
متعلق
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If divine will is pre-eternal and its object is temporal, then will itself must be temporal.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that the attribute of will is pre-eternal; only its specific relation (talluq) to the created act is temporal.

Checkpoint

C) Irada is not pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari defense of irada's pre-eternality hinges on distinguishing the eternal attribute from its temporal relation to created events.

The First Objection Against Divine Will: Need for a Preponderator and Its Answer
Author presents first objection against divine will and its answer, plus a sub-objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against divine will—that it requires an external preponderator—and how the kalam response argues that divine will itself is intrinsically specifying, thereby avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Divine will needs no external preponderator; its essence is specification.

The first objection against the divine attribute of will argues that if will relates equally to all possibilities, then its specification of one action requires an external preponderator, leading to an infinite regress. In response, the kalam tradition holds that will is an attribute whose very nature is to specify and preponderate, even among equals. This passage presents that objection, its answer, and a follow-up concern about free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full argument: the objection, the main answer, and the sub-objection with response.
Notice how the author structures the objection: first stating the equality of will's relation, then the need for a preponderator, and the threat of infinite regress.
2
Identify the structure of the objection
Break down the objection: (1) will's relation is equal to all possibilities, (2) if equal, specification requires a preponderator, (3) that preponderator would need another will, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic kalam argument against the Ash'arite position.
3
Understand the main answer
The answer: divine will does not need an external preponderator because its very function is specification (takhsis) and preponderance (tarjih). It preponderates by its essence, not by an external cause.
This distinguishes the existence of a contingent (which needs a cause) from the specification by will.
4
Examine the sub-objection and its answer
The sub-objection: if will attaches to an act, then the ability to abandon is negated, so free choice is lost. The answer: 'necessitation by choice is pure choice' (al-wujub bil-ikhtiyar huwa mahd al-ikhtiyar).
This reinforces the compatibility of divine determinism with human free will in the Ash'arite framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمثل ما مرَّ في العلم والقدرة، وقد يرد ههنا إشكالات :
الأول : أن نسبة الإرادة أيضاً إلى الفعل والترك وإلى جميع الأوقات على السواء، إذ لو لم يجز تعلقها بالطرف الآخر، وفي الوقت الآخر لزم نفس القدرة والاختيار، وإذا كانت على السواء فتعلقها بالفعل دون الترك، وفي هذا الوقت، دون غيره، يفتقر إلى مرجح ومخصص لامتناع وقوع الممكن بلا مرجح كما ذكرتم ، ويلزم تسلسل الإرادات . والجواب : أنها إنما تتعلق بالمراد لذاتها من غير افتقار إلى مرجح آخر لأنها صفة شأنها التخصيص والترجيح، ولو للمساوي، بل المرجوح، وليس هذا من وجود الممكن بلا موجد وترجيحه بلا مرجح في شيء. فإن قيل : مع تعلق الإرادة لا يبقى التمكن من الترك وينتفي الاختيار. قلنا : قد مرّ غير مرة أن الوجوب بالاختيار محض الاختيار.
Like what was mentioned regarding knowledge and power, and there may occur here objections: The first: that the relation of will also to action and abandonment and to all times is equal, for if it is not permissible for it to be attached to the other side and at the other time, then the same power and free choice would be necessitated. And if they are equal, then its attachment to the action without abandonment, and at this time without another, requires a preponderator and a specifier, due to the impossibility of a contingent occurring without a preponderator as you have mentioned, and there would follow an infinite regress of wills. The answer: that it indeed attaches to the object of will by its essence without needing another preponderator because it is an attribute whose function is specification and preponderance, even for the equal, rather for the preponderated. And this is not at all from the existence of a contingent without an existentiator or its preponderance without a preponderator. If it is said: With the attachment of will, the ability to abandon does not remain and free choice is negated. We say: It has been mentioned more than once that necessitation by choice is pure choice.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against divine will and its answer. The objection argues that if divine will relates equally to all possibilities, then its specification of one action over another requires an external preponderator, leading to an infinite regress. The answer states that divine will itself is the preponderator by its very nature—its function is specification and preponderance, even among equals or preponderated options. This does not imply a contingent coming into existence without a cause. A sub-objection is then raised: if will attaches to an act, the ability to do otherwise disappears, negating free choice. The response is that necessitation arising from choice (wujub bil-ikhtiyar) is pure choice itself, as previously established.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
مرجح (preponderator)
تعلق (attachment)
اختيار (free choice)
وجوب بالاختيار (necessitation by choice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if divine will preponderates without external cause, it is arbitrary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that will's essence is specification, so it is not arbitrary but intrinsic.

Key Takeaway
Divine will specifies by its essence, not by an external cause, and necessitation by choice is pure choice.

?
The objection implies that God's will needs an external reason to choose one thing over another. But the answer says will's essence is specification. Does your faith in God's goodness sometimes waver when you can't see a reason for His decrees? What would it take for you to accept that His will is sufficient even without explanation?

Second Objection: Will Ceases After Creation
Author presents second objection and its answer regarding the cessation of will's attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection fails because only the temporal attachment of will ceases, not the eternal attribute itself.

Core Idea
Will's attachments change, not the eternal attribute itself.

The second objection claims that if divine will does not persist after creation, the eternal attribute would cease, which is impossible. The answer clarifies that it is only the temporal attachment of will to its object that ceases, leaving the eternal will unchanged. This distinction is key to repelling the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the passage from 'الثاني: أن الإرادة لا تبقى بعد الإيجاد ضرورة' to 'ولا تحقق له في الأعيان.' Notice the objection's structure and the answer's key distinction.
The answer introduces the concept of 'attachment' (تعلق) as distinct from the attribute itself.
2
Identify the core distinction
The answer states that will is an attribute that may attach to action or abandonment. When the object occurs, its temporal attachment (تعلقها الحادث) ceases, but the attribute of will remains eternal. This is the central point.
The phrase 'عند وقوع المراد يزول تعلقها الحادث' is crucial.
3
Understand how it repels the objection
The objection argued that cessation of will means the eternal ceases. The answer shows it is only the temporal attachment that ceases, not the eternal attribute itself, so the objection is invalid.
Also, the answer addresses the claim that eternality of will implies eternality of the world: God's eternal will is for the world to come into existence at its proper time.
4
Note the problem of time
The passage mentions a special difficulty with the creation of time itself, suggesting time might be an object of power without concrete realization.
This is a subtle point; it's acceptable to note it without full analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن الإرادة لا تبقى بعد الإيجاد ضرورة ، فيلزم زوال القديم وهو محال . والجواب : أنها صفة قد تتعلق بالفعل، وقد تتعلق بالترك، فيخصص ما تعلقت به ويرجحه وعند وقوع المراد يزول تعلقها الحادث، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال : إنها لا تكون بدون المراد فيلزم من قدمها قدم المراد فيلزم قدم العالم على أن قدم المراد لا يوجب قدم العالم، لأن معناه : أن يريد الله تعالى في الأزل إيجاد العالم وإحداثه في وقته، ويشكل بإيجاد الزمان، إلا أن يجعل أمراً مقدوراً لا تحقق له في الأعيان. فإن قيل : نحن نردد في الأثر الذي هو المراد كالعالم مثلاً، بأنه إما لازم للإرادة فيلزم قدمه أو لا فيكون مع الإرادة جائز الوجود والعدم، فلا تكون الإرادة مرجحة . قلنا : هو جايز الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى نفس الإرادة، وأما مع تعلقها بالوجود، فالوجود مترجح بل لازم وقد يمنع استحالة زوال القديم، وهو مدفوع بما سبق من البرهان، والاستناد بأنه لا يعلم في الأزل أن العالم معدوم سيوجد، وبعد الإيجاد لا يبقى ذلك التعلق الأزلي مدفوعاً بما عرفت في المبحث السابق .
The second: That will does not persist after creation necessarily, so the cessation of the eternal would follow, and that is impossible. The answer: That it is an attribute which may attach to action and may attach to abandonment, so it specifies what it attaches to and preponderates it, and when the object of will occurs, its temporal attachment ceases. And by this, what is said is repelled: That it does not exist without the object of will, so from its eternality would follow the eternality of the object of will, and thus the eternality of the world, although the eternality of the object of will does not necessitate the eternality of the world, because its meaning is that God, exalted, wills in eternity the bringing into existence of the world and its origination in its time. And it is problematic with the bringing into existence of time, unless it is made a thing that is an object of power that has no realization in concrete entities. If it is said: We consider the effect which is the object of will, like the world for example, as either necessary for will, so its eternality would follow, or not, then it is together with will possible of existence and non-existence, so will would not be a preponderator. We say: It is possible of existence and non-existence with respect to will itself, but with its attachment to existence, existence is preponderated rather necessary. And the impossibility of the cessation of the eternal may be prevented, and it is repelled by what preceded of the proof, and the appeal to the fact that it is not known in eternity that the world is non-existent and will come to exist, and after creation, that eternal attachment does not remain, is repelled by what you have known in the previous inquiry.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection states that divine will does not persist after its object is created, so the eternal attribute would cease, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between the eternal attribute of will and its temporal attachments (ta'alluqat). Will attaches to an object, and when the object occurs, that particular temporal attachment ends, but the attribute itself remains. This repels the claim that the world must be eternal because will is eternal; rather, God's eternal will is for the world to come into existence at its proper time. A sub-objection questions whether the effect (the world) is necessary due to will or merely possible. The response is that with respect to will itself, the effect is possible, but with will's attachment to existence, existence becomes preponderated or even necessary (relative to that attachment). The argument against the impossibility of the eternal ceasing is refuted by previous proofs.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
تعلق (attachment)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (temporal)
مراد (object of will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If will is eternal and does not persist after creation, then the eternal attribute ceases, which is impossible, so the doctrine is false.
Correction: Cessation of will's temporal attachment does not mean the eternal attribute ceases; only the attachment ends.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what ceases when the object of will occurs?
A) The attribute of will itself.
B) The temporal attachment of will.
C) God's knowledge.
D) The eternality of will.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because only the temporal attachment of will ceases, not the eternal attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that God's will for the world is tied to a specific time — 'its time.' When you look at your own life, do you truly believe that God's will for every event has an appointed time? What event are you struggling to wait for right now, and how can you actively trust His timing rather than demanding immediate results?
?
God's will attaches to actions and then the attachment ceases when the action is done. Reflect on your own intentions: When you accomplish something good, does your intention (niyya) persist or does it end? How can you cultivate intentions that remain connected to God even after the deed is done, so that your actions are never 'abandoned' by your will for His sake?
?
The objection claims that if God's will is eternal, the world must be eternal, which is false because God's will is for the world to begin in time. Does your understanding of God's eternal will affect how you view your own past, present, and future? Are you living with the awareness that your entire existence is precisely timed by God's decree? What would change if you took that truth seriously today?

Sub-Objection: Is the World Necessary Due to Will?
Author presents a sub-objection about necessity of effect and its response concerning possibility and preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sub-objection that the world must be either necessary or not due to will, and the response that with respect to will itself the world is possible, but with will's attachment to existence it becomes preponderated.

Core Idea
The world is possible with respect to will itself, but preponderated by attachment.

A sub-objection questions whether the created effect (e.g., the world) is necessary because of the divine will. If it is necessary, the world would be eternal; if not, will does not truly preponderate. The answer distinguishes between the world's status relative to will itself (possible) and relative to will's attachment to existence (preponderated). This clarifies that will is still effective without necessitating the world's eternality.

Guided Path
1
Read the sub-objection and response
Read from 'فإن قيل: نحن نردد في الأثر' to the end of the passage. Understand the dilemma posed.
The sub-objection considers the effect (الأثر) which is the object of will, like the world.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The sub-objection says: The effect is either necessary for will (so eternal) or not (so will does not preponderate). Both seem problematic.
'نحن نردد في الأثر الذي هو المراد كالعالم مثلاً' indicates the effect is the object of will.
3
Understand the response
The response says: With respect to will itself, the effect is possible (جائز الوجود والعدم). But with will's attachment to existence, existence becomes preponderated (مترجح بل لازم). This maintains will's role as preponderator without making the effect necessary in itself.
The phrase 'أما مع تعلقها بالوجود، فالوجود مترجح بل لازم' is key.
4
Note the refutation of the impossibility of cessation
The passage ends by saying that the impossibility of the eternal ceasing is repelled by previous proofs, and the argument that we know the world will come to exist does not affect the eternal attachment.
This links back to the earlier discussion about the first objection.

?

?

?

Do you ever wonder why God created the world when He did, or why He chose to attach His will to existence? This lesson reminds us that the attachment is eternal and not subject to change. How does that assurance—that God's will for your life is attached from eternity—comfort you or challenge you to surrender more fully?

The Third Objection: Preponderating or Futile
Author presents third objection that will is either preponderating or futile, and gives a brief answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the divine will's attachment and how it is answered by reference to the earlier proof about power.

Core Idea
God's will does not need a preponderating reason nor does it lead to futility.

This lesson presents a sharp objection against the necessity of God's will: either it chooses what is better, implying external perfection, or it does not, implying futility. The answer, however, is not a new argument but a pointer to the earlier discussion on power, showing the interconnectedness of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two horns of the dilemma: if God's will attaches to what is 'awla' (more fitting), then He is completed by another; if not, then it is 'abath' (futile).
This dilemma assumes that all action must have a purpose or be pointless.
2
Identify the answer's reference
The answer says 'ما مرّ في بحث قدرته' – what has passed in the discussion of His power. This refers back to an earlier proof that God's power does not need a preponderating factor and does not lead to futility. Understand that the same reasoning applies to will.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does the earlier argument about power resolve this objection? The earlier argument likely showed that God's attributes are not for completion and that His actions are not futile because they are not compelled by external ends.
This lesson shows the logical consistency in kalam: objections are answered by referring to established principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن تعلق إرادته إما أن يكون أولى فيلزم استكماله بالغير، أو لا فيلزم العبث. والجواب : ما مرّ في بحث قدرته .
The third: That the attachment of His will is either preponderating, so His being completed by another would follow, or not, so futility would follow. The answer: What has passed in the discussion of His power.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents a very brief third objection and its answer. The objection argues that God's attachment of will to an object is either because it is better (awla), which would imply God acquires perfection from another, or not better, implying futility (al-'abath). The answer does not repeat the full reasoning but refers back to the earlier discussion on divine power (qudra), where the same objection was presumably resolved. This shows the logical interdependence of kalam arguments.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
تعلق (attachment)
أولى (more fitting)
عبث (futility)
قدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will must have a purpose or reason for why it chooses one thing over another.
Correction: The answer shows that God's will, like His power, is not dependent on external reasons or ends; it is self-sufficient and not futile because divine attributes are not for completion.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the third objection?
A) By proving will is necessary.
B) By referring to the earlier discussion on power.
C) By denying the implication.
D) By asserting that will is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
The third objection is deflected by appealing to the same logic used for divine power: God's will is neither completed by another nor futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship that feels pointless, do you secretly demand that God's will must have a reason you can understand? How does knowing that His will is neither for completion nor futility challenge you to surrender without needing an explanation? Will you today release your need for justification and trust His perfect will?
?
Do you ever hesitate to obey Allah because you think your choice is 'better' or more fitting? The objection says that if God's will were based on what is preponderating, He would be completed by another. Does your heart truly believe God's will is complete without your approval? What would change if you submitted to His command right now, even if it seems not 'better' in your eyes?

Three Objections on Divine Will and Their Answers
Three objections on divine will with brief refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against the divine will and how the author refutes them by referring to earlier proofs.

Core Idea
Every objection to divine will has been answered in prior discussions.

In this passage, the author presents three common objections concerning God's will (irada). These are not new challenges but are raised by critics who may not have followed the earlier arguments. The author briefly states each objection and directs the reader to the earlier proofs that already refute them. This lesson will help you see how the theological system is interconnected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the numbered objections and the brevity of each answer.
2
Identify the three objections
Find each of the three objections. Notice what each one challenges about divine will.
The first objection questions preponderance and the removal of the eternal; the second relies on knowledge in pre-eternity; the third frames preference versus futility.
3
Understand each refutation's source
For each objection, identify where the author sends the reader for the answer. This shows the interconnectedness of the theological arguments.
The refutations refer to 'what preceded of the proof', 'what you knew in the previous discussion', and 'what has passed in the discussion of His power'.
4
Note the connection to previous topics
Reflect on how this passage depends on earlier sections. The answers are not new; they are reminders of established proofs.
This passage is a summary of refutations, not a full argument.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider why the author uses this method of brief reference rather than repeating the full argument. How does it strengthen the overall text?
It shows that the objections have already been anticipated and resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) افالوجود مترجح بل لازم وقد يمنع استحالة زوال القديم، وهو مدفوع بما سبق من البرهان، والاستناد بأنه (۲) لا يعلم في الأزل أن العالم معدوم سيوجد، وبعد الإيجاد لا يبقى ذلك التعلق الأزلي مدفوعاً بما عرفت في المبحث السابق . الثالث : أن تعلق إرادته إما أن يكون أولى فيلزم استكماله بالغير، أو لا فيلزم العبث. والجواب : ما مرَّ في بحث قدرته .
(1) Is existence preponderant? Rather, necessary? And it may be prevented that the removal of the eternal is impossible, and it is refuted by what preceded of the proof. And the reliance that (2) it is not known in pre-eternity that the world, non-existent, will come into existence, and after creation that eternal attachment does not remain, refuted by what you knew in the previous discussion. The third: that the attachment of His will either is preferable, entailing His completion by another, or not, entailing futility. And the answer: what has passed in the discussion of His power.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents three objections concerning the divine will and their refutations. The first objection questions whether existence is preponderant or necessary, and suggests that the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to be might be denied. The answer is that it is refuted by the proof already given. The second objection relies on the claim that in pre-eternity it is not known that the world will come into existence, and after creation the eternal attachment no longer remains. The answer refers to what was established in the previous discussion. The third objection states that the attachment of God's will either is preferable (which would imply completion by another) or not preferable (which would imply futility). The answer refers to the discussion on God's power (qudra). The author is summarizing classic objections and showing that each has been answered elsewhere.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
قديم (qadim)
أزلي (azali)
ترجح (tarajjuh)
عبث (abath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these objections are unanswered or that the author fails to provide a refutation.
Correction: The passage shows that each objection is refuted by earlier arguments, which the author references briefly. The answers are already established in previous sections.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the third objection about the attachment of God's will being either preferable or not?
A) It is refuted by the proof of the impossibility of eternal removal.
B) It is answered by the discussion on God's power (qudra).
C) It is refuted by the knowledge that the world will come into existence.
D) It is answered by the concept of completion by another.

Key Takeaway
All objections against divine will are already answered in the earlier discussions on necessity, knowledge, and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever privately doubted that God's will is truly wise and purposeful, especially when life seems chaotic or unfair? This passage shows that such objections are answered. What specific doubt do you need to surrender to the truth that His will is neither arbitrary nor futile?
?
The third objection implies that if God's will is not 'preferential' it must be futile. Do you sometimes measure your own life's value by how 'preferred' or successful you appear, rather than trusting that God's will for you is perfect even when hidden? How can you repent from that mindset today?
?
This lesson says all objections are refuted by prior discussions. Are you honestly seeking answers to your theological questions from the full tradition of Islamic scholarship, or do you settle for surface-level doubts that keep you from deeper certainty? What step will you take this week to study a refutation of a doubt you hold?

Erroneous Views on the Nature of Divine Will
Enumerates five flawed theories on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five major erroneous theories about divine will's temporality and the reasons for their rejection.

Core Idea
False theories about divine will reduce it to something else or imply logical absurdities.

Sanusi begins by listing several misguided views on divine will from different theological and philosophical schools. Each attempt to define will ends up either contradicting reason or denying the very concept of will. This lesson surveys these positions before moving to the Mu'tazila's more detailed theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of views
Read the Arabic passage that lists the Karramiyya, Jabba'iyya, philosophers, al-Najjar, and al-Ka'bi. Notice how each view is described briefly.
These are all criticized as insufficient or impossible.
2
Identify the specific error in each view
The commentary explains why each view fails: infinite regress, locus of events, denial of will, etc. Make sure you understand each objection.
The errors are stated concisely.
3
Connect to the broader debate
These views represent attempts to explain how God wills. The Mu'tazila theory (next lesson) is another attempt. Understanding these failures sets the stage.

Source Text

قال : القائلون بحدوث الإرادة والرد عليهم وحدوثها مع قيامها بذاته على ما هو رأي الكرامية يوجب التسلسل وكونه محل الحوادث. ومع قيامها بنفسها على ما هو رأي الجبائية ضروري البطلان . وقول الحكماء إنه العلم بالنظام الأكمل نفي لما نسميه الإرادة. وكذا قول النجار أنه كونه غير مكره، ولا ساء، وقول الكعبي : إنه في فعله العلم و في فعل غيره الأمر، وذهب كثير من المعتزلة إلى أنها الداعية فقيل في الغائب خاصة، وقيل فيهما جميعاً، ومعنى الداعية في الشاهد العلم والاعتقاد أو الظن بنفع زاد في الفعل، وفي الغائب العلم بذلك، واحتجوا بأن الإرادة فعل المريد قطعاً، والفاعل يجب أن يكون له شعور لفعله، ولا شعور لنا إلا بالداعي الخالص أو المرجح على الصادق. ورد بأن لا نسلم أنه اختياري، وأنه لا شعور بغير الداعي بل الشعور بحالة بعقبه، وعرض بأن العطشان والهارب يميل إلى أحد الماءين أو الطريقين عند التساوي).
He said: Those who affirm the temporality (huduth) of will and refutation against them: And its temporality while it subsists in His essence, according to the opinion of the Karramiyya, necessitates infinite regress (tasalsul) and His being the locus of temporal events. And while it subsists in itself, according to the opinion of the Jabba'iyya, is necessarily invalid. And the statement of the philosophers that it is knowledge of the most perfect order is a denial of what we call will. Likewise the statement of al-Najjar that it is His being not compelled and not evil. And the statement of al-Ka'bi: that it is in His act knowledge, and in the act of another command. Many of the Mu'tazila went to that it is the motive (da'iya); it was said specifically in the absent (gha'ib), and it was said in both. And the meaning of motive in the witnessed (shahid) is knowledge and belief or presumption of benefit that increases the act, and in the absent it is knowledge of that. They argued that will is the act of the willer definitively, and the agent must have perception (shu'ur) for his act, and we have no perception except of the pure motive or the preponderant over the truthful. And it was refuted by that we do not concede that it is voluntary, and that there is no perception other than the motive, but perception of a state that follows it, and it was objected that the thirsty and the fleeing person inclines to one of two waters or two paths when they are equal.)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage enumerates various erroneous views on the nature of divine will and refutes the Mu'tazila's theory of da'iya. First, the Karramiyya view that will is temporal (hadith) and subsists in God's essence is said to lead to infinite regress (tasalsul) and to God being a locus of temporal events. Second, the Jabba'iyya view that it subsists in itself is necessarily invalid. Third, the philosophers' view that will is simply knowledge of the most perfect order denies what we mean by will. Fourth, al-Najjar's definition (being not compelled and not evil) is also dismissed. Fifth, al-Ka'bi's distinction (knowledge regarding His own act, command regarding another's act) is mentioned. Finally, the majority of Mu'tazila hold that will is a motive (da'iya). The passage explains the meaning of da'iya: in the witnessed realm it is knowledge, belief, or presumption of benefit that increases the act; in the absent (i.e., God) it is knowledge of that benefit. Their proof: will is an act of the willer, and every agent must have perception of his act, and we perceive nothing but the motive or the preponderant. The refutation: we deny that will is voluntary (in the sense they claim), and that perception is not limited to the motive; rather, there is perception of a subsequent state. The objection: the thirsty or fleeing person inclines to one of two equal waters/paths, showing that will is not solely based on motive. This critique demonstrates the inadequacy of the da'iya theory.

إرادة (irada)
حدوث (huduth)
كرامية (Karramiyya)
جبائية (Jabba'iyya)
حكماء (hukama')

Checkpoint

A) It explains divine will fully
B) It denies what we call will
C) It is compatible with the Karramiyya view
D) It is supported by al-Ka'bi

Key Takeaway
The five theories all fail to correctly define divine will, each due to a specific logical or theological error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that the philosophers reduce God's will to mere knowledge of the best order, ask yourself: Do I sometimes treat Allah's will as if it is just His knowing what is best, rather than a distinct, active power? Will you from now on honor His will as a unique attribute, separate from His knowledge?
?
The Karramiyya's error was to make Allah subject to change. Is there an area in your life where you imagine Allah's will changing to fit your desires—like hoping He will overlook your sin because He 'understands'? Repent and affirm that His will is eternal, unchangeable, and not bound by your emotions.
?
Al-Najjar's definition of will as 'not compelled and not evil' is too vague. Have you ever contented yourself with a shallow understanding of Allah's will, never meditating on its depth? Commit today to studying the names and attributes of Allah with the seriousness they deserve.

The Mu'tazila Theory of Da'iya and Its Proof
Presents Mu'tazila da'iya theory and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's da'iya theory of divine will and their proof based on perception of the agent.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila reduce divine will to a motive (da'iya), but their proof assumes will is a voluntary act.

The majority of Mu'tazila define will as a motive (da'iya) that drives action. Sanusi explains this theory and then presents their proof that will must arise from a perceived motive. This lesson focuses on their definition and argument, setting up the refutation in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of da'iya
Read the passage that defines da'iya in both the witnessed realm (shahid) and the absent (gha'ib). Note the difference.
In shahid it includes presumption of benefit; in gha'ib it is pure knowledge.
2
Understand the proof structure
The Mu'tazila argue that will is an act of the willer, so the agent must have perception (shu'ur) of his act. They claim the only perception possible is of the motive or the preponderant factor.
This leads to the conclusion that will is identical to the motive.
3
Evaluate the argument's strength
Consider whether all acts of will require a perceived motive. The next lesson will show weaknesses, but for now, grasp the logical chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
The Mu'tazila argue that even God's will is a motive based on knowledge. Does this perspective affect how you trust God's decrees? Have you ever questioned why God willed something difficult in your life? How does understanding His will as perfect knowledge of benefit change your response to trials?
?
If your will is simply a response to what you perceive as beneficial, then what is the role of your spiritual aspirations? Are your acts of worship driven by a genuine desire for God, or by a calculated benefit? Search your heart: is your 'da'iya' pure enough to lead you to sincere devotion? What would it take to purify your motives?

Refutation of the Da'iya Theory and the Equal Inclination Objection
Refutes Mu'tazila da'iya theory with denial and objection from equal alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the da'iya theory, including the denial that will is voluntary and the objection from choosing between equals.

Core Idea
The da'iya theory fails because will can occur without any perceived motive, as shown by equal alternatives.

After presenting the Mu'tazila's da'iya theory and its proof, Sanusi now refutes it. He first denies key premises and then provides a strong counterexample: a thirsty person who chooses between two equal waters, or a fleeing person between two equal paths. This shows that will can exist without a perceived motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage containing the refutation. Note that it begins with 'wa rudda bi-anna' (and it was refuted by that).
Two points are raised: denial of voluntariness and alternative perception.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection about the thirsty person. It shows that one can incline to one of two equal options without any motive tipping the balance.
This directly undermines the da'iya theory.
3
Reflect on the significance
This example demonstrates that will is not always based on a perceived benefit; it can be arbitrary or based on another factor. Thus the da'iya theory is insufficient.

?

?

Ask Allah to strengthen your will to choose what pleases Him, even when no worldly motive compels you.
?

Today, make a small decision that has no clear 'best' outcome, and consciously thank Allah for the freedom of your will.
