Refutation of the Karramiyya View on Divine Will
Refutes Karramiyya claim via two arguments and an objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why the Karramiyya claim that God's will is temporal and subsistent in His essence is invalid, and how the objection is answered.

Core Idea
A temporal will in God would unravel the basis of creation.

The Karramiyya sect argued that God's will is a temporal event occurring within His essence. The author firmly rejects this, providing two arguments and addressing a potential objection. This passage demonstrates how classical kalam defends the timelessness of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the author's style of stating the sect's view before giving arguments.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage gives two reasons against the Karramiyya view. Find each one and note how they are introduced (e.g., 'first' by the general principle, 'second' by the circularity/regress).
The first argument refers to a previously established impossibility.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection tries to allow temporal attributes via necessity and a temporal condition. Read the response carefully: it mentions an endless succession and the condition being either divine or not.
This shows the depth of refutation by anticipating counterpoints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وحدوثها يشير إلى نفي مذاهب المبطلين، فمنها قول الكرامية إن إرادة الله تعالى حادثة قائمة بذاته وهو فاسد لما مر من استحالة قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى، ولأنه صدور الحادث عن الواجب لا يكون إلا بالاختيار فيتوقف على الإرادة ويلزم الدور أو التسلسل . فإن قيل : استناد الصفات إلى الذات إنما هو بطريق الإيجاب دون الاختيار فلم لا يجوز أن يكون البعض منها موقوفاً على شرط حادث فيكون حادثاً . :قلنا لما يلزم من تعاقب حوادث لا بداية لها، وقد بينا استحالته، ولأن تلك الشروط ، إما صفات للباري، فيلزم حدوثه لأن ما لا يخلو عن الحادث حادث،
His statement: 'and its temporality' (wuhduthuha) refers to the negation of the doctrines of the invalidators. Among them is the statement of the Karramiyya that the will of God Almighty is temporally originated (haditha) subsisting in His essence, and it is invalid because of what has passed of the impossibility of temporal events subsisting in the essence of God Almighty, and because the emanation of the temporal from the Necessary cannot be except by choice (ikhtiyar), so it depends on will, and circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. If it is said: The attribution of attributes to the essence is only by way of necessity (ijab) without choice, so why is it not permissible that some of them be dependent on a temporal condition and thus be temporal? We said: because of what follows from a succession of temporal events with no beginning, and we have shown its impossibility, and because those conditions are either attributes of the Creator, so His temporality follows, for that which is not free of temporal events is temporal.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed refutation of the Karramiyya view that God's will is temporal (hadith) and subsists in His essence. The author explains that 'and its temporality' in the text refers to negating the false sects. The Karramiyya claim is invalid for two reasons: first, because it is impossible for temporal events to subsist in God's essence (this has been established earlier). Second, because the emanation of a temporal being from the Necessary Existent must be by choice (ikhtiyar), and choice depends on will. If will itself is temporal, then either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) results. An objection is then raised: the attribution of attributes to the essence is by necessity (ijab), not by choice, so why can some attributes not depend on a temporal condition? The answer is that this would lead to a succession of temporal events with no beginning, which has been shown impossible. Moreover, those conditions are either attributes of God, and then God would be temporal because whatever is not free of temporal events is itself temporal. This argument effectively blocks the Karramiyya position.

Key Terms
كرامية (Karramiyya)
حادثة (haditha)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
اختيار (ikhtiyar)
إيجاب (ijab)
شرط حادث (shart hadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will could be a temporal event that subsists in His essence without contradiction.
Correction: The passage shows that this leads to impossible consequences: either temporal events in God's essence (invalid per prior proof) or circularity/infinite regress, and the attempt to avoid these via a temporal condition also fails.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given for the invalidity of the Karramiyya view that God's will is temporal?
A) It implies that God is a locus of temporal events.
B) It requires that temporal events subsist in God's essence.
C) It leads to circularity or infinite regress because emanation of the temporal requires will.
D) It contradicts the necessity of attributes.

Key Takeaway
The divine will cannot be temporal because that would lead to absurdities such as infinite regress or the impossibility of temporal events existing in God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's will is eternal and unchanging, how would that transform your response when your desires conflict with His decree? What specific area of your life are you most reluctant to submit to His unchangeable will?
?
The Karramiyya attempted to preserve God's transcendence but ended up projecting a human-like changeability onto Him. Where in your own thinking about God have you unconsciously lowered His perfection to fit your limited understanding? Will you repent from that today?
?
Consider the logical chains in this argument: if will were temporal, it would require an infinite regress of wills. In your daily life, do you act as if God's will is something that can be 'negotiated' or 'changed' by your actions? What would it look like to live with the certainty that His eternal will cannot be altered?

5 min
The Mu'tazila Argument from Motivation and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents and refutes the Mu'tazila claim that will is a motivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that will is a motivation and how it is refuted with the example of equal alternatives.

Core Idea
Will does not always require a preponderant motivation; inclination can occur without it.

In this passage, Taftazani presents the Mu'tazila theory that will is a 'motivation' (al-da'iyah), distinguishing its meaning in the witnessed and unseen realms. He then refutes it by denying that will is voluntary and by showing that consciousness is of a state after the will, not of the motivation itself. A powerful objection is given using the example of the thirsty person at two equal waters, demonstrating that inclination can exist without any preponderant factor.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila say will is a motivation, defining it in the witnessed as knowledge/belief/opinion of benefit, and in the unseen as knowledge of that benefit.
Note the distinction between witnessed (human) and unseen (God).
2
Examine the Mu'tazila argument
They argue: will is an act of the willing subject; every agent has consciousness of his act; we only have consciousness of the pure motivation or the preponderant factor. Therefore will must be the motivation.
This is a logical deduction from the premise about consciousness.
3
Follow the refutation
Taftazani refutes by: 1) not conceding that will is voluntary (i.e., something we choose), and 2) stating that consciousness is not of the motivation but of a state that follows the will.
This undermines the link between will and consciousness of motivation.
4
Consider the objection with equal alternatives
The example of the thirsty person at two equal waters or the fleeing person at two equal roads shows that inclination can occur without a preponderant motivation, refuting the need for a motivation.
This is a concrete counterexample to the Mu'tazila claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب كثير من المعتزلة إلى أنها الداعية فقيل في الغائب خاصة، وقيل فيهما جميعاً، ومعنى الداعية في الشاهد العلم والاعتقاد أو الظن بنفع زاد في الفعل، وفي الغائب العلم بذلك، واحتجوا بأن الإرادة فعل المريد قطعاً، والفاعل يجب أن يكون له شعور لفعله، ولا شعور لنا إلا بالداعي الخالص أو المرجح على الصادق. ورد بأن لا نسلم أنه اختياري، وأنه لا شعور بغير الداعي بل الشعور بحالة بعقبه، وعرض بأن العطشان والهارب يميل إلى أحد الماءين أو الطريقين عند التساوي.
And many of the Mu'tazila went to that it is the motivation, and it was said [it is] in the unseen specifically, and it was said in both of them. And the meaning of motivation in the witnessed is knowledge, belief, or opinion of a benefit that increases in the action, and in the unseen is knowledge of that. And they argued that the will is certainly an act of the willing subject, and the agent must have consciousness of his act, and we have consciousness only of the pure motivation or the preponderant over the truthful. And it was refuted by that we do not concede that it is voluntary, and that there is no consciousness other than of the motivation; rather consciousness is of a state after it. And it was objected that the thirsty and the fleeing person incline to one of the two waters or two roads when equal.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the Mu'tazila theory that will is a 'motivation' (al-da'iyah). They distinguish between the meaning in the witnessed world (human beings) where it is knowledge, belief, or opinion of a beneficial quality that increases the action, and in the unseen (God) where it is mere knowledge of that benefit. They argue that will is an act of the subject, and every agent must have consciousness of his act; for us, that consciousness is only of the pure motivation or the preponderant factor. The refutation: first, we do not accept that will is a voluntary action (i.e., that it is something we choose); second, consciousness is not of the motivation but of a state that follows the will. The objection is then raised using the example of the thirsty person at two equal water sources or a fleeing person at two equal roads: they incline to one despite no preponderant motivation. This shows that inclination can exist without a preponderant motivation, undermining the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
الداعية
الشاهد
الغائب
الشعور
المرجح
التساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will always requires a preponderant motivation to incline.
Correction: Inclination can occur without a preponderant motivation, as shown by the example of equal alternatives where the agent still chooses one.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation use the example of the thirsty person and the fleeing person?
A) To show that will depends on motivation even in equal cases.
B) To show that inclination can occur without a preponderant motivation.
C) To show that God does not have equal alternatives.
D) To show that consciousness always accompanies volition.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument that will requires a motivation is refuted by showing that will can incline without a preponderant factor, as in the case of equal alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with two equally good choices, do you experience any pull toward one without a clear reason? How does this lesson's refutation of the need for a preponderant motivation challenge you to trust that your will can operate without fully rational justification?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that every action must be motivated by a known benefit. Are there areas in your life where you feel pressured to justify your choices by calculating benefits, even in matters of faith? How might this lesson free you to act on inclinations that are not fully explained?
?
This lesson shows that consciousness of will is not consciousness of the motivation itself but of a state that follows. When you make a decision from the heart, do you often try to rationalize it afterward? What would it look like for you to accept that your will can move without a complete explanation, and to surrender that movement to God?

Refuting the Karramiyya: God's Will Cannot Be Created
Taftazani refutes the Karramiyya claim that God's will is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyya view that God's will is created and subsists in His essence is impossible, and how the argument against it avoids logical pitfalls.

Core Idea
A created will in God leads to infinite regress or circularity.

In this passage, Taftazani tackles a specific sectarian claim: that God's will is a created accident residing in His essence. This might seem like a minor point, but it threatens the foundational Islamic understanding of God's attributes and agency. Taftazani dismantles the view using two concise reasons and then responds to a possible objection, showing the depth of classical kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the compact, logical style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the Karramiyya position and two reasons
Find where Taftazani states their view and then the two reasons he gives for its falsity. The first reason is from previous proof, the second from the need for will in origination.
The two reasons are introduced by 'li-mā marra' and 'li-annahu'.
3
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection starting with 'fa-in qīla' and see how Taftazani counters it by pointing to an infinite regress of events and the implication of dependence.
This objection-response structure is common in kalam.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider why infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr) are considered impossible. How does this reinforce God's transcendence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدوثها مع قيامها بذاته على ما هو رأي الكرامية يوجب التسلسل وكونه محل الحوادث. قوله : وحدوثها يشير إلى نفي مذاهب المبطلين، فمنها قول الكرامية إن إرادة الله تعالى حادثة قائمة بذاته وهو فاسد لما مر من استحالة قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى، ولأنه صدور الحادث عن الواجب لا يكون إلا بالاختيار فيتوقف على الإرادة ويلزم الدور أو التسلسل. فإن قيل : استناد الصفات إلى الذات إنما هو بطريق الإيجاب دون الاختيار فلم لا يجوز أن يكون البعض منها موقوفاً على شرط حادث فيكون حادثاً. قلنا لما يلزم من تعاقب حوادث لا بداية لها، وقد بينا استحالته، ولأن تلك الشروط، إما صفات للباري، فيلزم حدوثه لأن ما لا يخلو عن الحادث حادث، أو لا فيلزم افتقاره في صفاته وكمالاته إلى الغير.
And its createdness while it subsists in His essence, according to the view of the Karramiyya, necessitates infinite regress and His being a locus of accidents. His saying: 'and its createdness' refers to the negation of the schools of the invalidators. Among them is the saying of the Karramiyya that the will of God, exalted, is created and subsisting in His essence, and this is corrupt due to what has passed regarding the impossibility of accidents subsisting in the essence of God, exalted, and because the emanation of the originated from the Necessary is only by choice, so it depends on will, leading to circularity or infinite regress. If it is said: The attribution of attributes to the essence is by way of necessity, not choice, so why is it not permissible that some of them be dependent on a created condition, thus being created? We say: Because there would follow an infinite succession of events without beginning, and we have shown its impossibility, and because those conditions are either attributes of the Creator, so His createdness follows because whatever is not free of the created is created, or not, so His need of another in His attributes and perfections would follow.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage refutes the Karramiyya view that God's will is created and subsists in His essence. The author first states the view and its implications: it necessitates infinite regress and makes God a locus of accidents. He then gives two reasons for its falsity: (1) it is impossible for accidents to subsist in God's essence, as previously proven; (2) the originated must proceed from the Necessary Being by choice, which depends on will, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. An objection is then raised: perhaps some attributes are not volitional but necessarily flow from God's essence, so they could depend on a created condition without circularity. The author answers: this would entail an infinite chain of events with no beginning, which is impossible; moreover, the conditions would either be attributes of God (making Him subject to accidents) or not (making Him dependent on something else for His attributes). Thus the Karramiyya position is invalid.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الكرامية
التسلسل
محل الحوادث
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the objection (attributes could be non-volitional and depend on a created condition) offers a valid escape from the argument.
Correction: Taftazani shows this leads to an infinite regress of accidents or makes God dependent on something else for His attributes, both impossible.

Checkpoint
What two problems does the author identify in the Karramiyya view that God's will is created and subsists in His essence?
A) It implies God is a locus for accidents and leads to circularity or infinite regress.
B) It contradicts the fact that God is eternal and cannot have accidental properties.
C) It implies God's will is independent of His essence and is self-subsisting.
D) It leads to the conclusion that God's will is eternal and uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya view fails because it either makes God a locus of accidents or leads to an infinite regress, both of which are impossible for the Self-Sufficient Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or a delay in your life, do you truly believe that God's will for you is eternal and unchangeable? If He does not 'change His mind' because His will is not created, how does that challenge your response to your circumstances?
?
The argument in this passage insists that God does not depend on anything external for His attributes or perfection. Are there areas in your life where you secretly think God needs something from you—your worship, your obedience—to complete His perfection? How does this passage correct that?
?
If you were to internalize that even one created attribute in God would lead to an infinite regress, would you still be tempted to think of God in temporal or changing terms (e.g., 'God was angry but now He is pleased')? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you truly believed His will is eternally settled?

Refutation of Self-Subsisting Will
Taftazani refutes the Basran Mu'tazila claim that God's will is a self-subsisting accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Basran Mu'tazila view that God's will subsists by itself is necessarily invalid according to Taftazani.

Core Idea
A self-subsisting attribute is a contradiction.

In this passage, Taftazani directly confronts a key Mu'tazilite position on the divine will. He argues that if God's will subsists by itself, it cannot be an attribute — a logical impossibility. This refutation relies on a fundamental distinction between attributes and self-subsisting entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the strong language ('necessarily invalid', 'more appropriate').
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two refutations: (1) what subsists by itself cannot be an attribute, and (2) God's attributes are not accidents. Identify both.
3
Reflect on the sectarian context
Consider why this matters for Ash'ari theology. The Basran Mu'tazila tried to preserve God's will as a real entity, but in doing so they made it an accident. Taftazani shows the flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومع قيامها بنفسها على ما هو رأي الجبائية ضروري البطلان. ومنها قول أكثر معتزلة البصرة : إن إرادته حادثة قائمة بنفسها لا بمحل ، وبطلانه ضروري فإن ما يقوم بنفسه لا يكون صفة، وهذا أولى من أن يقال: إن العرض لا يقوم إلا بمحل للاطبات على أن صفات الباري ليست من قبيل الأعراض، وفي كلام بعض المعتزلة. أن العرض نفسه سيس بضروري، بل استدلالي فكيف حكمه الذي هو استحالة قيامه بنفسه، وفساده بين؟
And with its subsistence by itself, according to the view of the Jubbā'iyya, it is necessarily invalid. And among them is the saying of most of the Basran Mu'tazila: that His will is created, subsisting by itself, not in a substrate, and its invalidity is necessary, for whatever subsists by itself cannot be an attribute. This is more appropriate than saying: the accident subsists only in a substrate, to establish that the attributes of the Creator are not of the kind of accidents. And in the speech of some of the Mu'tazila: that the accident itself is [؟] by necessity, rather by inference, so how [can] their ruling which is the impossibility of its subsistence by itself [stand]? And its corruption is clear.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes the Basran Mu'tazila view that God's will is created and subsists by itself without a substrate. The author declares this necessarily invalid because whatever subsists by itself cannot be an attribute. He notes that this argument is even stronger than the claim that an accident can only subsist in a substrate, because God's attributes are not accidents at all. He then mentions that some Mu'tazila argue that the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself is not self-evident but rather inferred; yet their own ruling against it is still valid, and the corruption of their position is evident. Thus the view that God's will is a self-subsisting accident is refuted.

Key Terms
الإرادة
معتزلة البصرة
الجبائية
العرض
القيام بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'self-subsisting' is a valid way to describe God's will, making it a real entity independent of His essence.
Correction: An attribute by definition depends on its subject; a self-subsisting quality cannot be an attribute. Thus, the Mu'tazila view undermines the very nature of will as an attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the view that God's will subsists by itself necessarily invalid?
A) Because it makes God's will an accident without a substrate.
B) Because whatever subsists by itself cannot be an attribute.
C) Because it implies that will is not a separate attribute.
D) Because it leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The Basran Mu'tazila view that God's will is a self-subsisting accident is invalid because self-subsistence contradicts the nature of an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, does it ever feel like something separate from Him, like a decision He makes? How does this lesson challenge you to see His will as an intrinsic attribute, not a self-standing thing?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you have unconsciously assumed that God's attributes are like human attributes—temporary, changing, or independent? What would it mean for your worship to truly believe that His will is nothing like your will, and that it is eternal and perfect?
?
The Basran Mu'tazila were intelligent theologians, yet they fell into a contradiction by trying to protect God's transcendence. In your own attempts to understand God through reason, where might you be overconfident, and how can you submit to the sound teachings of Ahl al-Sunna?

Refuting the Philosophers' Equation of Will with Knowledge
Taftazani refutes the philosophers' equation of will with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' view of divine will as mere knowledge of the best order is incompatible with the Islamic concept of will.

Core Idea
God's will is not just knowledge; it is a distinct, volitional attribute.

The philosophers argue that God's will is nothing but His knowledge of the most perfect order, a concept they call 'care' (al-'inaya). This passage from Taftazani exposes how this definition effectively denies the independent attribute of will. You will see how their reasoning leads to a conclusion that contradicts Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the philosophers' terminology and Taftazani's critical tone.
Pay attention to the term 'العناية' (care) and how it is defined.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition
Find the definition of 'care' in the passage: it is the representation of the entire system of existence in God's eternal knowledge. This is key to their argument.
3
Understand the negation
Taftazani points out that this definition negates the will Muslims affirm. Ask yourself: why does equating will with knowledge remove the volitional aspect?
The philosophers claim that emanation follows knowledge, not will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الحكماء إنه العلم بالنظام الأكمل نفي لما نسميه الإرادة. ومنها قول الحكماء : إن إرادته تعالى، ويسمونها العناية بالمخلوقات . هو تمثل نظام جميع وجود الموجودات من الأزل إلى الأبد في علمه السابق على هذه الموجودات مع الأوقات المترتبة غير المتناهية التي تجب وتليق أن يقع كل موجود منها في واحد من تلك الأوقات. قالوا : وهذا هو المقتضى لإفاضته ذلك النظام على ذلك التركيب، والتفصيل، إذ لا يجوز أن يكون صدوره عن الواجب وعن العقول المجردة بقصد الإرادة، ولا بحسب طبيعته ، ولا على سبيل الاتفاق والجزاف، لأن العلل العالية لا تفعل لغرض في الأمور السافلة ، فقد صرحوا في إثبات هذه العناية بنفي ما نسميه الإرادة، وقد عرفت مرادهم بإحاطة علم الله تعالى بالكل، ولأنها ليست إلا وجود الكل.
And the saying of the philosophers that it is knowledge of the best order is a negation of what we call will. And among them is the saying of the philosophers: that His will, exalted, and they call it 'care' for the creatures, is the representation of the system of all existence of existents from pre-eternity to post-eternity in His knowledge preceding these existents, with the ordered infinite times which are necessary and fitting for each existent to occur in one of those times. They said: This is what necessitates the emanation of that system in that composition and detail, for it is not permissible that its emanation from the Necessary and from the abstract intellects be by intent of will, nor according to its nature, nor by chance and haphazard, because the higher causes do not act for a purpose in lower things. They explicitly, in proving this 'care', negate what we call will. And you have known their intent regarding the encompassing of God's knowledge of the whole, and because it is nothing but the existence of the whole.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophers' view that God's will is identical to His knowledge of the optimal order. The author first quotes their definition: they call it 'care' (al-'inaya), which is the representation in God's eternal knowledge of the entire system of existence from eternity to eternity, including all the specific times fitting for each existent. This knowledge itself necessitates the emanation of that order in detail. The philosophers argue that the Necessary Being and the separate intellects cannot act by will, by nature, or by chance; rather, their causation is through knowledge. Thus they negate the existence of will as a distinct attribute, reducing it to knowledge. The author notes that this effectively denies what Muslims mean by 'will'. He then remarks that this view is well known and that the philosophers' 'care' is nothing but the existence of the whole in God's knowledge.

Key Terms
الحكماء
العناية
النظام الأكمل
العقول المجردة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge of the best possible world is sufficient to explain His creative action, and that this is what is meant by 'will'.
Correction: The passage shows that this view actually negates the will as a distinct, voluntary attribute. Islamic theology affirms that will is separate from knowledge and involves choice.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers mean by 'care' (al-'inaya) for the creatures?
A) God's will to benefit His creatures.
B) The representation of the entire system of existence in God's eternal knowledge.
C) God's command that brings things into existence.
D) The best possible world that God could have created.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' concept of 'care' equates God's will with knowledge, thereby negating the volitional attribute that Muslims affirm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will were merely His knowledge of the best order, would there be any genuine contingency or need for supplication? When you pray, do you honestly believe you are petitioning a God who can will something new in response to your plea, or do you act as if His will is a frozen blueprint already set in His knowledge?
?
Do you treat God's will as an active, personal choice that intervenes in your life moment by moment, or have you unconsciously adopted a view where God is just a passive 'cause' that set everything in motion? Examine your daily reliance on Him.
?
The philosophers argued that higher causes do not act for purposes in lower things. In your worship, do you assume God is too 'exalted' to care about your specific needs, or do you live with the conviction that His will is intimately involved in every detail of your existence?

Al-Najjar and al-Kaabi on Divine Will
Taftazani cites the definitions of divine will by al-Najjar and al-Kaabi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of divine will proposed by al-Najjar and al-Kaabi and the critical questions they raise.

Core Idea
Divine will is not simply the absence of coercion or a synonym for knowledge/command.

In this passage, Taftazani cites two more Mu'tazilite attempts to define divine will. Al-Najjar defines it negatively, while al-Kaabi splits it depending on context. These definitions, though brief, illustrate the struggle to capture a positive concept of will that does not reduce it to other attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two scholars and their distinct approaches to defining divine will.
Al-Najjar uses negation, al-Kaabi uses context.
2
Identify the key terms
Highlight 'غير مكره' (not coerced), 'غير ساه' (not forgetful), 'العلم' (knowledge), and 'الأمر' (command). These terms are central to the definitions.
3
Consider the implications
Think about why these definitions might be considered inadequate. Does defining will as 'not coerced' capture its essence? Is will merely knowledge or command in different contexts?
This connects to the broader critique of Mu'tazilite views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا قول النجار أنه كونه غير مكره، ولا ساء، وقول الكعبي : إنه في فعله العلم و في فعل غيره الأمر. ومنها قول النجار من المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى كونه غير مكره ولا ساه، وقول الكعبي : وكثير
And likewise the saying of al-Najjar that it is His being not coerced nor neglectful, and the saying of al-Kaabi: that in His own action it is knowledge, and in the action of another it is command. And among them is the saying of al-Najjar from the Mu'tazila: that the will of God, exalted, is His being not coerced nor forgetful, and the saying of al-Kaabi: and many...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage briefly presents two more views on divine will from the Mu'tazila. Al-Najjar defines will negatively: it is God's not being coerced (gayr mukrih) nor forgetful (la sahīw / la sā'?), implying will is simply the absence of constraint and heedlessness. Al-Kaabi distinguishes between God's own actions and others' actions: with respect to His own actions, will is just knowledge; with respect to others' actions, it is command. The author's commentary adds that al-Najjar says will is God's being 'not coerced nor forgetful', and al-Kaabi's statement is cut off at 'and many...' The author does not provide explicit refutation in this window, but the brief mention suggests these definitions are also considered inadequate.

Key Terms
النجار
الكعبي
غير مكره
غير ساه
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining will as 'not coerced' captures its essence.
Correction: It merely describes a condition of will, not a positive attribute that distinguishes it from knowledge or power.

Checkpoint
According to al-Kaabi, what is God's will with respect to His own actions?
A) Knowledge
B) Command
C) Absence of coercion
D) Care

Key Takeaway
Al-Najjar defines will as the absence of coercion and forgetfulness, while al-Kaabi reduces it to knowledge for God's own acts and command for others, both failing to capture a distinct positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God’s will, do you ever reduce it to a mere lack of restraint, as al-Najjar did? How does that affect your trust in His active, purposeful sovereignty over every detail of your life? Will you today consciously affirm that His will is a positive, powerful attribute?
?
Al-Kaabi claimed that God’s will for your actions is merely His command. When you disobey, do you treat that command as just a suggestion rather than the expression of His absolute will? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that every command of Allah is a direct manifestation of His will for you?

Refutation of the Basran Mu'tazila View on God's Will as a Self-Subsisting Accident
Taftazani refutes the Basran Mu'tazila claim that God's will is a self-subsisting accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani considers the Basran Mu'tazila view that God's will is a self-subsisting accident to be necessarily false.

Core Idea
What subsists by itself cannot be an attribute; a self-subsisting accident is a contradiction.

Taftazani addresses a specific claim by the Basran Mu'tazila that God's will is an accident subsisting by itself without a locus. He refutes it by appealing to the necessary distinction between attribute and accident, and critiques the inferential basis of the concept of accident itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to get the gist of Taftazani's argument.
Notice the concise and forceful style of refutation.
2
Identify the Basran claim and Taftazani's judgment
Find the Basran claim (إن إرادته حادثة قائمة بنفسها لا بمحل) and Taftazani's immediate response (وبطلانه ضروري). Mark how quickly he dismisses it as self-evidently false.
3
Understand the core argument
Taftazani argues: 'ما يقوم بنفسه لا يكون صفة' (what subsists by itself cannot be an attribute). This is the key logical premise that makes the Basran view contradictory.
This premise is presented as necessary (ضروري), meaning it requires no proof.
4
Explore the alternative argument and the Mu'tazila counterpoint
Taftazani prefers his argument over the usual one that accidents require a locus, because it avoids classifying God's attributes as accidents. Then he mentions a counterpoint from some Mu'tazila: the concept of accident itself is inferential (استدلالي), not necessary. He questions how a judgment (impossibility of self-subsistence) could be made if the concept is inferential.
This shows Taftazani's awareness of deeper epistemological issues.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how this refutation upholds the uniqueness of divine attributes and distances them from created accidents. The passage ends with 'وفساده بين' (its corruption is clear), underscoring the confidence in the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قول أكثر معتزلة البصرة : إن إرادته حادثة قائمة بنفسها لا بمحل ، وبطلانه ضروري فإن ما يقوم بنفسه لا يكون صفة، وهذا أولى من أن يقال: إن العرض لا يقوم إلا بمحل للاطبات على أن صفات الباري ليست من قبيل الأعراض، وفي كلام بعض المعتزلة أن العرض نفسه ليس بضروري، بل استدلالي فكيف حكمه الذي هو استحالة قيامه بنفسه، وفساده بين؟
And among them is the saying of most of the Mu'tazila of Basra: that His will is an accident (ḥādith) subsisting by itself without a locus, and its falsehood is necessary (ḍarūrī), for what subsists by itself cannot be an attribute, and this is more appropriate than saying that an accident (ʿaraḍ) does not subsist except in a locus, in order to establish that the attributes of the Creator (al-Bārī) are not of the category of accidents. And in the speech of some of the Mu'tazila: the accident itself is not necessary (ḍarūrī), but rather inferential (istidlālī); so how can its judgment, which is the impossibility of its subsisting by itself, [be valid]? And its corruption is clear.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a specific Mu'tazili view from the Basran school regarding the nature of God's will. The Basrans claimed that God's will is an accident (ḥādith) that subsists by itself without needing a locus or substrate. The author (Taftazani) immediately declares this false in a necessary (ḍarūrī) way, meaning its falsity is self-evident. He argues that anything that subsists by itself cannot be an attribute (ṣifa), because attributes inhere in a subject. This is a stronger argument than the standard one that accidents cannot subsist without a locus, because it avoids classifying God's attributes as accidents (aʿrāḍ). The author then notes a counterpoint from some Mu'tazila: the notion of an accident itself is not a necessary concept but an inferential one (istidlālī). He questions how the judgment that an accident cannot subsist by itself can be established if the concept itself is inferential. Nonetheless, he dismisses the view as evidently corrupt.

الصفة
العرض
الحادث
الضروري
الاستدلالي
إرادة الله
معتزلة البصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The falsity of the Basran view is merely dogmatic (based on theological authority).
Correction: Taftazani grounds the refutation in a logical distinction: a self-subsisting entity cannot be an attribute. This is presented as necessary (self-evident), not as a mere doctrinal rejection.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author rejects the Basran Mu'tazila view that God's will is a self-subsisting accident?
A) Because accidents cannot exist at all.
B) Because what subsists by itself cannot be an attribute.
C) Because God's will is eternal, not temporal.
D) Because the Basrans are heretics.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the Basran view by arguing that a self-subsisting accident cannot be an attribute, and that this is self-evidently false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will, do you ever imagine it as something that 'happens' or 'changes' within God? The Basran view turns will into a temporary accident. Ask yourself: is your concept of God's will anchored in the eternal, unchanging nature of Allah, or do you subtly project creaturely limitations onto Him? Commit to rectifying any such thought using the truth that His attributes are not like created things.
?
Taftazani says the falsity of the Basran view is 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) — self-evident to any rational person. Yet intelligent scholars disagreed. This should unsettle you: how often do you assume your theological opinions are self-evident when they might be mere inferences? Examine your heart: how would you respond if someone challenged a belief you hold as 'obvious'? Are you open to being wrong, or do you cling to your own certainty?
?
The passage warns against confusing God's attributes with created accidents. Have you ever reduced one of Allah’s attributes — like His mercy, power, or speech — to something you can fully comprehend or compare to human traits? If so, you have unknowingly committed the very sin this lesson guards against. Repent and resolve to affirm His attributes as He describes them, without resemblance.

The Philosophers' Doctrine of Divine Care (al-`Ināyah)
Taftazani explains and critiques the philosophers' equation of will with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' doctrine of divine care (al-`ināyah), which identifies God's will with His eternal knowledge of the best system, and why it effectively denies a distinct attribute of will.

Core Idea
The philosophers reduce God's will to mere knowledge, denying divine choice.

This passage presents a key philosophical challenge to the orthodox understanding of God's will. The philosophers, drawing on Neoplatonic ideas, argue that what we call 'will' in God is nothing but His eternal knowledge of the best possible order. Taftazani explains their view and signals its problematic implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the definition of 'care' (al-`ināyah) and how it is tied to knowledge.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition of will
The philosophers equate God's will with His knowledge of the best system. Find the phrase that describes this equation.
They call this 'care' (al-`ināyah).
3
Note what the philosophers deny
They argue that emanation from God cannot be by intention, nature, or chance. This effectively denies will as a distinct attribute involving choice.
This sets up the orthodox critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قول الحكماء : إن إرادته تعالى، ويسمونها العناية بالمخلوقات . هو تمثل نظام جميع وجود الموجودات من الأزل إلى الأبد في علمه السابق على هذه الموجودات مع الأوقات المترتبة غير المتناهية التي تجب وتليق أن يقع كل موجود منها في واحد من تلك الأوقات. قالوا : وهذا هو المقتضى لإفاضته ذلك النظام على ذلك التركيب، والتفصيل، إذ لا يجوز أن يكون صدوره عن الواجب وعن العقول المجردة بقصد الإرادة، ولا بحسب طبيعته ، ولا على سبيل الاتفاق والجزاف، لأن العلل العالية لا تفعل لغرض في الأمور السافلة ، فقد صرحوا في إثبات هذه العناية بنفي ما نسميه الإرادة، وقد عرفت مرادهم بإحاطة علم الله تعالى بالكل، ولأنها ليست إلا وجود الكل.
And among them is the saying of the philosophers: that His will, may He be exalted, is—and they call it 'care' (al-ʿināya) for creatures—the representation of the system of all the existence of existents from eternity to eternity in His knowledge that precedes these existents, together with the ordered infinite times in which each existent necessarily and fittingly occurs in one of those times. They said: This is what necessitates the outpouring of that system upon that composition and detail, since it is not permissible that its emanation from the Necessary (al-Wājib) and from the abstract intellects be by the intention of will, nor according to its nature, nor by chance and caprice, because the higher causes do not act for a purpose in lower matters. Thus they explicitly affirmed in establishing this 'care' the negation of what we call will. And you have known their meaning by the encompassing knowledge of God Almighty of the whole, and because it is nothing but the existence of the whole.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophical view that identifies God's will with His eternal knowledge of the best possible system of existence, which they term 'care' (al-`ināya). According to this view, God's will is not a separate attribute of volition but rather His knowledge of the optimal order, including all beings from eternity to eternity, along with the infinite sequence of times in which each thing occurs appropriately. The philosophers argue that the emanation of existence from God and the separate intellects cannot be by intended choice, nor by natural necessity, nor by chance. They conclude that what we call 'will' is in fact just this comprehensive knowledge. The author (Taftazani) notes that the philosophers' account effectively denies the will of God as usually understood, i.e., as voluntary choice. He then remarks that the reader already knows that their concept of God's knowledge is equivalently the whole of existence, implying a pantheistic tendency. The critique sets the stage for defending the orthodox view of divine will as a distinct attribute involving purpose and choice.

Key Terms
العناية
تمثل
النظام
العلل العالية
الإرادة
العقول المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' view is compatible with Islamic theism.
Correction: In fact it reduces God's will to mere knowledge and denies divine choice, which contradicts the orthodox attribute of will.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is God's will (irāda) actually?
A) A separate eternal attribute.
B) A temporal accident in God.
C) His eternal knowledge of the best possible system of existence.
D) His command to human beings.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define divine will as God's eternal knowledge of the best system, effectively reducing will to knowledge and denying a distinct attribute of choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though God's will is a mechanical unfolding of His knowledge, or as a personal, purposeful choice that could have been otherwise? How would your prayer life change if you truly believed that God's will is not just a blueprint but a living intention that listens and responds?
?
The philosophers deny purpose in God's actions concerning lower matters. Have you ever doubted that God has a specific purpose for the details of your life, treating your trials or blessings as mere outcomes of a system rather than willed acts of love? Reflect on one event where you need to see God's personal will at work.
?
If God's will were just His knowledge, then everything would be necessary and nothing could be different. Do you really think your choices matter to God, or do you secretly believe that everything is already fixed regardless of your actions? This lesson forces you to choose: is God's will an active choice or a passive awareness?

Al-Najjar's Reduction of God's Will to Negations
Al-Najjar defines God's will as His being not compelled and not forgetful, with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Najjar's definition of God's will as a negation and the objection that it would apply to inanimate objects.

Core Idea
Al-Najjar reduces God's will to mere negatives: not compelled, not forgetful.

Al-Najjar of the Mu'tazila proposed a minimalist view of divine will, defining it not as a positive attribute but as the absence of compulsion and forgetfulness. This lesson examines his definition and the common objection that it would make inanimate objects 'willing' too, which Taftazani dismisses as irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Najjar's definition
Identify how al-Najjar defines God's will: as His being not compelled (ghayr mukrah) and not forgetful (sāhī). This is a negative definition.
Note that this reduces will to a negation, not a positive attribute.
2
Consider the objection
Read the objection: if will is just not being compelled/forgetful, then inanimate objects also qualify. Taftazani responds that the definition is specific to God.
Taftazani dismisses the objection as irrelevant because the definition is only for God's will.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this view was controversial: it denies God's will as a distinct, selective attribute and aligns with philosophical negation.
This view contrasts with Ash'ari orthodoxy that will is a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قول النجار من المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى كونه غير مكره ولا ساه، وقول الكعبي : وكثير من معتزلة بغداد . أن إرادته لفعله هو علمه به ، أو كونه غير مكره ولا ساه، ولفعل غيره هو الأمر به، حتى إن ما لا يكون مأموراً به لا يكون مراداً له، ولا خفاء في أن هذا موافقة للفلاسفة في نفي كون الواجب تعالى مريداً، أي فاعلاً على سبيل القصد والاختيار ومخالفة للنصوص الدالة على أن إرادته تتعلق بشيء دون شيء وقت دون وقت وأنه قد أمر العباد بما لم يشا منهم . قال تعالى : ويريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر، وفي : إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه، ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعاً إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى، ولا فرق بين المشيئة والإرادة إلا عند الكرامية، حيث جعلوا المشيئة صفة واحدة أزلية، تتناول ما يشاء الله بها من حيث تحدث، والإرادة حادثة متعددة، بعدد المرادات، وأما الاعتراض على قول النجار بأنه يوجب كون الجماد مريداً، فليس بشيء لأنه إنما يفسر بذلك إرادة الله تعالى .
And among them is the saying of al-Najjār of the Muʿtazila: that God's will is His being not compelled and not forgetful. And the saying of al-Kaʿbī and many of the Muʿtazila of Baghdad: that His will for His own act is His knowledge of it, or His being not compelled and not forgetful, and for the act of another is His command of it, such that what is not commanded is not willed by Him. And there is no concealment that this is an agreement with the philosophers in negating that the Necessary, exalted, is willing (murīd), i.e., an agent by way of intention and choice, and a contradiction of the texts indicating that His will pertains to something without something, time without time, and that He commanded the servants what He did not will from them. He, exalted, said: 'And Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship for you' (2:185), and: 'Our only word to a thing when We will it is that We say to it "Be" and it is' (16:40), and: 'And if your Lord had willed, those on earth would have believed altogether' (10:99), and so on beyond counting. And there is no difference between wish (mashīʾa) and will (irāda) except among the Karāmiyya, for they made wish a single eternal attribute that encompasses what God wishes by it in terms of what occurs, and will a multiple temporal [attribute] according to the number of objects of will. As for the objection against the saying of al-Najjār that it necessitates that an inanimate object be willing, it is nothing, because he only interprets God's will by that.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents two related Mu'tazili views that reduce God's will to something other than a distinct attribute of volition. Al-Najjar defines God's will simply as His being not compelled (ghayr mukrah) and not forgetful (sāhī), i.e., it is a negative attribute. Al-Ka'bi and many Baghdad Mu'tazila distinguish: for God's own act, His will is His knowledge of it (or His not being compelled/forgetful); for another's act, His will is His command of it. Consequently, anything not commanded is not willed by God. The author (Taftazani) declares that this view aligns with the philosophers in denying that God is truly willing (murīd) in the sense of acting by intentional choice. Moreover, it contradicts numerous Quranic verses that show God's will is selective—He wills something at one time and not another, and He commands what He does not will (e.g., He commands belief but does not will it for all). The author cites three verses and says there are many more. Then he notes an exception: the Karramiyya distinguish between mashī'a (wish) and irāda (will), making mashī'a a single eternal attribute and irāda multiple temporal attributes. Finally, he replies to an objection: if al-Najjar's definition (not compelled/forgetful) applies to inanimate objects, they would be 'willing' too. The author dismisses this as irrelevant because al-Najjar's definition is specifically for God's will, not for creatures. This lesson highlights both the reductionist tendency in kalam and the author's insistence on the orthodox understanding of divine will as a real, distinct, and selective attribute.

Key Terms
النَجّار
المكره
الساه
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Najjar's definition preserves divine will without anthropomorphism.
Correction: Actually, it reduces will to a negation, denying it as a positive attribute and contradicting scriptural evidence of God's selective will.

Checkpoint
According to al-Najjar, what is God's will?
A) His command
B) His being not compelled and not forgetful
C) His knowledge
D) His wish

Key Takeaway
Al-Najjar's definition of God's will as negations reduces it to a non-attribute, which Taftazani rejects as inadequate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find it hard to accept God's decree, do you subconsciously reduce His will to a mere 'not unjust' or 'not arbitrary' instead of affirming that He actively and personally wills everything that happens to you? How will you train your heart this week to submit to His positive will in a specific trial you are facing?
?
This lesson shows that even a theologian's attempt to safeguard God's transcendence can lead to emptying His attributes of meaning. Reflect: Are there any areas of your faith where you 'define God by negation' to avoid the discomfort of His sovereignty? What concrete step will you take today to worship God as actively ruling over every detail of your life?

Al-Ka'bi's Reduction: Will as Knowledge or Command
Al-Ka'bi distinguishes God's will for His own act (knowledge) and for another's act (command), with Taftazani's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's view that God's will for His own act is knowledge and for another's act is command, and why Taftazani considers this a philosophical negation of divine will.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi reduces God's will to knowledge for His own acts and command for others' acts.

Al-Ka'bi and many Baghdad Mu'tazila proposed a dual reduction: God's will regarding His own actions is simply His knowledge (or negative attributes), and regarding others' actions it is His command. This lesson unpacks that view and Taftazani's critique that it amounts to denying that God is truly willing, contradicting the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's distinction
Notice the two parts: for His own act, will is knowledge of it; for another's act, will is command of it. Also note the alternative negative definition (not compelled/forgetful).
This implies whatever is not commanded is not willed by God.
2
Understand Taftazani's critique
Taftazani accuses this view of aligning with philosophers who deny God acts by intention and choice, and of contradicting Quranic verses that show God's will is selective.
He cites verses like 2:185, 16:40, 10:99 to prove that God wills some things and not others.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the problem: if God's will for others is just command, then everything commanded must be willed, but the Quran shows God commands what He does not will (e.g., belief for all).
Thus the reduction fails to account for the distinction between command and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the saying of al-Kaʿbī and many of the Muʿtazila of Baghdad: that His will for His own act is His knowledge of it, or His being not compelled and not forgetful, and for the act of another is His command of it, such that what is not commanded is not willed by Him. According to al-Ka'bi, what is God's will for the acts of another?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ka'bi's view reduces God's will to knowledge and command, removing His personal, intentional choice. Do you ever treat God's will as a set of impersonal rules rather than a living, intentional guidance? Where in your life have you reduced God's will to a mere concept, and what step can you take today to restore a personal relationship with His will?
?
The Quranic verses cited show that God wills ease for you and sometimes wills what He does not command (e.g., He commands all to believe but does not will faith for all). How does this truth challenge you when you face hardship or unanswered prayers? Do you trust that God's will is always purposeful, even when you cannot see it? What would change in your heart if you fully embraced God's selective, intentional will?
?
Taftazani calls al-Ka'bi's view agreement with philosophers who deny God acts by choice. Have you ever, even subconsciously, thought of God as a distant cause rather than a personal, willing Lord? What must you do today to realign your heart with the Quranic depiction of a God who wills with intentional care for every part of your life?

The Karramiyya on Wish vs. Will
The Karramiyya differentiate between eternal mashī'a and temporal irāda.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya distinction between mashī'a (eternal wish) and irāda (temporal will).

Core Idea
The Karramiyya distinguish eternal wish from multiple temporal wills.

While most theologians treat 'wish' (mashī'a) and 'will' (irāda) as synonymous, the Karramiyya sect proposed a distinction: mashī'a is a single eternal attribute encompassing all that occurs, while irāda is a temporal attribute that multiplies according to the objects of will. This lesson examines that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Karramiyya view
Note that they distinguish between mashī'a and irāda: mashī'a is one eternal attribute, irāda is multiple temporal attributes.
This is contrary to the majority view that they are synonyms.
2
Understand the reasoning
Mashī'a (wish) is the eternal determination that encompasses everything God wishes to bring about; irāda (will) is the temporal act of willing each thing as it occurs.
Thus there is one wish but many wills.
3
Consider the implications
Think about what this distinction solves: perhaps it addresses how God's will relates to temporal events without changing His eternal nature.
However, it creates a division in the divine attribute of will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قول النجار من المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى كونه غير مكره ولا ساه، وقول الكعبي : وكثير من معتزلة بغداد . أن إرادته لفعله هو علمه به ، أو كونه غير مكره ولا ساه، ولفعل غيره هو الأمر به، حتى إن ما لا يكون مأموراً به لا يكون مراداً له، ولا خفاء في أن هذا موافقة للفلاسفة في نفي كون الواجب تعالى مريداً، أي فاعلاً على سبيل القصد والاختيار ومخالفة للنصوص الدالة على أن إرادته تتعلق بشيء دون شيء وقت دون وقت وأنه قد أمر العباد بما لم يشا منهم . قال تعالى : ويريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر، وفي : إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه، ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعاً إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى، ولا فرق بين المشيئة والإرادة إلا عند الكرامية، حيث جعلوا المشيئة صفة واحدة أزلية، تتناول ما يشاء الله بها من حيث تحدث، والإرادة حادثة متعددة، بعدد المرادات، وأما الاعتراض على قول النجار بأنه يوجب كون الجماد مريداً، فليس بشيء لأنه إنما يفسر بذلك إرادة الله تعالى .
And among them is the saying of al-Najjār of the Muʿtazila: that God's will is His being not compelled and not forgetful. And the saying of al-Kaʿbī and many of the Muʿtazila of Baghdad: that His will for His own act is His knowledge of it, or His being not compelled and not forgetful, and for the act of another is His command of it, such that what is not commanded is not willed by Him. And there is no concealment that this is an agreement with the philosophers in negating that the Necessary, exalted, is willing (murīd), i.e., an agent by way of intention and choice, and a contradiction of the texts indicating that His will pertains to something without something, time without time, and that He commanded the servants what He did not will from them. He, exalted, said: 'And Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship for you' (2:185), and: 'Our only word to a thing when We will it is that We say to it "Be" and it is' (16:40), and: 'And if your Lord had willed, those on earth would have believed altogether' (10:99), and so on beyond counting. And there is no difference between wish (mashīʾa) and will (irāda) except among the Karāmiyya, for they made wish a single eternal attribute that encompasses what God wishes by it in terms of what occurs, and will a multiple temporal [attribute] according to the number of objects of will. As for the objection against the saying of al-Najjār that it necessitates that an inanimate object be willing, it is nothing, because he only interprets God's will by that.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents two related Mu'tazili views that reduce God's will to something other than a distinct attribute of volition. Al-Najjar defines God's will simply as His being not compelled (ghayr mukrah) and not forgetful (sāhī), i.e., it is a negative attribute. Al-Ka'bi and many Baghdad Mu'tazila distinguish: for God's own act, His will is His knowledge of it (or His not being compelled/forgetful); for another's act, His will is His command of it. Consequently, anything not commanded is not willed by God. The author (Taftazani) declares that this view aligns with the philosophers in denying that God is truly willing (murīd) in the sense of acting by intentional choice. Moreover, it contradicts numerous Quranic verses that show God's will is selective—He wills something at one time and not another, and He commands what He does not will (e.g., He commands belief but does not will it for all). The author cites three verses and says there are many more. Then he notes an exception: the Karramiyya distinguish between mashī'a (wish) and irāda (will), making mashī'a a single eternal attribute and irāda multiple temporal attributes. Finally, he replies to an objection: if al-Najjar's definition (not compelled/forgetful) applies to inanimate objects, they would be 'willing' too. The author dismisses this as irrelevant because al-Najjar's definition is specifically for God's will, not for creatures. This lesson highlights both the reductionist tendency in kalam and the author's insistence on the orthodox understanding of divine will as a real, distinct, and selective attribute.

Key Terms
الكرامية
المشيئة
الإرادة
الأزلية
الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya, how many attributes of wish (mashī'a) are there?
A) One eternal
B) Multiple eternal
C) One temporal
D) Multiple temporal

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya uniquely distinguish eternal mashī'a from temporal irāda, dividing the divine will into two types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face unexpected events, do you see them as part of God's eternal wish or as His temporal will? How does this perspective affect your trust in Him?
?
The Karramiyya made a bold distinction to protect God's transcendence. Is there an area where you need to make a similar distinction to protect your understanding of God? What is it?
?
Do you sometimes struggle to reconcile God's eternal decree with your daily choices? How does the Karramiyya's distinction—one eternal wish and multiple temporal wills—challenge or comfort you?

The Mu'tazili View that Will is the Motive (Dā'ī)
Taftazani reports the Mu'tazili equation of will with motive and the differing scopes of its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili identification of divine will with the motive (dā'ī) and the scope of its application according to two Mu'tazili scholars.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila reduce will to a motive; applying it to God only or to both God and humans.

In the previous lesson, we saw the view that will is knowledge. Here, another reduction is presented: will is identical to the motive that prompts action. Taftazani reports this view from many Mu'tazila, with a notable difference between two thinkers regarding whether this applies to the seen and unseen realms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise structure: the view, then the two scholars' positions.
The passage is one sentence but contains two distinct opinions.
2
Identify the two scholars and their positions
Find Rukn al-Dīn al-Khwārizmī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī. Note that al-Khwārizmī applies the equation to both shāhid and ghāʼib, while al-Baṣrī only to al-ghāʼib.
Al-shāhid refers to the witnessed world (human experience), al-ghāʼib to the unseen (God).
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter whether the motive applies to both or only to God? Consider implications for human free will and divine attribute reductionism.
This touches on the Mu'tazili tendency to deny distinct attributes in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب كثير من المعتزلة إلى أن الإرادة ليست سوى الداعي إلى الفعل وهو اختيار ركن الدين الخوارزمي في الشاهد والغائب جميعاً، وأبي الحسين البصري في الغائب خاصة .
And many of the Muʿtazila went to [the view] that will is nothing but the motive (dāʿī) to the action. And this is the choice of Rukn al-Dīn al-Khwārizmī in the witnessed and the unseen both, and of Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī in the unseen only.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This brief passage presents a further Mu'tazili reduction of divine will. According to many Mu'tazila, will (irāda) is nothing other than the motive (dāʿī) that impels an agent to act. On this view, God's will is not an attribute distinct from His knowledge and power; it is simply the reason or incentive that leads Him to act. The author reports that Rukn al-Dīn al-Khwārizmī applied this definition to both the witnessed world (al-shāhid, i.e., human experience) and the unseen (al-ghāʼib, i.e., God), while Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī applied it only to the unseen, implying a different account for human will. This view continues the theme of reducing divine will to something non-volitional, aligning with the philosophical and earlier Mu'tazili tendencies criticized in the previous lessons.

Key Terms
الداعي
الشاهد
الغائب
ركن الدين الخوارزمي
أبي الحسين البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the motive (dā'ī) is an independent attribute in God.
Correction: The dā'ī is a conceptual factor, not an entity or attribute subsisting in God.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila view reported here, what is God's will (irāda) identical to?
A) His command.
B) His knowledge.
C) The motive (dāʿī) to action.
D) His nature.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila defined divine will as the motive for action, with disagreement on whether this applies universally or only to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision to obey God, do you ever stop to ask whether your will is truly directed toward Him or just following an impulse? How does reducing will to motive affect the sincerity of your actions? This week, before one act of worship, pause and consciously affirm that your will is a real choice for God, not just a reaction.
?
The Mu'tazila disagreed on whether the definition of will as motive applies to God alone or also to humans. In your own life, do you treat God's will as something limited to 'religious' matters while keeping your daily decisions under your own control? Today, identify one area where you habitually exclude God's will and commit to seeking His guidance there.
?
If divine will is merely a reason for action, does that make God's commands less personal? How does your heart respond to the idea that God's will is not a separate attribute but simply His motive? Let this truth humble you: you cannot fully comprehend God's will, but you can trust that every command from Him is rooted in perfect wisdom.

Mu'tazila of Baghdad on Divine Will and Refutation
Presents and refutes the Mu'tazila of Baghdad view on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila of Baghdad's restricted definition of divine will and the scriptural and rational arguments that refute it.

Core Idea
God's will is not reducible to knowledge or command; it is real and selective.

The Mu'tazila of Baghdad attempted to preserve divine transcendence by redefining God's will, but their reduction actually undermines the very meaning of will. This lesson presents their view, shows how it aligns with Greek philosophy, and then deploys clear Quranic texts to demonstrate that God's will is genuine, particular, and not identical to His command. The refutation is sharp and rooted in scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Note the structure: first the Mu'tazila position, then the author's refutation with Quranic evidence.
Pay attention to the two parts of their definition: for God's own act and for others' acts.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila definition
The passage states that for God's own act, His will is either His knowledge or His not being coerced or forgetful. For another's act, it is His command. This reduces will to something else.
Compare this to the Ash'ari view that will is an eternal attribute distinct from knowledge and command.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author makes two points: this view agrees with philosophers who deny God acts by purpose and choice, and it contradicts scripture. List the three Quranic verses cited.
Think about why each verse is effective in showing that will can be selective and distinct from command.
4
Reflect on the implications
If God's will were just knowledge, then He would will everything He knows, including evil and disbelief. But the verses show He wills ease, not hardship, and could will universal belief but does not.
This preserves divine wisdom and the reality of human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من معتزلة بغداد أن إرادته لفعله هو علمه به، أو كونه غير مكره ولا ساه، ولفعل غيره هو الأمر به، حتى إن ما لا يكون مأموراً به لا يكون مراداً له، ولا خفاء في أن هذا موافقة للفلاسفة في نفي كون الواجب تعالى مريداً، أي فاعلاً على سبيل القصد والاختيار ومخالفة للنصوص الدالة على أن إرادته تتعلق بشيء دون شيء وقت دون وقت وأنه قد أمر العباد بما لم يشأ منهم. قال تعالى: ويريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر. وفي: إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه. ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعاً. إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى.
From the Mu'tazila of Baghdad: His will for His act is His knowledge of it, or His being not coerced nor forgetful; and for another's act it is His command. Such that whatever is not commanded is not willed by Him. And it is not hidden that this is an agreement with the philosophers in negating that the Necessary (al-Wajib), exalted is He, is a willer—i.e., an agent by purpose and choice—and a contradiction of the texts indicating that His will pertains to some things and not others, and to some times and not others, and that He commanded servants with what He did not will from them. He said, exalted: 'And Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' (2:185); and: 'Indeed Our word to a thing when We intend it' (16:40); and: 'And if your Lord had willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them entirely' (10:99). And so on beyond enumeration.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal position and its critique. It first states the view of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad: they define God's will for His own act as either His knowledge of that act or the state of being uncontrolled and not forgetful. For acts of others, they define it as His command, so that what is not commanded is not willed. The author then refutes this, saying it clearly agrees with philosophers in denying that God is a willer in the sense of acting by purpose and free choice. Moreover, it contradicts scriptural texts that show God's will attaches to some things and not others, to some times and not others, and that He commands people to do things He did not will from them—like believing without coercion. Three Quranic verses are cited: one showing God intends ease not hardship, one stating that His word to a thing when He intends it is 'Be', and one stating that if He willed, all on earth would believe. The passage concludes that such proofs are countless.

أمر
الفلاسفة
قصد
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is merely His knowledge or His command; it is not a real, selective attribute.
Correction: Scripture and reason show God's will is real, particular, and distinct from knowledge and command.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila of Baghdad, what is God's will for another's act?
A) His knowledge of it
B) His command
C) The act itself
D) His being uncompelled

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila of Baghdad's reduction of will to knowledge or command is refuted by clear Quranic evidence that God's will is real, selective, and not identical to His command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God 'willed' it? This lesson shows that God commands what He does not will. How does this change your understanding of personal responsibility and reliance on His mercy? What will you do the next time you feel stuck in a pattern of disobedience?
?
God wills ease for you, not hardship (2:185). In your current struggles, do you truly believe that God's will for you is ease, or do you doubt His good intention? How can you actively surrender your hardship to His wise and selective will today?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced God's will to an abstract concept—knowledge or command. Are you ever tempted to think of God as a distant, impersonal force rather than a personal Lord who chooses and wills? This lesson calls you to relate to Him as a living, purposeful agent. What one change can you make in your prayer life to speak to Him as a willer, not a knower?

Karramiyya Distinction: Mashiyya Eternal, Irada Created
States the Karramiyya view and refutes an objection to al-Najjar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya's unique distinction between mashiyya (will) as an eternal attribute and irada (will) as multiple created acts, and why a common objection against al-Najjar is baseless.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya separate God's eternal will from created acts of will.

Most Muslim theologians treat mashiyya and irada as synonyms. The Karramiyya, however, draw a sharp line: mashiyya is a single eternal attribute, while irada is multiple and created. This passage presents their view and also defends al-Najjar from a misguided objection. Understanding this clarifies how different sects conceive of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a general statement, then the Karramiyya position, then an objection and refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Karramiyya definitions
Find how the Karramiyya define mashiyya and irada. Mashiyya is a single eternal attribute; irada is created and multiple, corresponding to each willed thing.
This is the key doctrinal point.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
Understand the objection against al-Najjar: his view allegedly makes an inanimate object a 'willer'. Then see why the author dismisses it: al-Najjar is only explaining divine will, not attributing will to the object.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا فرق بين المشيئة والإرادة إلا عند الكرامية، حيث جعلوا المشيئة صفة واحدة أزلية، تتناول ما يشاء الله بها من حيث تحدث، والإرادة حادثة متعددة، بعدد المرادات، وأما الاعتراض على قول النجار بأنه يوجب كون الجماد مريداً، فليس بشيء لأنه إنما يفسر بذلك إرادة الله تعالى.
And there is no difference between mashiyya (will) and irada (will) except among the Karramiyya, for they made mashiyya a single eternal attribute, encompassing whatever Allah wills by it as it occurs, and irada as created and multiple according to the number of willed things. As for the objection to al-Najjar's saying that it makes an inanimate object a willer, it is nothing, because he only interprets thereby the will of Allah Most High.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage briefly addresses the distinction between mashiyya and irada. It states that there is no difference between these two terms except among the Karramiyya sect. They define mashiyya as a single eternal attribute that applies to whatever Allah wills as it comes into existence, while irada is multiple temporal attributes, one for each willed thing. Then an objection is raised against al-Najjar's view: his definition allegedly implies that an inanimate object (like a stone) could be a 'willer'. The author dismisses this objection as baseless, because al-Najjar is only explaining divine will in that manner, not attributing will to the object.

إرادة (irada)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Najjar's view implies that inanimate objects have will (irada).
Correction: The objection is invalid because al-Najjar is only interpreting God's will, not attributing will to the object.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya uniquely distinguish mashiyya as an eternal single attribute from irada as multiple created acts of will, and the objection against al-Najjar is dismissed as unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

When you hear a criticism of a theological view, do you rush to accept it, or do you first seek to understand the actual claim? How can you train yourself to be more careful in judging others' beliefs?
?
The Karramiyya made a distinction that others do not. Is there an area in your own faith where you have added a distinction (like between God's mercy and justice) that might actually lead to confusion? How can you seek a more unified understanding of Allah's attributes from the Quran and Sunnah?
