Irada as Da'i: Mu'tazila View and Refutation
Presents and refutes the Mu'tazila view on irada as da'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that irada is the da'i (motive) and the refutation based on infinite regress and the nature of human consciousness.

Core Idea
The will cannot be a motive because that would require an infinite chain of wills.

The Mu'tazila argued that God's will is simply His knowledge of benefit. But their own proof unwittingly leads to a logical paradox. Let's examine their reasoning and the counterarguments from the Sharh al-Maqasid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the passage where the Mu'tazila define irada as the da'i (motive) to action and their proof from ordinary language and the Quran.
Notice how they equate will with knowledge of benefit for God.
2
Understand the objection
The text raises an objection: if irada is an act of the willer, the agent must be conscious of it. Yet we are not conscious of any preponderating factor besides the da'i.
This objection uses the concept of 'shu'ur' (consciousness).
3
Examine the refutation's first part
The refutation distinguishes two meanings of 'act of the willer': mere attribution vs. voluntary effect. If the latter, it leads to infinite regress.
The key is that reward and punishment attach to consequences, not the will itself.
4
Examine the refutation's second part
The refutation denies the Mu'tazila claim that we are not conscious of any preponderating factor besides the da'i; we find an inclined state (hala milaniyya).
The passage is incomplete but the thrust is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب كثير من المعتزلة إلى أن الإرادة ليست سوى الداعي إلى الفعل وهو اختيار ركن الدين الخوارزمي في الشاهد والغائب جميعاً، وأبي الحسين البصري في الغائب خاصة. قالوا: وهو العلم أو الاعتقاد أو الظن باشتمال الفعل والترك على المصلحة ولما امتنع في حق الباري تعالى الظن والاعتقاد كان الداعي في حقه تعالى هو العلم بالمصلحة، واحتجوا بأن الإرادة فعل المريد قطعاً واتفاقاً. يقال فلان يريد هذا، ويكره ذاك، ولهذا يمدح بها، ويذم ويثاب عليها ويعاقب. قال الله تعالى: تريدون عرض الدنيا والله يريد الآخرة. وقال: منكم من يريد الدنيا ومنكم من يريد الآخرة. فهذا الفعل لو كان غير الداعي لكان للفاعل شعور به ضرورة أن الفاعل هو المؤثر بالشيء بالقصد والاختيار، وذلك لا يكون إلا بعد الشعور لكن اللازم باطل، لأنا لا نشعر عند الفعل أو الترك بمرجح سوى الداعي الخالص، أو المترجح على الصافي. والجواب: أنه إن أريد بكونها فعلاً للمريد مجرد استنادها إليه، كما في قولنا: فلان يقدر على كذا، ويعجز عن كذا، فهذا لا يقتضي كونه أثراً صادراً عنه بالقصد والاختيار ليلزم الشعور به وإن أريد أنه أثر له بطريق القصد والاختيار فممنوع، ولا يبعد دعوى الاتفاق على نقيض ذلك. كيف ولو كان كذلك لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى وتسلسلت ثم ترتب الثواب والعقاب على الإرادة إنما هو باعتبار ما يلزمها من الأفعال، أو تحصيل الدواعي، أو نفي الصوارف أو نحو ذلك مما للقصد فيه مدخل، وإما المدح والذم على الشيء فلا يقتضيان كونه فعلاً اختيارياً وهو ظاهر. ثم لا نسلم أنه لا شعور لنا بمرجح سوى الداعي بمعنى اعتقاد المصلحة والمنفعة، بل نجد من أنفسنا حالة ميلانية منبعثة عن
And many of the Mu'tazila went to the view that irada (will) is nothing but the da'i (motive) to the action. This is the choice of Rukn al-Din al-Khwarizmi regarding both the witnessed and the absent, and of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri regarding the absent specifically. They said: It is knowledge, or belief, or opinion about the act and omission comprising benefit. Since belief and opinion are impossible regarding the Most High, the da'i in His case is knowledge of benefit. And they argued that irada is an act of the willer (fi'l al-murid) decisively and unanimously. One says: 'So-and-so wills this and dislikes that,' and for this he is praised and blamed, rewarded and punished. Allah Most High said: 'You desire the goods of this world while Allah desires the Hereafter' (8:67); and He said: 'Among you are those who desire this world and among you are those who desire the Hereafter' (3:152). Now if this act (irada) were other than the da'i, the agent would have consciousness of it necessarily because the agent is the one who effects the thing with purpose and choice, and that cannot be except after consciousness. But the consequent is false, for we are not conscious, at the time of action or omission, of a preponderating factor (murajjih) other than the pure da'i or that which outweighs over the clear. And the answer: If by its being an act of the willer is meant merely its attribution to him, as in our saying 'So-and-so is able to do this and unable to do that,' then this does not entail its being an effect proceeding from him with purpose and choice so that consciousness would be necessary. And if it is meant that it is an effect from him by way of purpose and choice, then it is denied, and it is not far to claim consensus on the contrary. How so? If it were like that, it would need another irada, leading to infinite regress. Then the reward and punishment for irada is only by virtue of what follows from it of actions, or the obtaining of motives, or the removal of deterrents, or the like in which purpose plays a part. As for praise and blame for a thing, they do not necessitate it being a voluntary act, and this is evident. Then we do not concede that we have no consciousness of a preponderating factor besides the da'i in the sense of belief in benefit and advantage. Rather, we find in ourselves an inclined state (hala milaniyya) originating from...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila view that irada (will) is the da'i (motive) that drives an action, then provides a refutation. It begins by stating that many Mu'tazila, specifically Rukn al-Din al-Khwarizmi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, define irada as the da'i to action. They explain that this da'i is knowledge (or belief or opinion) that an action or its omission contains benefit. For God, since belief and opinion are impossible, His da'i is knowledge of benefit. Their proof is linguistic: we say 'So-and-so wills this' and he is praised or blamed, rewarded or punished, so irada must be an act of the willer (since it is the basis of moral responsibility). They support it with Quranic verses distinguishing God's will and human will. Then an objection is raised: if irada is an act of the willer, then the agent must be conscious of it, because acting with purpose and choice requires consciousness. But we are not conscious of any preponderating factor besides the da'i (the belief in benefit). The answer refutes this: if 'act of the willer' means merely attribution (like ability or inability), it does not entail consciousness. If it means a deliberately chosen effect, this is denied, and it would lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul) because each act of will would need another will. Reward and punishment for irada is only considered because of what follows from it (actions, motives, removal of deterrents), not because irada itself is a voluntary act. Moreover, the Mu'tazila claim we are not conscious of any preponderating factor besides the da'i is false; we do find in ourselves an inclined state (hala milaniyya) arising from... (the text cuts off).

Key Terms

إرادة (irada)
مصلحة (benefit)
علم (knowledge)
فعل المريد (act of the willer)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
شعور (consciousness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila argument from ordinary language (praise and blame) proves that irada is a voluntary act of the willer.
Correction: The refutation shows that this only proves attribution, not voluntary action, and that reward/punishment relate to consequences, not the will itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with the Mu'tazila claim that irada is an act of the willer?
A) It contradicts the Quran
B) It would require infinite regress of wills
C) It implies God is passive
D) It denies free will

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila equation of will with motive fails because it leads to infinite regress and ignores the distinction between attribution and voluntary action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between two options, do you ever reduce your will to a mere calculation of benefits, ignoring the deeper 'inclined state' (hala milaniyya) that already points you in a direction? How often do you override that inner pull because it doesn't fit your rationalization?
?
The Mu'tazila thought will is just knowledge of what's beneficial. But you know moments when you willed something despite knowing it wasn't beneficial. Can you recall such a moment? What does that say about the nature of your will? Will you own that truth today?
?
If every act of will required another will, you'd never act. Yet you act every day. Where in your life have you been paralyzed by overthinking, and how does this lesson's call to recognize your already-present inclined state free you to move?

Objection and Answer: Does the Divine Will Lead to Infinite Regress?
The author refutes the objection that if will is an act, it would require another will ad infinitum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that if will is an act of the agent, it would require another will ad infinitum, by distinguishing between mere attribution and voluntary act.

Core Idea
Will as act does not imply regress; only voluntary acts require another will.

The objection seems powerful: if the divine will is an act of Allah, doesn't it itself need to be willed? That would lead to an infinite chain. The author shows that this objection rests on a confusion about what it means for something to be an 'act'. By clarifying two different senses—mere attribution versus voluntary production—the author dissolves the regress and also explains how reward and punishment attach to will through its effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if will is an act, the agent must be aware of it, but we aren't aware of any preponderant factor besides the motive.
Notice the objector uses the principle that voluntary action requires awareness.
2
Grasp the answer's key distinction
The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'act of the agent': (a) mere attribution, like saying someone is able, and (b) voluntary act with intention and choice.
This distinction is central to avoiding the regress.
3
See the regress is avoided
If will is merely attributed, no regress follows. If it is a voluntary act, it would need another will—but the author denies that will is a voluntary act.
The regress only arises under the second interpretation, which is rejected.
4
Learn the implications for reward and punishment
Reward and punishment for will are not because will itself is a voluntary act, but due to its consequences: actions, acquiring motives, or removing deterrents.
Praise and blame do not prove voluntariness, as is evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا الفعل لو كان غير الداعي لكان للفاعل شعور به ضرورة أن الفاعل هو المؤثر بالشيء بالقصد والاختيار، وذلك لا يكون إلا بعد الشعور لكن اللازم باطل، لأنا لا نشعر عند الفعل أو الترك بمرجح سوى الداعي الخالص، أو المترجح على الصافي . والجواب : أنه إن أريد بكونها فعلاً للمريد مجرد استنادها إليه، كما في قولنا : فلان يقدر على كذا، ويعجز عن كذا ، فهذا لا يقتضي كونه أثراً صادراً عنه بالقصد والاختيار ليلزم الشعور به وإن أريد أنه أثر له بطريق القصد والاختيار فممنوع ، ولا يبعد دعوى الاتفاق على نقيض ذلك. كيف ولو كان كذلك لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى وتسلسلت ثم ترتب الثواب والعقاب على الإرادة إنما هو باعتبار ما يلزمها من الأفعال، أو تحصيل الدواعي، أو نفي الصوارف أو نحو ذلك مما للقصد فيه مدخل ، وإما المدح والذم على الشيء فلا يقتضيان كونه فعلاً اختيارياً وهو ظاهر.
This act, if it were other than the motive, would require the agent to have awareness of it, necessarily, because the agent is the one who effects the thing with intention and choice, and that is only after awareness. But the consequent is false, because we are not aware at the time of action or omission of a preponderant factor other than the pure motive, or the one that outweighs the pure. The answer: If by its being an act of the agent is meant merely its being attributed to him, as in our saying: So-and-so is able to do this and unable to do that—this does not entail that it is an effect proceeding from him with intention and choice, so as to necessitate awareness of it. And if it is meant that it is an effect of his by way of intention and choice, then it is denied, and the claim of consensus to the contrary is not unlikely. How so? For if it were so, it would need another will, and [the series] would be infinite. Moreover, reward and punishment for will are only by virtue of what follows from it of actions, or the acquiring of motives, or the removal of deterrents, or the like, wherein intention has a part. As for praise and blame for a thing, they do not entail that it is a voluntary act, and that is evident.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection begins by assuming that if will is an act of the agent (فعل المريد) but distinct from the motive (الداعي), then the agent must be aware of it, because voluntary action requires awareness. But the objector argues that we are not aware of any preponderant factor besides the motive itself. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'act of the agent'. First, if it means mere attribution (مجرد استنادها إليه), like saying someone is able or unable, that does not imply the will is a voluntary product requiring awareness. Second, if it means a voluntary act (أثر بالقصد والاختيار), then the answer denies that claim, noting that the consensus on the opposite is plausible. The answer then points out that if will were a voluntary act, it would need another will to cause it, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). Reward and punishment for will are not because will itself is a voluntary act, but because of what it entails: actions, acquiring motives, or removing deterrents—things involving intention. Similarly, praise and blame for a thing do not prove it is a voluntary act; that is evident (وهو ظاهر).

العقاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if will is an act, it must be directly willed, leading to regress.
Correction: The answer shows that this only applies if it is a voluntary act, which is denied; mere attribution does not require another will.

Checkpoint
How does the author avoid the infinite regress objection against will being an act?
A) By denying that will is an act of the agent at all
B) By distinguishing between mere attribution and voluntary act
C) By claiming that will is not an attribute but an action
D) By accepting the regress as necessary

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection against will being an act is avoided by clarifying that 'act' can mean mere attribution, which does not require another will, and that will is not a voluntary act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is not a voluntary act in the human sense, how does that confront your inclination to judge His decisions based on your limited understanding? When life doesn't go as you planned, do you ever question God's wisdom? How can this lesson reshape your response to His decree?
?
The lesson teaches that reward and punishment for will are based on what follows from it, not on the will itself. Think of a recent intention you had—was it purely good or mixed? Are you aware of hidden motives you might have? How does this truth push you to seek sincerity and rely on Allah's mercy rather than your own perceived purity?
?
Consider the infinite regress: if every will needed another will, there would be no first. How does this truth about God's self-sufficiency expose your own dependence? In what areas of your life are you trying to be self-sufficient, forgetting that even your will is derived from Allah? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge His sovereignty over your intentions?

Will and Perception of Benefit: A Critical Distinction
Conclusion distinguishing will from benefit perception, with divine exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the will is distinct from the mere perception of benefit, and why God's case requires separate treatment.

Core Idea
Will is not just seeing what's good; it includes an inclinational state.

In this concluding passage, the author firmly distinguishes the concept of will from the perception of benefit. While this distinction is obvious to any rational person familiar with language, he notes that God's will might be understood differently—a point that opens a deeper theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's conclusion come through.
Notice the author's tone: he says the distinction should not be hidden from any rational person.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find the two concepts being contrasted: 'will' (الإرادة) and 'perception of benefit' (الشعور بالمصلحة). Understand that they are not the same.
3
Consider the rational basis
The author says this distinction is obvious to anyone 'acquainted with meanings and usages' (العارف بالمعاني والأوضاع). Reflect on why linguistic usage supports the distinction.
Think about how we use the word 'will' in everyday language.
4
Note the special treatment of God
The author then says that for God, one might negate the 'inclinational state' (الحالة الميلانية) and instead restrict will to knowledge of benefit. This is a separate discussion.
This acknowledges that divine attributes may be unique.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why this distinction matters: if God's will is just knowledge of benefit, it could differ from human will significantly. This affects how we understand divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة. فكون مسمى لفظ الإرادة مغايراً للشعور بالمصلحة في الفعل أو الترك مما لا ينبغي أن يخفى على العاقل العارف بالمعاني والأوضاع . نعم، لو ادعى في حق الباري تعالى انتفاء مثل هذه الحالة الميلانية والاقتصار على العلم بالمصلحة، فذلك بحث آخر.
In sum, that the meaning of the word 'will' is different from the perception of benefit in action or omission is something that should not be hidden from any rational person acquainted with meanings and usages. Yes, if one claims with respect to God Most High the negation of such an inclinational state and restricting [His will] to knowledge of benefit, that is another discussion.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the discussion on the nature of will. The author asserts that it is evident to any rational person (العاقل العارف بالمعاني والأوضاع) that the term 'will' (الإرادة) does not mean mere perception of benefit (الشعور بالمصلحة) in action or omission. This is a summary judgment based on the preceding arguments. However, the author adds an important qualification: if someone claims that for God (في حق الباري تعالى) there is no such inclinational state (الحالة الميلانية) and that His will is simply knowledge of benefit, that is a separate discussion (بحث آخر). This acknowledges that while the general linguistic and rational analysis applies to human will, the divine case may be different because God is not subject to inclinational states. Thus the author leaves open the possibility that God's will could be identified with His knowledge of what is beneficial, though this requires further investigation.

The Divine Will Encompasses All Things: Sunni Doctrine
The author states the Sunni doctrine that everything willed by God happens and everything that happens is willed by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that God's will encompasses all existence, including what He does not command or approve, and how this differs from the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
Everything that happens is willed by Allah, even if He does not command or approve it.

This passage from al-Maqasid presents a foundational Sunni belief about divine will: its scope is universal. The author states that whatever Allah wills comes to pass, and whatever occurs is willed by Him, regardless of whether He is pleased with it. This stands in contrast to the Mu'tazilite position, which limits the effectiveness of Allah's will concerning human actions. The passage also notes that this topic is closely linked to the later discussion on the creation of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the key phrases: 'كل ما أراد الله تعالى فهو كائن' and 'كل كائن فهو مراد له'.
The passage is a 'khatima' or conclusion, so it summarizes foundational doctrine.
2
Identify the Sunni position
Notice that the Sunni position is stated clearly: everything God wills exists, and everything that exists is willed by Him. Also note the qualification 'even if it is not pleasing, nor commanded'.
This emphasizes that God's will is not limited to what He commands or approves.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view
Read the Mu'tazilite position: they claim God wills obedience from disbelievers but that will does not occur, and sins occur without His will. Understand the difference.
The Mu'tazila separate divine will from effectiveness in moral matters.
4
Reflect on the Salaf saying
The passage cites the well-known Salaf maxim: 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' This encapsulates the Sunni doctrine succinctly.
This saying is often used in Islamic theology to assert divine omnipotence.
5
Note the postponement
The author says detailed discussion is deferred to the topic of acts (af'al) because of its connection to the creation of actions. Recognize that this passage sets the stage for that later discussion.
This shows the systematic structure of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة
خاتمة
إرادته تعم جميع الكائنات وبالعكس خلافاً للمعتزلة في الأصلين وسيجيء في بحث الأفعال.
قال: خاتمة مذهب أهل الحق أن كل ما أراد الله تعالى فهو كائن، وأن كل كائن فهو مراد له، وإن لم يكن مرضياً، ولا مأموراً به، بل منهياً عنه، وهذا ما اشتهر من السلف أن ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن، وخالفت المعتزلة في الأصلين ذهاباً إلى أنه يريد من الكفار والعصاة الإيمان والطاعة ولا يقع مراده، ويقع منهم الكفر والمعاصي، ولا يريدها، وكذا جميع ما يقع في العالم من الشرور والقبائح، وأخرنا الكلام في ذلك إلى بحث الأفعال لما له من زيادة التعلق بمسألة خلق الأعمال.
He said: Conclusion. Conclusion: His will encompasses all things and vice versa, contrary to the Mu'tazila in the two principles, and it will come in the discussion of acts. He said: Conclusion: The doctrine of the people of truth is that everything that God Most High wills, it exists, and everything that exists is willed by Him, even if it is not pleasing, nor commanded, but rather forbidden. This is what is well-known from the Salaf: what God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen. The Mu'tazila opposed in the two principles, going to the view that He wills from the disbelievers and sinners faith and obedience, but His will does not occur, and there occurs from them disbelief and sins, and He does not will them, and likewise all that occurs in the world of evils and ugly acts. And we have deferred the discussion of that to the discussion of acts because of its greater connection to the issue of creation of actions.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a conclusory statement (khatima) on the divine will. The author first announces the topic and states that God's will encompasses all things and vice versa, contrary to the Mu'tazila in two principles (likely that God wills only good, and that His will is always effective). He then clarifies the Sunni position: everything God wills comes to exist, and everything that exists is willed by Him, regardless of whether He is pleased with it or commands it—it may even be prohibited. He cites the well-known Salaf saying, 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' The Mu'tazila, by contrast, hold that God wills faith and obedience from disbelievers and sinners, but that will does not occur, and that sins occur without His will, similarly for all evils. The author notes that detailed discussion is postponed to the topic of acts (af'al) because of its strong connection to the issue of the creation of actions (khalq al-a'mal).

Key Terms
إرادة الله
الكائنات
المعتزلة
الأصلين
السلف
مرضي
مأمور
منهي
الشرور
القبائح
خلق الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God wills everything, then He wills sin and evil in the same way He wills obedience, meaning He approves of them.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God wills everything in terms of existence, but He may not be pleased with it or command it. He wills all events, including evil, as part of His decree, but He does not approve of moral evil.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni doctrine stated in this passage, what is the relationship between God's will and the existence of things?
A) God wills only good actions, and evil actions occur without His will.
B) Everything that God wills exists, and everything that exists is willed by Him.
C) God wills all things, but some of His will does not come to pass.
D) God's will is limited to actions He commands.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is that Allah's will is all-encompassing: everything that exists is willed by Him, even if He does not command or approve it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If Allah wills even forbidden acts, does that make Him the author of your sins? How does this lesson compel you to take full responsibility for your actions while still affirming that nothing occurs outside His will?
?
The text says 'what Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' Is there any area of your life where you secretly think something happened outside His will? How does holding this doubt affect your trust in His sovereignty and your peace of heart?

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Divine Will vs. Mu'tazila
Ash'ari doctrine of divine will vs. Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God's will is all-encompassing and the Mu'tazila counterview, and why this matters for divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Everything that exists is willed by God—even if He does not approve it.

This passage presents the concise Ash'ari creed on divine will (الإرادة) and contrasts it with the Mu'tazila view. The author, Sharif al-Jurjani, outlines the core doctrine known from the Salaf: 'What God wills happens; what He does not will does not happen.' He then highlights the Mu'tazila opposition on two key points, postponing a fuller discussion to the investigation of actions. This lesson grounds you in the foundational Sunni position on divine will versus the Mu'tazila rationalist approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the phrase 'مَا شَاءَ اللَّهُ كَانَ' and the two principles of Mu'tazila opposition.
The passage is a conclusion (خاتمة) so it summarizes a larger discussion.
2
Understand the Ash'ari position
Identify the core claim: everything God wills comes to be, and everything that comes to be is willed by Him. Note that this includes even actions He prohibits (like disbelief).
This is the 'مذهب أهل الحق'.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Find the two principles: (1) God wills faith from disbelievers but it does not occur; (2) disbelief occurs without God willing it. The Mu'tazila say God only wills good, not evil.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask: If God wills everything, does He will evil? The Ash'ari answer: yes, He wills it in the sense of creating it, but He does not approve it or command it. This distinction is key.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة إرادته تعم جميع الكائنات وبالعكس خلافاً للمعتزلة في الأصلين وسيجيء في بحث الأفعال . قال : خاتمة مذهب أهل الحق أن كل ما أراد الله تعالى فهو كائن ، وأن كل كائن فهو مراد له ، وإن لم يكن مرضياً، ولا مأموراً به، بل منهياً عنه، وهذا ما اشتهر من السلف أن ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، وخالفت المعتزلة في الأصلين ذهاباً إلى أنه يريد من الكفار والعصاة الإيمان والطاعة ولا يقع مراده، ويقع منهم الكفر والمعاصي، ولا يريدها، وكذا جميع ما يقع في العالم من الشرور والقبائح ، وأخرنا الكلام في ذلك إلى بحث الأفعال لما له من زيادة التعلق بمسألة خلق الأعمال.
Conclusion: His will encompasses all existing things and vice versa, contrary to the Mu'tazila on the two principles, and it will come in the investigation of actions. He said: Conclusion: The doctrine of the people of truth is that everything that God, exalted, wills, it comes to be, and that everything that comes to be is willed by Him, even if it is not approved, nor commanded, but rather prohibited. And this is what is well-known from the Salaf: 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' The Mu'tazila opposed in two principles, holding that He wills from the disbelievers and sinners faith and obedience, but His will does not occur, and from them occur disbelief and sins, and He does not will them. Likewise all that occurs in the world of evils and abominations. And we have deferred the discussion of that to the investigation of actions because of its additional connection to the issue of the creation of deeds.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal conclusion (خاتمة) that first gives a concise summary: God's will encompasses all existing things, and conversely all existing things are willed by God, contrary to the Mu'tazila on two points. Then it expands the Ash'ari creed. The author states that the people of truth (أهل الحق) believe that everything God wills necessarily comes to be, and everything that comes to be is willed by Him, regardless of whether it is approved (مرضيّ), commanded (مأمور به), or prohibited (منهي عنه). This is the well-known Salaf formulation: 'مَا شَاءَ اللَّهُ كَانَ وَمَا لَمْ يَشَأْ لَمْ يَكُنْ' (What God wills happens, what He does not will does not happen). The Mu'tazila disagreed on two counts: first, that God wills faith and obedience from disbelievers and sinners, yet these do not occur, implying God's will is ineffective; second, that disbelief and sins occur from them without God willing them. The same applies to all evils and abominations in the world. The author notes that a fuller discussion is postponed to the investigation of actions (بحث الأفعال) because of its close connection to the issue of the creation of deeds (خلق الأعمال).

Key Terms

معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view implies that God wants evil and approves it.
Correction: The Ash'ari distinction: God wills evil in the sense of creating it, but He does not approve it nor command it. His will encompasses all, but His good pleasure (رضا) is only for what He commands.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on whether God wills disbelief from disbelievers?
A) Yes, and He approves it.
B) Yes, but He does not approve it.
C) No, He does not will it.
D) Only as a punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of divine will affirms that everything that occurs is willed by God, while distinguishing between His creative will and His approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity that seems unjust, can you truly say from your heart, 'ما شاء الله كان'? If not, what does that reveal about your trust in God's will?
?
Think of a sin you committed recently. Do you believe that sin occurred by God's will? If yes, how does that change your understanding of repentance? Are you willing to both accept that God willed it and still hate it as He does?
?
The Mu'tazila were troubled by the idea that God wills evil. Are you ever troubled by the same? When you see evil in the world, do you tend to distance God from it, or do you affirm His will while trusting His wisdom? What would it cost you to completely submit to 'whatever God wills happens'?

Proofs for God's Life, Hearing, and Sight
Al-Sanusi provides scriptural, consensus, and rational proofs for God's attributes of life, hearing, and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural, consensus, and rational proofs that establish God's attributes of life, hearing, and sight.

Core Idea
God's life, hearing, and sight are proven by scripture, consensus, reason, and the impossibility of divine deficiency.

The author begins the fifth investigation by presenting multiple lines of evidence for God's attributes of life, hearing, and sight. He cites divine scriptures, prophetic consensus, and rational arguments based on God's knowledge and power. These proofs together yield certainty, though some may admit debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of proof
Read the passage and identify the scriptural, consensus, and rational proofs mentioned. Note how each is presented.
The author mentions 'divine scriptures', 'consensus of prophets', and 'rational indication'.
2
Understand the rational argument
Focus on the reasoning: knowledge and power indicate life, and life indicates the validity of hearing and sight. This is a causal inference within kalam.
The argument assumes that knowledge and power are already established for God.
3
Reflect on the deficiency argument
Consider why lacking these attributes would be a deficiency and why that is impossible for God. This shows the necessity of these attributes.
The author states that deficiency is a shortcoming in perfection, and God must be free from that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس في أنه سميع بصير حي . في أنه حي سميع بصير شهدت به الكتب الإلهية وأجمع عليه الأنبياء، بل جمهور العقلاء، ودل العلم والقدرة على الحياة والحياة على صحة السمع والبصر فيثبتان بالفعل ولا خفاء في أن الخلو عن هذه الصفات في حق من يصح اتصافه بها نقيصة وقصور في الكمال لا أقل، وباطل أن يتسم الواجب تعالى بالنقصان أو بكونه أقل كمالاً من الإنسان فهذه بجملتها تفيد القطع، وإن كان في البعض للجدال مجال ويثبت على أصل أصحابنا صفات قديمة هي الحياة والسمع والبصر، ولا يلزم قدم المسموع والمبصر لجواز حدوث التعلق وما يقال إنها نفس اعتدال المزاج وتأثير الحاسة أو مشروطة بذلك ممنوع في الشاهد فكيف في الغائب.
The fifth investigation: that He is Hearing, Seeing, Living. That He is Living, Hearing, Seeing: the divine scriptures have witnessed it, the prophets have agreed upon it, indeed the majority of rational people. Knowledge and power indicate life, and life indicates the validity of hearing and sight, so they are established by actuality. And it is no secret that the absence of these attributes for one to whom it is befitting to be described with them is a deficiency and a shortcoming in perfection, at the least. And it is false for the Necessary, exalted, to be characterized by deficiency or to be less perfect than a human. So these altogether yield certainty, although in some there is room for debate. And it is established according to our school's principle that there are eternal attributes: life, hearing, and sight. And the eternality of the heard and seen does not follow, because of the possibility of the occurrence of relation. And what is said that they are just temperament and the effect of the sense or conditioned by that is rejected in the witnessed, so how in the unseen?
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is the Fifth Investigation (المبحث الخامس) which proves that God is Living, Hearing, and Seeing. The author begins by stating that these attributes are witnessed by the divine scriptures, agreed upon by the prophets, and indeed by the majority of rational people. Then he gives a rational proof: knowledge and power (which are already established for God) indicate life because living is a prerequisite for knowledge and power, and life indicates the validity of hearing and sight. Therefore, these attributes are established as actual (بالفعل) for God. The author argues that lacking these attributes for one who can possibly possess them is a deficiency (نقيصة) and a shortcoming in perfection, and it is impossible for the Necessary Being (الواجب) to be deficient or less perfect than a human. These proofs together yield certainty, though some points may admit debate. The author then states the Ash'ari school's position: life, hearing, and sight are eternal attributes (صفات قديمة). However, the fact that God has hearing does not imply that the heard objects are eternal, because the relation (تعلق) between the attribute and its object can occur in time. Finally, the author refutes an objection: hearing and sight are not merely a physical temperament (اعتدال المزاج) or the effect of the sense (تأثير الحاسة), nor are they conditioned by physical organs, because such things are not even true in the witnessed world (الشاهد), let alone in the unseen (الغائب).

Key Terms
سميع بصير حي
كتب إلهية
إجماع
علم
قدرة
نقيصة
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do knowledge and power indicate?
A) Life
B) Hearing and sight directly
C) Both life and then hearing/sight
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of life, hearing, and sight are established by multiple converging proofs including scripture, consensus, and rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's life, hearing, and sight are proven by reason and that His perfection demands these attributes, would you live differently knowing that He hears every word and sees every action? What would you change today?
?
The author argues that lacking these attributes would be a deficiency. How does this affect your view of God's majesty? Are you comfortable with a God who is perfectly hearing and seeing, including your innermost thoughts?
?
If the proofs for God's attributes are certain, what is your excuse for not letting that certainty transform your daily life? Will you commit to living with the awareness that God is Living, Hearing, and Seeing in every moment?

The Eternality of God's Attributes of Life, Hearing, and Sight
Al-Sanusi affirms the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal attributes and refutes objections based on physical conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that life, hearing, and sight are eternal attributes, and the refutation of objections regarding their dependence on physical conditions.

Core Idea
God's attributes of life, hearing, and sight are eternal, but their objects are not; they are not conditioned by physical temperament.

In the second part of the investigation, al-Sanusi clarifies the Ash'ari school's position that life, hearing, and sight are eternal attributes of God. He addresses a potential objection: that these attributes might be merely physical phenomena. He argues that such a view is false even in the witnessed world, so how could it apply to the unseen?

Guided Path
1
Affirmation of eternal attributes
Read the passage and note the statement that life, hearing, and sight are eternal attributes according to the Ash'ari school.
The author says 'it is established upon the principle of our school that there are eternal attributes'.
2
Why the objects are not eternal
Understand that the relation (ta'alluq) between an eternal attribute and its object can occur in time, so the heard and seen are not eternal.
This answers a potential objection that eternal attributes require eternal objects.
3
Refutation of physicalist objection
Examine the author's refutation of the view that hearing and sight are just temperament or sensory effect. He argues this is false even in the witnessed world.
The author uses the principle: what is false in the witnessed cannot be true in the unseen.

Source Text

Rational Proofs for God's Life, Hearing, and Sight
Taftazani presents rational proofs for God's life, hearing, and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reason alone can demonstrate that God is Living, Hearing, and Seeing, using the method of sabr wa-taqsīm.

Core Idea
Reason leads to the same conclusion as revelation: God is Living, Hearing, Seeing.

This lesson introduces the rational arguments for three of God's essential attributes. Taftazani begins with what is commonly known from scripture and consensus, then provides logical inferential proofs. We will examine the reasoning step by step, including the powerful method of sabr wa-taqsīm (examination and division).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on consensus and initial inference
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice how Taftazani first cites scripture and consensus, then moves to rational proofs.
The passage uses technical terms like 'صفات قديمة' (eternal attributes) and 'تعلق' (relation).
2
Trace the logical chain from knowledge/power to life to hearing/sight
The author argues that knowledge and power imply life, and life implies the capacity for hearing and sight. Understand why life is necessary for these attributes.
3
Understand the method of sabr wa-taqsīm
Examine how Taftazani uses the method of examination and division: inanimate objects lack hearing/sight, but when they become alive, they acquire the capacity unless defects occur. This shows life is the cause.
This method is a type of inductive reasoning used in kalam.
4
Reflect on why these attributes are eternal yet their objects temporal
The passage clarifies that God's attributes are eternal, but the specific objects of hearing and sight are created in time. This avoids the problem of co-eternity with creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الصفات في حق من يصح اتصافه بها نقيصة وقصور في الكمال لا أقل، وباطل أن يتسم الواجب تعالى بالنقصان أو بكونه أقل كمالاً من الإنسان فهذه بجملتها تفيد القطع، وإن كان في البعض للجدال مجال ويثبت على أصل عالم قادر حي بالضرورة وعلى السمع والبصر بأن كل حي يصح كونه سميعاً بصيراً، وكل ما يصح للواجب من الكمالات يثبت بالفعل لبراءته عن أن يكون له ذلك بالقوة والإمكان، وعلى الكل بأنها صفات كمال قطعاً، والخلو يخلو عنها وعن ضدها، لكن لا بد من بيان أن الحياة في الغائب أيضاً تقتضي صحة السمع والبصر، وغاية متشبسهم) في ذلك على ما ذكره إمام الحرمين طريق السبر والتقسيم. فإن الجماد لا يتصف بقبول السمع والبصر، وإذا صار حياً يتصف به، إن لم يقم به آفات، ثم إذا صيرنا صفات الحي لم نجد ما يصحح قبوله للسمع والبصر سوى كونه حياً، ولزم القضاء بمثل ذلك في حق الباري تعالى. وأوضح من هذا ما أشار إليه الإمام حجة الإسلام. أنه لا خفاء أن المتصف بهذه الصفات أكمل ممن لا يتصف بها، فلو لم يتصف الباري بها، لزم أن يكون الإنسان بل غيره من الحيوانات أكمل منه، وهو باطل قطعاً، ولا يرد عليه النقص بمثل الماشي، والحسن الوجه لأن استحالته في حق الباري تعالى يعلم مما لا علم قطعاً بخلاف السمع والبصر. والغرض من تكثير وجوه الاستدلال في أمثال هذه المقامات زيادة التوثيق والتحقيق، وأن الأذهان متفاوتة في القبول والإذعان ربما يحصل للبعض منها الأدلة السمعية، ولا خفاء في ثبوت الإجماع وقيام الأدلة السمعية القطعية على كونه تعالى حياً سميعاً بصيراً، فأي حاجة إلى سائر المقدمات التي ربما يناقض فيها . . ؟ . فجوابه: المنع إذ ربما يجزم بذلك من لا يلاحظ الإجماع عليه، أو لا يراه حجة أصلاً أو يعتقد أنه لا يصح في مثل هذا المطلوب التمسك به، وسائر الأدلة السمعية
The fifth investigation: Concerning that He is Living, Hearing, Seeing. The divine scriptures have testified to it, and the prophets have agreed upon it, indeed the majority of rational people. And knowledge and power indicate life, and life indicates the soundness of hearing and sight, so they are established by actuality. And there is no concealment that being devoid of these attributes in the case of one whose description with them is valid is a deficiency and a shortcoming in perfection, no less. And it is false that the Necessary (al-Wājib), exalted, should be characterized by deficiency or by being less perfect than a human. So these things collectively yield certainty, even if some of them allow room for argumentation. And it is established, according to the principle of our companions (Ashʿarīs), that there are eternal attributes, which are life, hearing, and sight. And the pre-eternality of what is heard and seen does not follow, due to the permissibility of the occurrence of relation (taʿalluq). And what is said that they are the very moderation of temperament and the effect of the sense organ, or are conditioned by that, is denied in the witnessed (shāhid), so how about the absent (ghāʾib)...? ... It is known by necessity from religion and established in the Book and the Sunnah, such that it cannot be denied nor interpreted away, that the Creator, exalted, is Living, Hearing, Seeing. And the consensus of the people of religions, indeed all rational people, has been concluded upon that. And life is sometimes inferred by that He is Knowing, Powerful, as has passed, and every knowing, powerful being is living by necessity. And hearing and sight are inferred by that every living being is valid to be hearing and seeing. And every perfection that is valid for the Necessary is established in actuality because He is free from having that potentially and possibly. And regarding all of them, they are attributes of perfection certainly, and being devoid of attributes of perfection in the case of one whose description with them is valid is deficiency, and that is impossible for God, exalted, as has passed. And this exposition does not need the explanation that death, deafness, and blindness are opposites of life, hearing, and sight, not the non-existence of faculties, and that one who is valid to be described with an attribute is not devoid of it nor its opposite. However, an explanation is needed that life in the absent also necessitates the soundness of hearing and sight. And the utmost of their clinging in that, according to what Imām al-Ḥaramayn mentioned, is the method of examination and division (sabr wa-taqsīm). For an inanimate object is not characterized with the acceptance of hearing and sight, and when it becomes living it is characterized with it, provided no defects occur to it. Then when we examine the attributes of the living, we do not find what validates its acceptance of hearing and sight except its being living. And the judgment of the like of that in the case of the Creator, exalted, follows. And clearer than this is what Imām Ḥujjat al-Islām (al-Ghazālī) pointed to: that there is no concealment that the one characterized with these attributes is more perfect than the one not characterized with them. So if the Creator were not characterized with them, it would necessarily follow that a human, indeed other animals, would be more perfect than Him, and that is certainly false. And the deficiency like walking and beauty of face does not apply to it, because their impossibility in the case of the Creator, exalted, is known from what is known certainly, unlike hearing and sight. And the purpose of multiplying aspects of inference in such contexts is to increase corroboration and verification, and because minds vary in acceptance and submission; perhaps some of them attain reassurance by some aspects without others, or by the totality of all, or by the lack thereof, along with the fact that each one has room for discussion. As for the objection that there is no way to [prove] the impossibility of deficiency and defect upon the Creator, exalted, except consensus, whose authority is based on scriptural evidences, and there is no concealment that consensus is established and scriptural evidences are certain that He, exalted, is Living, Hearing, Seeing—so what need is there for all the other premises that may be contradicted? Its answer is: denial (manʿ), because perhaps someone is certain of that without observing consensus upon it, or does not consider it a proof at all, or believes that it is not valid to adhere to it in such an objective, nor [valid to adhere to] other scriptural evidences.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive argument for God's attributes of life (ḥayāh), hearing (samʿ), and sight (baṣar). The author begins by stating that these attributes are attested by divine scriptures, agreed upon by prophets and the majority of rational people. He then provides rational inference: knowledge and power imply life, and life implies the soundness of hearing and sight; these attributes are actual (bil-fiʿl) in God, not potential. He emphasizes that lacking these attributes would be a deficiency and imperfection, which is impossible for God. The author notes that according to the Ashʿarī school, these are eternal attributes (ṣifāt qadīma), but their objects (the heard and seen) are not eternal because the relation (taʿalluq) between the attribute and its object can occur in time. He refutes the claim that hearing and sight are merely bodily faculties (like moderation of temperament or sense organ function) by a reasoning: if this were the case in observable beings, how could it apply to the unseen God? The argument then shifts to a rational demonstration: knowledge and power necessitate life, and living necessarily entails the capacity for hearing and sight. The author invokes the method of sibr wa-taqsīm (examination and division) as used by Imām al-Ḥaramayn: inanimate objects cannot perceive, but when they become alive they can perceive unless there are defects; thus life is the sole cause for the capacity to hear and see; by analogy, God's life implies His hearing and seeing. The author then presents a stronger argument from perfection attributed to Imām al-Ghazālī: a being with these attributes is more perfect than one without; if God lacked them, humans or animals would be more perfect than God, which is false. He dismisses the objection that other perfections like walking or a beautiful face are also lacking in God—they are impossible for God by rational necessity, whereas hearing and sight are not impossible. Finally, the author anticipates an objection: why not rely solely on scriptural consensus (ijmāʿ) and scriptural proofs to establish these attributes, without rational arguments? He answers that some people do not accept consensus or scriptural evidence, or they require rational demonstration for conviction; hence multiple proofs are valuable for different minds. The commentary proceeds sequentially, explaining each proof and counterargument as it appears in the source, preserving the author's nuanced theological positions.

صفات قديمة
سبر وتقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's hearing and sight are metaphorical or physically conditioned like human senses.
Correction: The passage argues that these attributes are real, eternal, and not dependent on bodily organs, using the sabr wa-taqsīm method to show that life alone grants the capacity.

Checkpoint

A) Direct scriptural citation
B) The method of examination and division (sabr wa-taqsīm)
C) Consensus of the prophets
D) The argument from perfection

Key Takeaway
Reason and revelation converge: God is Living, Hearing, and Seeing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson shows that reason and revelation both affirm these attributes. Do you ever resist letting rational proofs strengthen your faith, and if so, why?
?

Divine Attributes

The Perfection Argument and the Value of Multiple Proofs
Taftazani presents the perfection argument and defends the need for multiple rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strongest rational proof for God's attributes based on perfection, and why multiple arguments are needed to address different minds.

Core Idea
If God lacked hearing or sight, a human would be more perfect than God, which is impossible.

After establishing the rational inference from life to hearing and sight, Taftazani presents an even more powerful argument: it is a matter of perfection. A being with these attributes is more perfect than one without. Since God is the most perfect, He must possess them. The section also addresses an objection: why go through all this reasoning when we have scriptural evidence? The answer reveals Taftazani's pedagogical wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the perfection argument from al-Ghazali
Focus on the claim that a being with hearing, sight, and life is more perfect than one without. The conclusion is that if God lacked them, humans or animals would be more perfect, which is absurd.
This argument is attributed to Imām al-Ghazālī (Ḥujjat al-Islām).
2
Understand why other perfections like walking do not apply
Taftazani notes that attributes like walking or a beautiful face are impossible for God by rational necessity, but hearing and sight are not. Thus they must be affirmed.
3
Analyze the objection about relying solely on consensus
The objection suggests that since consensus and scripture already prove these attributes, rational arguments are unnecessary. Consider why someone might reject consensus or scripture.
4
Grasp the purpose of multiple arguments
Taftazani explains that different minds are convinced by different proofs, and multiple arguments increase corroboration.
This shows sensitivity to audience.

صار حياً يتصف به، إن لم يقم به آفات، ثم إذا صيرنا صفات الحي لم نجد ما يصحح قبوله للسمع والبصر سوى كونه حياً، ولزم القضاء بمثل ذلك في حق الباري تعالى. وأوضح من هذا ما أشار إليه الإمام حجة الإسلام. أنه لا خفاء أن المتصف بهذه الصفات أكمل ممن لا يتصف بها، فلو لم يتصف الباري بها، لزم أن يكون الإنسان بل غيره من الحيوانات أكمل منه، وهو باطل قطعاً، ولا يرد عليه النقص بمثل الماشي، والحسن الوجه لأن استحالته الاطمئنان ببعض الوجوه دون البعض، أو باجتماع الكل أو عدمه منها مع ما في كل واحد من محال المناقشة، وأما الاعتراض بأنه لا سبيل إلى استحالة النقص والآفة على الباري تعالى سوى الإجماع المستند حجيته إلى The fifth investigation: Concerning that He is Living, Hearing, Seeing. The divine scriptures have testified to it, and the prophets have agreed upon it, indeed the majority of rational people. Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive argument for God's attributes of life (ḥayāh), hearing (samʿ), and sight (baṣar). The author begins by stating that these attributes are attested by divine scriptures, agreed upon by prophets and the majority of rational people. He then provides rational inference: knowledge and power imply life, and life implies the soundness of hearing and sight; these attributes are actual (bil-fiʿl) in God, not potential. He emphasizes that lacking these attributes would be a deficiency and imperfection, which is impossible for God. The author notes that according to the Ashʿarī school, these are eternal attributes (ṣifāt qadīma), but their objects (the heard and seen) are not eternal because the relation (taʿalluq) between the attribute and its object can occur in time. He refutes the claim that hearing and sight are merely bodily faculties (like moderation of temperament or sense organ function) by a reasoning: if this were the case in observable beings, how could it apply to the unseen God? The argument then shifts to a rational demonstration: knowledge and power necessitate life, and living necessarily entails the capacity for hearing and sight. The author invokes the method of sibr wa-taqsīm (examination and division) as used by Imām al-Ḥaramayn: inanimate objects cannot perceive, but when they become alive they can perceive unless there are defects; thus life is the sole cause for the capacity to hear and see; by analogy, God's life implies His hearing and seeing. The author then presents a stronger argument from perfection attributed to Imām al-Ghazālī: a being with these attributes is more perfect than one without; if God lacked them, humans or animals would be more perfect than God, which is false. He dismisses the objection that other perfections like walking or a beautiful face are also lacking in God—they are impossible for God by rational necessity, whereas hearing and sight are not impossible. Finally, the author anticipates an objection: why not rely solely on scriptural consensus (ijmāʿ) and scriptural proofs to establish these attributes, without rational arguments? He answers that some people do not accept consensus or scriptural evidence, or they require rational demonstration for conviction; hence multiple proofs are valuable for different minds. The commentary proceeds sequentially, explaining each proof and counterargument as it appears in the source, preserving the author's nuanced theological positions.

The Proof from Division and Classification for God's Attributes
Taftazani presents the sabr wa taqsim proof for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of sabr wa taqsim (division and classification) proves that God possesses life, hearing, and sight.

Core Idea
Life is the sole ground for hearing and sight; God, being alive, must have them.

Taftazani introduces the first of two proofs for God's life, hearing, and sight. He uses a logical method known as sabr wa taqsim, which involves dividing beings into categories and identifying the common ground for an attribute. This passage lays out the reasoning that life itself necessitates the capacity for hearing and sight, and therefore God, who is necessarily living, must possess these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Note the logical structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the method of sabr wa taqsim
Look for the division into categories and the elimination of alternatives.
3
Understand the division into inanimate/living
The author divides beings into inanimate and living, observing that only the living can have hearing/sight.
This is the 'division' step.
4
Note the conclusion that life is the ground
After elimination, life remains as the only factor.
5
Apply the reasoning to God
Since God is definitely alive, He must have hearing and sight.
This is the 'application' step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن الممات والصمم والعمى أضداد للحياة والسمع والبصر، لا إعدام ملكات وأن من يصح اتصافه بصفة لا يخلو عنها وعن ضدها، لكن لا بد من بيان أن الحياة في الغائب أيضاً تقتضي صحة السمع والبصر، وغاية متشبثهم في ذلك على ما ذكره إمام الحرمين طريق السبر والتقسيم. فإن الجماد لا يتصف بقبول السمع والبصر، وإذا صار حياً يتصف به، إن لم يقم به آفات، ثم إذا صيرنا صفات الحي لم نجد ما يصحح قبوله للسمع والبصر سوى كونه حياً، ولزم القضاء بمثل ذلك في حق الباري تعالى. وأوضح من هذا ما أشار إليه الإمام حجة الإسلام أنه لا خفاء أن المتصف بهذه الصفات أكمل ممن لا يتصف بها، فلو لم يتصف الباري بها، لزم أن يكون الإنسان بل غيره من الحيوانات أكمل منه، وهو باطل قطعاً، ولا يرد عليه النقص بمثل الماشي والحسن الوجه لأن استحالته في حق الباري تعالى يعلم مما لا علم قطعاً بخلاف السمع والبصر. والغرض من تكثير وجوه الاستدلال في أمثال هذه المقامات زيادة التوثيق والتحقيق، وأن الأذهان متفاوتة في القبول والإذعان ربما يحصل للبعض منها الاطمئنان ببعض الوجوه دون البعض، أو باجتماع الكل أو عدمه منها مع ما في كل واحد من مجال المناقشة.
Statement that death, deafness, and blindness are opposites of life, hearing, and sight, not the absence of faculties, and that whoever is validly described by an attribute is not devoid of it and its opposite, but it is necessary to explain that life in the Unseen also necessitates the validity of hearing and sight. The utmost of their [the opponents'] clinging in this, according to what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned, is the method of division and classification. For the inanimate is not described as receiving hearing and sight, and when it becomes living it is described with them, unless defects are attached to it. Then when we examine the attributes of the living, we find nothing that validates its reception of hearing and sight except that it is living. So judgment must be passed likewise with respect to God Most High. Clearer than this is what Imam Hujjat al-Islam indicated: that there is no doubt that the one described with these attributes is more perfect than one not described with them. So if the Creator were not described with them, it would entail that man, nay other animals, would be more perfect than Him, which is absolutely false. And the objection [based on] deficiency like walking and beauty of face does not apply, because their impossibility with respect to God Most High is known with certainty, unlike hearing and sight. The purpose of multiplying the aspects of inference in such places is to increase documentation and verification, and that minds vary in acceptance and conviction; perhaps some of them attain tranquility through some aspects without others, or through the combination of all or their absence, despite every one of them being subject to discussion.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for God's life, hearing, and sight. The first proof uses the method of division (sabr wa taqsim): death, deafness, and blindness are opposites of life, hearing, and sight, not mere absences, so a being must have one or the other. The author argues that life in the Unseen also necessitates the capacity for hearing and sight. By dividing beings into inanimate and living, we see that only the living can have hearing and sight (barring defects), so life is the sole ground. The second proof is from perfection: a being with these attributes is more perfect than one without; God must be most perfect, so He has them. The author deflects a potential counterexample (walking, beauty) by noting their impossibility for God is known, unlike hearing and sight. The passage concludes by justifying multiple arguments: minds vary, and different arguments may convince different people.

Key Terms
السبر والتقسيم
الحياة
السمع
البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death, deafness, blindness are merely the absence of faculties, not opposites.
Correction: The author states they are opposites, meaning a being must have one or the other, not a neutral state.

Checkpoint
What is the sole factor that makes a being capable of receiving hearing and sight according to the sabr wa taqsim proof?
A) Being inanimate
B) Being living
C) Being perfect
D) Being human

Key Takeaway
Life is the necessary and sufficient condition for hearing and sight in all beings; thus God, the necessarily living, must possess them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How often do you use your hearing and sight in ways that displease the One who gave them to you? Commit today to use your senses for obedience, not for what angers Him.
?
If life is the ground for hearing and sight, then Allah's perfect life means He hears and sees everything, including your secret thoughts. What would you be ashamed of if you remembered that Allah hears and sees all your private moments? What one action will you change today because of this awareness?
?
This lesson shows that inanimate objects lack hearing and sight, but you have them because you are alive. How can you make your life a living proof of gratitude by using your hearing and sight to seek knowledge of Allah and serve His creation? Write one concrete step you will take this week.

The Perfection Argument for God's Hearing and Sight
Taftazani presents the perfection argument and the purpose of multiple proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from perfection for God's hearing and sight, and why it is not invalidated by mundane perfections.

Core Idea
God must be more perfect than all beings, so He possesses hearing and sight.

After presenting the division proof, Taftazani offers a clearer argument based on perfection. He argues that a being with hearing and sight is more perfect than one without, so God—who is absolutely perfect—must have them. He also addresses a potential objection about other attributes like walking, explaining that those are impossible for God. Finally, he explains why multiple proofs are useful.

Guided Path
1
Read the perfection argument
Read the passage and identify the key premise: a being with hearing/sight is more perfect.
This argument is attributed to Imam al-Ghazali (Hujjat al-Islam).
2
Understand the consequence
If God lacked these, a human or animal would be more perfect, which is false.
3
Consider the objection about walking/beauty
Someone might argue that we don't attribute walking or beauty to God, so why hearing/sight?
The author anticipates this objection.
4
Understand the refutation
Walking and beauty are impossible for God, but hearing/sight are not, so the objection fails.
5
Reflect on the purpose of multiple proofs
The author explains that different arguments may convince different people, and multiple proofs increase certainty.
This is a methodological note.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن الممات والصمم والعمى أضداد للحياة والسمع والبصر، لا إعدام ملكات وأن من يصح اتصافه بصفة لا يخلو عنها وعن ضدها، لكن لا بد من بيان أن الحياة في الغائب أيضاً تقتضي صحة السمع والبصر، وغاية متشبثهم في ذلك على ما ذكره إمام الحرمين طريق السبر والتقسيم. فإن الجماد لا يتصف بقبول السمع والبصر، وإذا صار حياً يتصف به، إن لم يقم به آفات، ثم إذا صيرنا صفات الحي لم نجد ما يصحح قبوله للسمع والبصر سوى كونه حياً، ولزم القضاء بمثل ذلك في حق الباري تعالى. وأوضح من هذا ما أشار إليه الإمام حجة الإسلام أنه لا خفاء أن المتصف بهذه الصفات أكمل ممن لا يتصف بها، فلو لم يتصف الباري بها، لزم أن يكون الإنسان بل غيره من الحيوانات أكمل منه، وهو باطل قطعاً، ولا يرد عليه النقص بمثل الماشي والحسن الوجه لأن استحالته في حق الباري تعالى يعلم مما لا علم قطعاً بخلاف السمع والبصر. والغرض من تكثير وجوه الاستدلال في أمثال هذه المقامات زيادة التوثيق والتحقيق، وأن الأذهان متفاوتة في القبول والإذعان ربما يحصل للبعض منها الاطمئنان ببعض الوجوه دون البعض، أو باجتماع الكل أو عدمه منها مع ما في كل واحد من مجال المناقشة.
Statement that death, deafness, and blindness are opposites of life, hearing, and sight, not the absence of faculties, and that whoever is validly described by an attribute is not devoid of it and its opposite, but it is necessary to explain that life in the Unseen also necessitates the validity of hearing and sight. The utmost of their [the opponents'] clinging in this, according to what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned, is the method of division and classification. For the inanimate is not described as receiving hearing and sight, and when it becomes living it is described with them, unless defects are attached to it. Then when we examine the attributes of the living, we find nothing that validates its reception of hearing and sight except that it is living. So judgment must be passed likewise with respect to God Most High. Clearer than this is what Imam Hujjat al-Islam indicated: that there is no doubt that the one described with these attributes is more perfect than one not described with them. So if the Creator were not described with them, it would entail that man, nay other animals, would be more perfect than Him, which is absolutely false. And the objection [based on] deficiency like walking and beauty of face does not apply, because their impossibility with respect to God Most High is known with certainty, unlike hearing and sight. The purpose of multiplying the aspects of inference in such places is to increase documentation and verification, and that minds vary in acceptance and conviction; perhaps some of them attain tranquility through some aspects without others, or through the combination of all or their absence, despite every one of them being subject to discussion.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for God's life, hearing, and sight. The first proof uses the method of division (sabr wa taqsim): death, deafness, and blindness are opposites of life, hearing, and sight, not mere absences, so a being must have one or the other. The author argues that life in the Unseen also necessitates the capacity for hearing and sight. By dividing beings into inanimate and living, we see that only the living can have hearing and sight (barring defects), so life is the sole ground. The second proof is from perfection: a being with these attributes is more perfect than one without; God must be most perfect, so He has them. The author deflects a potential counterexample (walking, beauty) by noting their impossibility for God is known, unlike hearing and sight. The passage concludes by justifying multiple arguments: minds vary, and different arguments may convince different people.

Key Terms
الكمال
الإمام حجة الإسلام
التوثيق
التحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The perfection argument fails because we don't attribute walking or beauty to God.
Correction: Those attributes are impossible for God, unlike hearing and sight which are not, so the argument stands.

Checkpoint

B) Their impossibility for God is known with certainty, unlike hearing and sight

D) Animals have walking and beauty

Key Takeaway
God must have hearing and sight because He is the most perfect being, and lacking them would imply imperfection.

The Objection of Eternal Hearing and Sight and Its Answer
Taftazani addresses the objection that eternal hearing implies eternal objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris defend the eternity of divine attributes like hearing and sight by distinguishing between the attribute itself and its temporal relations.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes can have temporal relations without requiring eternal objects.

This passage presents a common objection against the eternity of God's hearing and sight: if they are eternal, then what is heard and seen must also be eternal. Taftazani provides the Ash'ari response, which distinguishes between the eternal attribute and its temporal 'attachments' (ta'alliqat). Understanding this distinction is crucial for defending the traditional view of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector assumes that hearing requires an object of hearing, so an eternal hearing demands an eternal object. Note the logical structure: if hearing is eternal, then what is heard must also be eternal.
2
Study the answer
Taftazani responds by denying the necessary connection. He points to knowledge and power as examples of eternal attributes that have temporal relations. Consider how this analogy works.
3
Examine the counter-objection
Taftazani notes that the opponent might turn this into a dilemma, but the answer remains that temporal relations are possible. Reflect on why this answer is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو كان السمع والبصر قديمين، لزم قدم كون المسموع والمبصر كذلك لامتناع السمع بدون المسموع والإبصار بدون المبصر. قلنا: ممنوع الجواز أن يكون كل منهما صفة قديمة لها تعلقات حادثة كالعلم والقدرة. ويمكن أن يجعل هذا شبهة من قبل المخالف بأنه لو كان فإما أن يكون السمع والبصر قديمين فيلزم قدم المسموع والمبصر أو حادثين فيلزم كونه محلاً للحوادث.
For it is said: If hearing and sight were eternal, then the eternity of the heard and seen would follow, because hearing without what is heard and seeing without what is seen are impossible. We say: The permissibility [of that] is prevented; each of them can be an eternal attribute having temporal relations, like knowledge and power. And this can be made into a doubt from the opponent: that if [hearing and sight] were eternal, then the eternity of the heard and seen would follow, or if they were temporal, then His being a locus of temporal events would follow.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if hearing and sight are eternal (qadim), then their objects must also be eternal, which is absurd. The answer (qulna) denies the necessary connection: each attribute can be eternal while having temporal relations (ta'alliqat haditha), just as knowledge and power relate to temporal things. The author then notes that this can be turned into a dilemma by the opponent: either the attributes are eternal (leading to eternal objects) or temporal (making God a locus of temporal events). However, the initial answer that the attributes have temporal relations remains the standard Ash'ari response.

Key Terms
تعلقات حادثة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal attribute must have an eternal object.
Correction: An eternal attribute can have temporal relations, just as God's knowledge knows temporal things without being temporal itself.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'aris answer the objection that eternal hearing would require eternal objects?
A) They deny eternity of attributes
B) They say attributes have temporal relations
C) They claim objects are also eternal
D) They say hearing is not an attribute

Key Takeaway
The eternity of divine attributes like hearing does not require the eternity of their objects, because attributes can have temporal relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing is eternal and relates to your every word, even the ones you whisper in despair, does this truth make you more careful about what you say or more confident that He listens? Which one are you lacking right now?
?
The objector assumed that eternal hearing requires an eternal object. Do you ever live as if God's attributes are limited by your circumstances, as if He cannot hear you when you feel distant? What would change if you truly believed His hearing is not bound by time?
?
The answer uses the analogy of knowledge and power to show that eternal attributes can have temporal relations. How does this affect your understanding of God's awareness of your daily struggles? Will you act on that awareness today by speaking to Him as if He is listening right now?

Is Sensation Knowledge or a Distinct Attribute?
Taftazani cites two Ash'ari views on sensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Ash'ari views on whether sensation (al-ihsas) is a type of knowledge or a distinct attribute, and the nuance in al-Ash'ari's own position.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari considered sensation a type of knowledge, not a separate attribute.

In this passage, Taftazani presents two positions within the Ash'ari school regarding the nature of sensation (al-ihsas). The first, attributed to the school's founder himself, holds that sensation is simply a type of knowledge (ilm) of the sensed object, meaning no additional attribute beyond knowledge is required. The second, which became the well-known Ash'ari position, treats hearing and sight as distinct attributes separate from knowledge. However, Taftazani notes that this second view is not necessary given al-Ash'ari's own principles. Let's examine the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the passage is fragmentary and continues from a previous page.
The passage ends with 'من' indicating it continues.
2
Identify the two positions
Look for the two views: one from al-Shaykh (al-Ash'ari) and the well-known Ash'ari position. What does each say about sensation?
The first says sensation is knowledge, the second says hearing/sight are distinct attributes.
3
Analyze the implications
Consider why the author says the second view is not necessary according to al-Ash'ari's principle. What does this tell us about the diversity of opinion within the Ash'ari school?
This highlights that not all Ash'aris agreed on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: And according to what is transmitted as the well-known doctrine of the Ash'aris, each of hearing and sight is an attribute distinct from knowledge, except that this is not necessary according to the principle of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan regarding sensation, from
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage cites two positions on the nature of sensation (ihsas). The first, attributed to al-Ash'ari (al-Shaykh), holds that sensation is a type of knowledge (ilm) of the sensed object, so no separate attribute beyond knowledge is needed; different types of knowledge could be merely different relations. The second, the well-known Ash'ari position, treats hearing and sight as attributes distinct from knowledge. However, the author notes that the latter is not necessary according to al-Ash'ari's own principle. The passage is incomplete, breaking off at 'from'.
