Eternal Hearing and Sight: The First Objection
Statement of position and refutation of first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against God's eternal hearing and sight and the Ash'arite answer based on temporal relations.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes can have temporal relations without making their objects eternal.

The opponent argues that if God's hearing and sight are eternal, then what He hears and sees must also be eternal—an absurd implication. How do Ash'arites respond without falling into that trap? This passage presents the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Focus on the opponent's claim: if hearing and sight are eternal, then the heard and seen must be eternal. Understand the assumed impossibility of hearing without a heard object.
This objection relies on the physical analogy where hearing requires something heard.
2
Identify the Ash'arite answer
Notice the key phrase: 'each attribute is eternal but has temporal relations (ta'alluqāt ḥāditha)'. See how the author compares to knowledge and power.
Knowledge can be eternal while its objects are temporal—similar logic applies here.
3
Reflect on the concept of ta'alluqāt ḥāditha
Consider that relations to created things are temporal even if the attribute itself is eternal. This distinguishes the attribute from its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكون إنزال الكتب، وإرسال الرسل فرع كون الباري حياً سميعاً بصيراً. وبالجملة لما ثبت كونه حياً سميعاً بصيراً ثبت على قاعدة أصحابنا. له صفات قديمة : هي الحياة والسمع والبصر على ما بينا في العلم والقدرة . فإن قيل : لو كان السمع والبصر قديمين ، لزم قدم كون المسموع والمبصر كذلك لامتناع السمع بدون المسموع والإبصار بدون المبصر . قلنا : ممنوع الجواز أن يكون كل منهما صفة قديمة لها تعلقات حادثة كالعلم والقدرة، ويمكن أن يجعل هذا شبهة من قبل المخالف بأنه لو كان فإما أن يكون السمع والبصر قديمين فيلزم قدم المسموع والمبصر أو حادثين فيلزم كونه محلاً للحوادث وشبهة أخرى وهي أنه لو كان حياً سميعاً بصيراً لكان جسماً، واللازم باطل. وجه اللزوم أن الحياة اعتدال نوعي للمزاج الحيواني على ما سبق، أو صفة تتبعها مقتضية للحس والحركة الإرادية وقد عرفت أن المزاج من الكيفيات الجسمية، وأن السمع والبصر وسائر الإحساسات تأثر للحواس عن المحسوسات، أو حالة إدراكية تتبعه وليست الحواس الأقوى جسمانية والجواب : أنا لا نسلم كون الحياة والسمع والبصر عبارة عما ذكرتم أو مشروطة به في الشاهد، فضلاً عن الغائب. غاية الأمر أنها في الشاهد تقارن ما ذكرتم ، ولا حجة على الاشتراط ، وقد تكلمنا على ذلك فيما سبق .
Because sending down books and sending messengers is a branch of the Creator being living, hearing, seeing. In sum, when it is established that He is living, hearing, seeing, it is established, on the basis of our colleagues, that He has eternal attributes: life, hearing, and sight, as we explained in [the discussion of] knowledge and power. For if it is said: If hearing and sight were eternal, then the being of the heard and the seen would necessarily be eternal as well, because hearing without the heard and sight without the seen are impossible. We say: This is prevented; it is possible that each of them is an eternal attribute which has temporal relations (ta'alluqāt ḥāditha) like knowledge and power. And this can be made into an objection from the opponent: that either hearing and sight are eternal, necessitating the eternity of the heard and seen, or they are temporal, necessitating that He is a locus of temporal events. And another objection: that if He were living, hearing, seeing, He would be a body, and the consequent is false. The connection is that life is a specific balance of animal temperament as previously, or an attribute that follows from it requiring sense and voluntary motion, and you have known that temperament is among bodily qualities, and that hearing, sight, and all sensations are effects of the senses upon sensed objects, or a perceptual state that follows it, and the senses are not [merely] bodily. The answer: We do not concede that life, hearing, and sight are what you have mentioned or conditioned by that in the witnessed, let alone the unseen. At most, they are concomitant with what you mentioned in the witnessed, but there is no proof for conditionality. We have already spoken about that previously.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Ash'arite doctrine that God has eternal attributes of life, hearing, and sight, and answers them. The author first states the Ash'arite position. Then the first objection: if hearing and sight are eternal, what is heard and seen must also be eternal. The answer: they are attributes with temporal relations, like knowledge and power. The second objection: if God has life, hearing, sight, He would be a body, because in the witnessed world these imply a body. The answer: we do not accept that these attributes in the unseen are defined or conditioned by the witnessed; they merely accompany such conditions in the witnessed world, but that does not prove conditionality. Thus both objections are refuted.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
الصفات القديمة
التعلقات الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has eternal hearing, there must be an eternal object heard.
Correction: The attribute is eternal but its relation (ta'alluq) to the object is temporal; no eternal object is required.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the first objection that eternal hearing requires an eternal heard object?
A) By denying that hearing and sight are eternal.
B) By stating that they have temporal relations (ta'alluqāt ḥāditha).
C) By claiming that heard objects are also eternal.
D) By saying the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Eternal divine attributes do not require eternal objects because the relations (ta'alluqāt) are temporal.

?
You have learned that God's hearing and sight are eternal but His attention to your specific situations is temporal. When you feel that God is not 'hearing' your prayers, how does this truth challenge your impatience? What concrete step will you take this week to trust His timing more deeply?

How does this reshape your view of your past sins? Knowing that God saw them as temporal acts, how can you fully embrace His forgiveness and stop living in guilt?
?
If God's attributes can relate to temporal events without changing His essence, then He is intimately involved in every detail of your life without being diminished. How does this motivate you to be more mindful of His presence in your daily decisions? Write one specific situation today where you will consciously act as if God is hearing and seeing you.

The Second Objection: Attributes Imply Corporeality
Refutation of the second objection that divine attributes imply corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that God's attributes of life, hearing, and sight imply He is a body, and the Ash'arite response based on the distinction between the witnessed and unseen.

Core Idea
Conditions from the witnessed world cannot be imposed on the unseen divine attributes.

In the physical world, life, hearing, and sight are tied to bodies. The opponent argues that if God has these attributes, He must be a body. But the Ash'arites reject this analogy. How do they argue that the link is merely contingent, not necessary?

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's reasoning
Read the objection: life is a balance of temperament, hearing and sight are sensations requiring bodily organs. So if God has these, He must be a body.
This objection assumes that the attributes in the Creator must be identical to those in creatures.
2
Identify the key component of the answer
Notice the phrase 'We do not concede'—the Ash'arite does not accept that the attributes in the witnessed world are conditional. They are merely concomitant.
Concomitance (muqārana) does not imply conditionality (ishtirāṭ).
3
Reflect on the 'witnessed vs unseen' distinction
The answer highlights that what is true in the witnessed (shāhid) does not automatically apply to the unseen (ghā'ib).
This is a key principle in kalam: analogy from the witnessed to the unseen requires proof of shared conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكون إنزال الكتب، وإرسال الرسل فرع كون الباري حياً سميعاً بصيراً. وبالجملة لما ثبت كونه حياً سميعاً بصيراً ثبت على قاعدة أصحابنا. له صفات قديمة : هي الحياة والسمع والبصر على ما بينا في العلم والقدرة . فإن قيل : لو كان السمع والبصر قديمين ، لزم قدم كون المسموع والمبصر كذلك لامتناع السمع بدون المسموع والإبصار بدون المبصر . قلنا : ممنوع الجواز أن يكون كل منهما صفة قديمة لها تعلقات حادثة كالعلم والقدرة، ويمكن أن يجعل هذا شبهة من قبل المخالف بأنه لو كان فإما أن يكون السمع والبصر قديمين فيلزم قدم المسموع والمبصر أو حادثين فيلزم كونه محلاً للحوادث وشبهة أخرى وهي أنه لو كان حياً سميعاً بصيراً لكان جسماً، واللازم باطل. وجه اللزوم أن الحياة اعتدال نوعي للمزاج الحيواني على ما سبق، أو صفة تتبعها مقتضية للحس والحركة الإرادية وقد عرفت أن المزاج من الكيفيات الجسمية، وأن السمع والبصر وسائر الإحساسات تأثر للحواس عن المحسوسات، أو حالة إدراكية تتبعه وليست الحواس الأقوى جسمانية والجواب : أنا لا نسلم كون الحياة والسمع والبصر عبارة عما ذكرتم أو مشروطة به في الشاهد، فضلاً عن الغائب. غاية الأمر أنها في الشاهد تقارن ما ذكرتم ، ولا حجة على الاشتراط ، وقد تكلمنا على ذلك فيما سبق .
Because sending down books and sending messengers is a branch of the Creator being living, hearing, seeing. In sum, when it is established that He is living, hearing, seeing, it is established, on the basis of our colleagues, that He has eternal attributes: life, hearing, and sight, as we explained in [the discussion of] knowledge and power. For if it is said: If hearing and sight were eternal, then the being of the heard and the seen would necessarily be eternal as well, because hearing without the heard and sight without the seen are impossible. We say: This is prevented; it is possible that each of them is an eternal attribute which has temporal relations (ta'alluqāt ḥāditha) like knowledge and power. And this can be made into an objection from the opponent: that either hearing and sight are eternal, necessitating the eternity of the heard and seen, or they are temporal, necessitating that He is a locus of temporal events. And another objection: that if He were living, hearing, seeing, He would be a body, and the consequent is false. The connection is that life is a specific balance of animal temperament as previously, or an attribute that follows from it requiring sense and voluntary motion, and you have known that temperament is among bodily qualities, and that hearing, sight, and all sensations are effects of the senses upon sensed objects, or a perceptual state that follows it, and the senses are not [merely] bodily. The answer: We do not concede that life, hearing, and sight are what you have mentioned or conditioned by that in the witnessed, let alone the unseen. At most, they are concomitant with what you mentioned in the witnessed, but there is no proof for conditionality. We have already spoken about that previously.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Ash'arite doctrine that God has eternal attributes of life, hearing, and sight, and answers them. The author first states the Ash'arite position. Then the first objection: if hearing and sight are eternal, what is heard and seen must also be eternal. The answer: they are attributes with temporal relations, like knowledge and power. The second objection: if God has life, hearing, sight, He would be a body, because in the witnessed world these imply a body. The answer: we do not accept that these attributes in the unseen are defined or conditioned by the witnessed; they merely accompany such conditions in the witnessed world, but that does not prove conditionality. Thus both objections are refuted.

البصر
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has attributes like life, hearing, sight, He must be a body because in humans these imply a body.
Correction: The attributes in the unseen are not conditioned by the same factors; they are only concomitant in the witnessed, which does not prove conditionality.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the author uses to refute the second objection?
A) The attributes are temporal, not eternal.
B) The attributes in the witnessed are merely concomitant, not conditional.
C) The attributes are only metaphorical.
D) God does not have hearing or sight.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes in the unseen are not bound by conditions observed in the physical world; concomitance does not equal conditionality.

The objection assumes that because in our world life requires a body, God's life must too. Where in your own thinking have you assumed that God's ways must fit into your limited experience? What would it cost you to let go of that assumption?
?
If you truly believed that God's attributes are not conditioned by any of the limitations you know, how would your worship change tonight? Would you be more expectant, more reverent, or more honest? Make one concrete decision based on that belief before you sleep.

Hearing and Sight: Knowledge or Distinct Attributes?
Al-Ash'ari's view that sensation is knowledge and the common Ash'ari view, with objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether divine hearing and sight are reducible to knowledge or are distinct attributes, and the rational possibility raised by al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight may be just God's knowledge applied to different objects.

This passage addresses a key point of debate in Ash'ari theology: Are God's hearing and sight distinct from His knowledge? Al-Ash'ari himself suggested that sensation is knowledge, with the differences being merely relational. The later Ash'ari school took them as distinct, but the passage shows that al-Ash'ari's own principle does not force that conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the two contrasting positions presented.
2
Identify the two views
Notice al-Ash'ari's original view (sensation is knowledge) and the well-known Ash'ari position (hearing and sight are distinct attributes).
The passage notes that the common view is not necessitated by al-Ash'ari's principle.
3
Understand the objection and answer
See how the objection that different types of knowledge would be needed is answered by saying one attribute can have different relations.
This answer preserves the unity of God's knowledge while allowing for distinct perceptual experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : على ما نقل عن الشيخ أن الإحساس علم بالمحسوس، وإن كان نوعاً آخر من العلم لا يلزم ثبوت صفة أخرى لجواز أن يكون الأنواع المختلفة هي التعليقات. قال : وعلى ما نقل المشهور من مذهب الأشاعرة أن كلاً من السمع والبصر صفة مغايرة للعلم، إلا أن ذلك ليس بلازم على قاعدة الشيخ أبي الحسن في الإحساس من أنه علم بالمحسوس على ما سبق ذكره الجواز أن يكون مرجعهما إلى صفة العلم، ويكون السمع علماً بالمسموعات، والبصر علماً بالمبصرات. فإن قيل : هذا إنما يتم لو كان الكل نوعاً واحداً من العلم لا أنواعاً مختلفة على ما مر في بحث العلم. قلنا: يجوز أن يكون له صفة واحدة هي العلم، لها تعلقات مختلفة الأنواع المختلفة، بأن تتعلق بالبصر مثلاً تارة بحيث تحصل له حالة إدراكية تناسب تعلقنا إياه، وتارة بحيث تحصل حالة إدراكية تناسب إبصارنا إياه.
He said: According to what is transmitted from the Shaykh (al-Ash'ari), sensation is knowledge of the sensed, even if it is another type of knowledge; it does not necessitate the existence of another attribute because it is possible that the different types are [just] the relations (ta'alluqāt). He said: According to what is transmitted as the well-known position of the Ash'arites, each of hearing and sight is an attribute distinct from knowledge, but that is not necessitated by the principle of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan on sensation — that it is knowledge of the sensed — as mentioned previously; it is possible that their referent is the attribute of knowledge, and hearing is knowledge of audibles, and sight is knowledge of visibles. For if it is said: This would only hold if all were one type of knowledge, not different types, as has passed in the discussion of knowledge. We say: It is possible that He has one attribute which is knowledge, having different relations of different types, such that it relates to sight, for example, sometimes in a way that obtains for it a perceptual state appropriate to our hearing, and sometimes in a way that obtains a perceptual state appropriate to our seeing.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage discusses alternative views on the nature of hearing and sight relative to knowledge. First, it reports from al-Ash'ari that sensation is knowledge of the sensed, just a different type, so no additional attribute is needed; the different types are just different relations. Second, it reports the common Ash'ari view that hearing and sight are attributes distinct from knowledge, but notes that this is not necessitated by al-Ash'ari's own principle, which allows them to be reduced to the single attribute of knowledge. Then an objection: if they are all just knowledge, they would be the same type, but they are different. The answer: one attribute of knowledge can have different relations producing different perceptual states. So the common view is not the only possibility.

الإحساس
علم
السمع
البصر
التعليقات
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari and his school unanimously consider hearing and sight as distinct attributes.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's own view was that they are knowledge, and the common view is only one possibility, not logically necessitated.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the idea that hearing and sight reduce to knowledge?
A) That would make God a body.
B) That would require eternal objects.
C) That would mean all knowledge is of one type, but they are different.
D) That would negate God's life.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's principle allows that divine hearing and sight may be nothing but God's knowledge related to different objects, making the common Ash'ari view of distinct attributes not the only possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would change in your worship if you fully internalized that His hearing and sight are infinite knowledge rather than finite faculties?

Examine your heart: Do you prefer a God with multiple 'parts' because it feels more personal? Or does His simplicity draw you closer?
?
The passage suggests that the well-known Ash'ari view is not the only option—there is room for theological humility. Philosophers' and Mu'tazila View on Attributes
The author reports the view that life is just knowing and able, and hearing/sight are just knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila and philosophers' position that divine attributes like life, hearing, and sight reduce to knowledge and power, and their agreement that no attribute exists beyond the essence.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila and philosophers, divine attributes are not separate realities; they reduce to knowledge and power.

This passage presents the rationalist approach to divine attributes, where the philosophers and many Mu'tazila argue that God's life is simply that He knows and is able, and that hearing and seeing are nothing but His knowledge of what is heard and seen. While there is disagreement among the Mu'tazila on whether hearing/sight are identical to knowledge, all of them agree that no attribute exists as an extra entity beyond the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully; notice the key terms like 'الفلاسفة' and 'المعتزلة'.
The text is dense with theological distinctions.
2
Identify the core view
The philosophers and some Mu'tazila say life is just knowing and able, and hearing/sight are just knowledge. This means no separate attributes.
3
Note the disagreement among Mu'tazila
Al-Ka'bi and a group from Baghdad hold the same reduction, while the majority say hearing/sight are not identical to knowledge.
All still deny extra attributes.
4
Understand the universal agreement
Despite their differences, all these groups agree that no attribute exists as an addition to the essence.
This is a key point of consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعند الفلاسفة وبعض المعتزلة حياته كونه يعلم ويقدر، وسماعه وإبصاره علمه بالمسموعات والمبصرات. قال : وعند الفلاسفة على هذا لا يلزم ثبوت صفة زائدة فضلاً عن تعددها، وإلى هذا ذهب الكعبي وجماعة من معتزلة بغداد والأكثرون على أن كونه سميعاً بصيراً غير كونه عالماً. واتفق كلهم على نفي الصفة الزائدة على الذات.
He said: According to the philosophers and some Mu'tazila, His life is that He knows and is able, and His hearing and seeing is His knowledge of audibles and visibles. He said: According to the philosophers on this, no additional attribute is necessitated, let alone multiplicity. Al-Ka'bi and a group of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad adopted this, while the majority hold that His being hearing and seeing is other than His being knowing. They all agreed on negating the attribute additional to the essence.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the view of philosophers and some Mu'tazila on divine attributes. According to them, God's life is simply that He knows and is able; His hearing and seeing are His knowledge of audibles and visibles. Therefore no additional attribute exists, let alone multiplicity. Al-Ka'bi and a group of Mu'tazila from Baghdad held this. The majority of Mu'tazila, however, maintain that 'hearing' and 'seeing' are not identical to 'knowing', though all agree that no attribute exists over and above the essence.

God's Hearing and Seeing: Reducibility to Knowledge or Distinct Attributes?
Discussion of reducibility of hearing and seeing to knowledge, with objection, answer, and positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether God's attributes of hearing and seeing are reducible to His knowledge or are distinct, and the positions of different schools.

Core Idea
God's hearing and seeing are either identical to His knowledge or distinct but not separate from His essence.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines the nature of God's attributes of hearing and seeing. It begins by presenting the view that these attributes might be reducible to knowledge, then addresses an objection, and concludes by outlining the positions of philosophers, Mu'tazila, and the majority of theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from a possibility statement to an objection and response, then a report of views.
2
Identify the initial claim
Find the sentence that suggests hearing and seeing could be reduced to knowledge. Note how the author frames it as a possibility ('الجواز').
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection says the reduction only works if knowledge is one type. The answer says God's single knowledge can have different relations leading to different perceptual states. Trace the logic.
4
Learn the sectarian positions
The passage lists views: philosophers and some Mu'tazila (including al-Ka'bi) reduce hearing/seeing to knowledge; the majority say they are distinct. Note the final agreement that no attribute is a separate entity.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that Ash'aris deny any distinction between attributes and essence. The passage shows they affirm a cognitive distinction while rejecting existential separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه علم بالمحسوس على ما سبق ذكره الجواز أن يكون مرجعهما إلى صفة العلم، ويكون السمع علماً بالمسموعات، والبصر علماً بالمبصرات.
فإن قيل: هذا إنما يتم لو كان الكل نوعاً واحداً من العلم لا أنواعاً مختلفة على ما مر في بحث العلم. قلنا: يجوز أن يكون له صفة واحدة هي العلم، لها تعلقات مختلفة الأنواع المختلفة، بأن تتعلق بالبصر مثلاً تارة بحيث تحصل له حالة إدراكية تناسب تعلقنا إياه، وتارة بحيث تحصل حالة إدراكية تناسب إبصارنا إياه.
قال: وعند الفلاسفة وبعض المعتزلة حياته كونه يعلم ويقدر، وسماعه وإبصاره علمه بالمسموعات والمبصرات.
قال: وعند الفلاسفة على هذا لا يلزم ثبوت صفة زائدة فضلاً عن تعددها، وإلى هذا ذهب الكعبي وجماعة من معتزلة بغداد والأكثرون على أن كونه سميعاً بصيراً غير كونه عالماً. واتفق كلهم على نفي الصفة الزائدة على الذات.
That it is knowledge of the perceptible according to what was previously mentioned, the possibility that their reference is to the attribute of knowledge, such that hearing is knowledge of audible things, and sight is knowledge of visible things. If it is said: This is only valid if the whole were one type of knowledge, not different types as was passed in the discussion of knowledge. We say: It is possible that He has one attribute which is knowledge, having different relations of different types, such that it relates to sight, for example, sometimes in a way that a perceptual state occurs in it that corresponds to our seeing it, and sometimes in a way that a perceptual state occurs that corresponds to our sight of it. He said: According to the philosophers and some of the Mu'tazila, His life is His being knowing and powerful, and His hearing and seeing are His knowledge of audible and visible things. He said: According to the philosophers, on this basis, no additional attribute is necessitated, let alone multiplicity, and this view was held by al-Ka'bi and a group of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad, while the majority hold that His being hearing and seeing is other than His being knowing. And they all agreed on negating the additional attribute beyond the essence.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of whether God's attributes of hearing (sam') and seeing (baṣar) are reducible to the attribute of knowledge ('ilm) or are distinct attributes. It begins by stating the possibility that hearing and seeing are merely knowledge of audible and visible things, respectively. An objection is then raised: this reduction would only be valid if all knowledge were a single type, but knowledge is diverse (as discussed earlier). The answer is that God's single attribute of knowledge can have different relations (ta'alluqāt) to objects, resulting in distinct perceptual states—e.g., a relation that yields a state appropriate for hearing or for seeing. The author then reports the views: the philosophers and some Mu'tazila (including al-Ka'bi) hold that hearing and seeing are not additional attributes but identical to knowledge; the majority (al-aktharūn) hold that 'being hearing and seeing' is other than 'being knowing' (ghayr kawnihi 'āliman). Nevertheless, all parties agree on negating any attribute that is 'additional to the essence' (ṣifa zā'ida 'alā al-dhāt), meaning attributes are not separate entities but real yet non-subsistent distinctions. The passage thus clarifies the Ash'ari position: attributes are distinct in meaning but not existentially separate.

Key Terms
علم
سمع
بصر
صفة زائدة
تعلق
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny any distinction between attributes and essence, making them identical.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm a cognitive distinction (attributes have different meanings) but reject existential separation (they are not separate entities).

Checkpoint
What is the view of the majority (al-aktharūn) regarding God's hearing and seeing?
A) They are the same as His knowledge.
B) They are different from His knowledge.
C) They are identical to His essence.
D) They are additional attributes separate from His essence.

Key Takeaway
According to the majority of theologians, God's attributes of hearing and seeing are distinct from His knowledge in meaning, but no attribute is a separate entity from His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing is not just His knowledge but a distinct attribute, does that make you more aware that He truly listens to your words? How does this shape what you say and how you say it in prayer?

?

God's Perception of Sensible Qualities: Judgment vs. Terminology
Conclusion on divine perception of smells/tastes/touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on divine perception of sensible qualities: that God truly perceives smells, tastes, temperatures, and textures, but the physical terms 'smeller', 'taster', 'toucher' are not applied to Him due to linguistic and transcendental considerations.

Core Idea
God perceives all sensible qualities, but avoids physical terms to preserve His transcendence.

This conclusion from the text addresses a delicate point in divine attributes: if God knows all things, does He 'smell' or 'taste'? The Ash'ari tradition affirms that God indeed perceives these qualities, but the common human terms imply physical organs or contacts unbecoming of God. Imam al-Haramayn explains why we must affirm the judgments of these perceptions while avoiding the physical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the distinction between 'perception' (idrāk) and the physical terms (shāmm, dhā'iq, lāmis).
The text has two parts: a summary and then Imam al-Haramayn's elaboration.
2
Identify the argument
Trace the reasoning from Imam al-Haramayn: what obligates describing God with the judgments of hearing and sight also applies to other perceptions.
3
Apply the principle
Consider the example given: 'I smelled it but did not perceive its smell.' How does this illustrate the gap between linguistic expressions and real perception?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة. المذهب أنه تعالى يدرك الروايح والطعوم ومثل الحرارة والبرودة إلا أن الشرع لم يرد بذلك ولم يجوز العقل كونه شاماً ذائقاً لامساً لكونها من صفات الأجسام مع أنها لا تنبىء عن حقيقة الإدراك لصحة قولنا شممته فلم أدرك ريحه.
عندنا وجوب
قال: خاتمة. قال إمام الحرمين رحمه الله الصحيح المقطوع به وصف الباري تعالى بأحكام الإدراكات الأخر. أعني الإدراك المتعلق بالطعوم والمتعلق بالروائح، والمتعلق بالحرارة والبرودة واللين والخشونة، إذ كل ادراك يعقبه ضد هو آفة فما دل على وجوب وصفه بحكم السمع والبصر، دل على وجوب وصفه بأحكام الإدراكات، ثم يتقدس الباري تعالى عن كونه شاماً ذائقاً لامساً، فإن هذه الصفات تنبىء عن اتصالات يتعالى الرب عنها، مع أنها لا تنبىء عن حقائق الإدراكات. فإنك تقول: شممت تفاحة فلم أدرك ريحها، وكذلك الذوق واللمس.
He said: Conclusion. The doctrine is that He, exalted, perceives smells and tastes and such as heat and cold, except that the Law has not come with that, and reason does not permit that He be a smeller, taster, toucher because they are among the attributes of bodies, while they do not indicate the reality of perception, due to the validity of our saying: I smelled it but did not perceive its smell. According to us, obligation. He said: Conclusion. Imam al-Haramayn (may God have mercy on him) said: The correct and definitive position is that the description of God, exalted, with the judgments of the other perceptions, i.e., perception related to tastes, perception related to smells, and perception related to heat, cold, softness, and hardness, for every perception is followed by an opposite which is a deficiency; so whatever indicates the obligation of describing Him with the judgment of hearing and sight indicates the obligation of describing Him with the judgments of perceptions. Then God, exalted, is sanctified from being a smeller, taster, toucher, for these attributes indicate contacts from which the Lord is exalted, while they do not indicate the realities of perceptions. For you say: I smelled an apple but did not perceive its smell, and likewise tasting and touching.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion (khātima) concerning whether God perceives smells, tastes, temperature, texture, etc. The author states the Ash'ari doctrine: God indeed perceives these things (yudrik al-rawā'ih wa al-ṭu'ūm), but the Sacred Law does not apply the terms 'shāmm' (smeller), 'dhā'iq' (taster), or 'lāmis' (toucher) to God, and reason does not permit it. The reason is that these terms are among the attributes of bodies and do not necessarily indicate the reality of perception—for example, one may say 'I smelled it but did not perceive its smell,' showing that the term 'smelling' does not entail actual perception. The phrase 'عندنا وجوب' likely means 'with us it is obligatory' to affirm the judgments (aḥkām) of these perceptions. Imam al-Haramayn (Juwaynī) elaborates: the correct view is to describe God with the 'judgments' of these other perceptions, meaning the cognitive and existential implications of perception (i.e., that God knows these sensible qualities). The same reasoning that obligates describing God with the judgment of hearing and sight obligates describing Him with the judgments of these other perceptions. However, God is sanctified (yutaqaddas) from being described with the physical terms themselves, because those terms imply physical contact (ittiṣālāt) that transcendence negates, and they do not convey the true reality of perception (ḥaqā'iq al-idrākāt). The example of smelling an apple without perceiving its smell illustrates that the verbal expression does not capture the essence of perception.

Key Terms

شم
ذوق
لمس
حكم
أحكام الإدراكات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that God is a 'smeller' or 'taster' implies God has no knowledge of smells or tastes.
Correction: The passage explains that the physical terms do not capture the reality of perception; God truly perceives these qualities, but the terms are avoided because they suggest physical contacts unbecoming of God.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, why should God be described with the judgments of other perceptions (like taste and smell)?
A) Because the Sacred Law explicitly mentions them.
B) Because the same reasoning that obligates describing Him with the judgment of hearing and sight also applies.
C) Because these are considered greater perfections than hearing and sight.
D) Because the philosophers argued for it.

Key Takeaway
God perceives all sensible qualities, but the physical terms like 'smeller' are not applicable to Him because they imply physical contacts and do not capture the true reality of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God perceives every smell you smell, every taste you taste, every texture you touch. Do you live with the awareness that your most intimate physical experiences are known to Him? What would need to change in your daily life to make this awareness real?
?
The passage distinguishes between affirming God's perception and using human terms. Have you ever pictured God in a human way, even slightly? Are you willing to actively purify your mental image of God, even if it means unlearning comfortable habits of thought?
?
If God's knowledge of your sensations is so complete, yet so transcendent, what does that demand from you in terms of gratitude or accountability? How does this lesson change how you might relate to your own sensory experiences today?

God's Perception of Smells, Tastes, and Touch
Al-Juwayni argues for God's perception of smells, tastes, and touch while denying physical senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God can be described with the judgments of smell, taste, and touch without implying any physical attributes or imperfections.

Core Idea
God possesses all perfect perceptions, but no physical acts.

Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni presents a theological argument that extends the logic used for hearing and sight to other perceptions like smell, taste, and touch. He emphasizes that while God must be attributed with the effects of these perceptions, He is absolutely free from the physical actions and organs associated with them. This distinction safeguards divine transcendence without compromising divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic text and its literal English translation to get the full context of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: 'أحكام الإدراكات' (judgments of perceptions) and the verbs 'شام', 'ذائق', 'لامس'.
2
Identify the two main claims
Notice that the passage first asserts that God must be described with the judgments of smell, taste, and touch. Then it immediately exalts Him from being called a smeller, taster, or toucher.
These two claims work together to affirm perfection while negating physicality.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
The reasoning is that just as the absence of hearing and sight would be a defect (since their contraries are defects), the same applies to the other perceptions. Therefore, God must possess their positive judgments.
This is an argument from perfection: every perception has a defective opposite, so God must have the perfect form.
4
Grasp the distinction through the example
The author gives an example: 'I smelled an apple but did not perceive its scent.' This shows that the act of smelling does not guarantee actual perception, so the 'judgment' of perception can exist without the physical act.
This illustrates that God can have the judgment of perception without being a physical 'smeller'.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why the terms 'smeller', 'taster', and 'toucher' are inappropriate for God: they imply connections, organs, and physical changes. The judgment of perception is the real perfection, not the bodily process.
This preserves tanzih (transcendence) while affirming kamal (perfection).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال إمام الحرمين رحمه الله الصحيح المقطوع به وصف الباري تعالى بأحكام الإدراكات الأخر. أعني الإدراك المتعلق بالطعوم والمتعلق بالروائح، والمتعلق بالحرارة والبرودة واللين والخشونة، إذ كل إدراك يعقبه ضد هو آفة فما دل على وجوب وصفه بحكم السمع والبصر، دل على وجوب وصفه بأحكام الإدراكات، ثم يتقدس الباري تعالى عن كونه شاماً ذائقاً لامساً، فإن هذه الصفات تنبىء عن اتصالات يتعالى الرب عنها، مع أنها لا تنبىء عن حقائق الإدراكات. فإنك تقول: شممت تفاحة فلم أدرك ريحها، وكذلك الذوق واللمس.
Imam al-Haramayn (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The correct and definitive (view) is describing Allah Most High with the judgments (ahkam) of the other perceptions—meaning perception related to tastes, perception related to smells, and perception related to heat, cold, softness, and hardness. For every perception is followed by a contrary which is a defect. So what indicates the obligation of describing Him with the judgment of hearing and sight indicates the obligation of describing Him with the judgments of the perceptions. Then Allah Most High is exalted from being a smeller, a taster, a toucher, for these attributes indicate connections from which the Lord is exalted, while they do not indicate the realities of perceptions. For you say: I smelled an apple but did not perceive its scent, and likewise for taste and touch.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing that God possesses the judgments (ahkam) of the perceptions of smell, taste, and touch, while being utterly transcendent (tanazzuh) from having physical sensory organs or undergoing the physical processes that accompany these perceptions. The author, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, begins by stating the definitive position: that God must be described with the 'judgments of the other perceptions'—i.e., the effects or states that result from sensing smells, tastes, and tactile qualities such as heat, cold, softness, and hardness. He then provides the logical basis: just as God is necessarily described with the judgment of hearing and sight (since hearing and sight are perfections and their opposite, deafness and blindness, are defects), the same reasoning applies to these other perceptions. Every perception has a contrary which is a defect; therefore, God must possess the positive judgment of each perception. However, He is absolutely exalted (yutaqaddas) from being described as a 'smeller' (shāmm), 'taster' (dhā'iq), or 'toucher' (lāmis), because these terms imply physical contact, connection, and bodily instruments—attributes unbefitting the Creator. To illustrate that the judgment of perception can exist without the physical act, the author gives the example: you can say 'I smelled an apple but did not perceive its scent,' meaning the act of smelling does not guarantee actual perception; similarly, the 'judgment' of perception can be attributed to God without implying the corporeal act.

Key Terms

شام
ذائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing the judgment of smell, taste, or touch to God implies He has physical sensory organs or undergoes physical changes.
Correction: The passage clearly separates the 'judgment of perception' (the cognitive effect) from the physical act. God has the judgment without the physical process.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why must God be described with the judgments of smell, taste, and touch?
A) Because physical senses are attributed to Him in the Quran.
B) Because the same reasoning that applies to hearing and sight applies to these perceptions.
C) Because prophets explicitly stated that God smells and tastes.
D) Because it is rationally impossible for God to lack any perception.

Key Takeaway
God possesses the judgments of all perceptions (smell, taste, touch) just as He possesses hearing and sight, but He is absolutely transcendent beyond any physical acts or organs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge of your innermost feelings and sensations, do you unconsciously imagine a human-like 'awareness' that relies on a brain or nerves? What does this lesson reveal about that assumption, and how will you actively replace it with a more transcendent understanding?
?
Al-Juwayni argues that every perception has a contrary defect. Reflect on a time you attributed a limitation to Allah (even subtly)—perhaps wondering if He 'feels' your pain or 'smells' your deeds. Confess that thought, and write down one concrete resolution to remind yourself of His absolute perfection.
?
If Allah perceives all smells, tastes, and touch without any physical connection, how does that reshape your sense of His nearness and intimacy? Are you comfortable with a Lord who knows every detail of your physical experience yet is utterly unlike His creation? What will you change today in your worship to acknowledge this truth?

Proofs That Allah is a Speaker
Al-Taftazani enumerates two proofs for divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main proofs (tawatur and perfection argument) that establish Allah as Mutakallim (the Speaker).

Core Idea
God's speech is proven by prophetic consensus and the perfection argument.

The passage begins by establishing that God is described as 'the Speaker' based on the continuous reports of the prophets, confirmed by miracles, without needing direct speech. It also mentions a rational argument from perfection: silence in a living being is a defect, so the Perfect God must possess speech. This lesson unpacks these two foundational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
Read the Arabic passage once and note the two distinct arguments presented. The first is based on tawatur (mass transmission) and the second on rationality.
The phrase 'على ما قد مر' indicates a reference to a previous discussion.
2
Understand the tawatur proof
Understand why the tawatur of prophetic statements about Allah's speech, combined with their truthfulness confirmed by miracles, suffices as a proof without requiring direct revelation of speech.
3
Analyze the perfection argument
Consider why silence is considered a deficiency in a living being, and how this leads to the conclusion that Allah must be a speaker to be perfect.
This argument is part of a broader principle discussed earlier.
4
Reflect on the proofs together
These two proofs together establish the attribute of speech for Allah from both transmitted and rational angles.
Both proofs are foundational for the Ash'ari school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس في أنه متكلم تواتر القول بذلك عن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام مع ثبوت صدقهم بالمعجزات من غير توقف على الكلام وقد يستدل بأن ضده في الحي نقص أو قصور في الكمال على ما قد مر ثم كلامه عندنا صفة أزلية منافية للسكوت والآفة يدل عليها بالعبارة أو الكتابة ليست من جنس الأصوات والحروف، وخالفنا في ذلك جميع الفرق ذهاباً إلى أن المعقول من الكلام هو الحي دون النفي ولم يقل بقدمه إلا الحنابلة والحشوية جهلاً منهم أو عناداً إذ لا خفاء في ترتب أجزائه وامتناع بقائه وزعم الكرامية أنه مع حدوثه قائم بذات الله تعالى وسموه قوله وجعلوا كلامه عبارة عن القدرة على إيجاد القول وعند المعتزلة هو حادث في جسم ما، ومعنى تكلم الباري تعالى به خلقه فيه.
The sixth investigation: concerning that He is a speaker (mutakallim). The statement of that has been transmitted continuously (tawatur) from the Prophets, peace be upon them, along with the confirmation of their truthfulness by miracles, without depending on speech. And it may be argued that its opposite in a living being is deficiency or imperfection in perfection, as has passed. Then His speech, in our view, is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) incompatible with silence and defect; it is indicated by expression (ibarah) or writing (kitabah), not being of the genus of sounds and letters. All sects have opposed us in this, holding that the rational (ma'qul) of speech is the living being (al-hayy) without negation. And none said its pre-eternity (qidam) except the Hanabila and Hashwiyya, out of ignorance or obstinacy, since there is no hiddenness in the sequence of its parts and the impossibility of its remaining. And the Karamiyya claimed that it is, while originated (hadith), subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High, and they called it His statement (qawl). And they made His speech an expression of the ability to produce speech. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is originated in some body, and the meaning of Allah Most High speaking is His creating it in that body.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a proof and a classification of theological positions regarding the divine attribute of speech (kalam). The author begins with the proof: the fact that God is 'speaking' (mutakallim) is known through the tawatur (massively transmitted reports) of the prophets, whose truthfulness is confirmed by miracles—so this belief does not require direct revelation of speech. Another proof is from the principle of perfection: silence (the opposite of speech) in a living being is a defect, so God must possess speech. The author then defines the Ash'ari position: God's speech is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) that is incompatible with silence and defect. It is not composed of sounds or letters; rather, it is indicated (yadullu 'alayha) by expressions (ibarah) or writing (kitabah). This distinguishes the eternal meaning (ma'na) of speech from the created words that express it. Next, the author surveys the opposing sects: all other groups reject the Ash'ari definition. He specifies that only the Hanabila and Hashwiyya affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of speech, but they are ignorant or obstinate because speech has sequential parts (ḥurūf wa aṣwāt) which cannot be eternal. The Karamiyya hold that speech is originated (hadith) but subsists in God's essence, and they also identify it with the power to create speech. The Mu'tazila hold that speech is an accident created in some body (e.g., the speech of the tree or the angel), and God's 'speaking' means He creates that speech in another locus. The author implicitly refutes all these views by upholding the Ash'ari position that speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not composed of temporal elements.

Key Terms
متكلم
تواتر
المعجزات
نقص
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of divine speech requires direct audibility of God's speech.
Correction: The author argues that the tawatur of prophetic reports, confirmed by miracles, is sufficient without direct audibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first proof that Allah is a speaker?
A) Rational argument from perfection
B) Mass transmission from prophets confirmed by miracles
C) Direct revelation of speech
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Allah is proven to be a speaker through both transmitted and rational proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that silence is a deficiency in a perfect being, how does that challenge your own reluctance to speak the truth in situations where you remain quiet out of fear?
?
The prophets' testimony about Allah's speech is confirmed by miracles—does your daily life reflect that same level of certainty in the Qur'an as Allah's speech, or do you act as if it were merely human words?
?
Given that Allah's attribute of speech is proven both by transmitted reports and rational necessity, what excuse do you have for neglecting the Qur'an, which is the very speech of Allah?

The Ash'ari Definition of Divine Speech
Al-Taftazani defines the Ash'ari view of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of divine speech as an eternal attribute indicated by expression or writing, not composed of sounds and letters.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute that is expressed through created words but is not itself composed of sounds.

After establishing that Allah is a speaker, al-Taftazani defines what divine speech is according to the Ash'ari school. He states it is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) that is incompatible with silence or defect, and it is indicated by expression (ibarah) or writing (kitabah), but it is not of the genus of sounds and letters. This lesson clarifies the crucial distinction between the eternal attribute of speech and its temporal expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note key terms
Read the Arabic carefully, focusing on terms like 'صفة أزلية', 'منافية للسكوت', 'عبارة', 'كتابة', and 'ليست من جنس الأصوات والحروف'.
These terms define the Ash'ari position.
2
Understand the three characteristics
Identify that the attribute is (1) eternal, (2) incompatible with silence and defect, and (3) indicated by expression or writing.
3
Recognize what it is not
Emphasize that it is not composed of sounds and letters, which distinguishes it from created speech.
This is a key refutation of those who anthropomorphize divine speech.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that the Quran as the eternal speech of Allah refers to the meaning (ma'na), while the Arabic words are created expressions.
This is a core Ash'ari doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Refutation of Opposing Sects on Divine Speech
Al-Taftazani enumerates and refutes opposing sects' views on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Hanabila/Hashwiyya, Karamiyya, and Mu'tazila on divine speech and why the Ash'ari school rejects them.

Core Idea
Only the Ash'ari school correctly identifies divine speech as an eternal attribute; other sects err by either taking it as created, or as composed of temporal parts.

Al-Taftazani now addresses the divergent views of other sects. He states that all other groups oppose the Ash'ari position. He specifies that only the Hanabila and Hashwiyya affirm the pre-eternity of speech but they are ignorant because speech has sequential parts which cannot be eternal. The Karamiyya claim speech is originated but subsists in God's essence. The Mu'tazila claim it is an accident created in a body. This lesson surveys these positions and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the three sects
Read the Arabic and identify the three groups mentioned: Hanabila/Hashwiyya, Karamiyya, Mu'tazila.
The phrase 'جميع الفرق' indicates opposition from all other groups.
2
Understand the Hanabila/Hashwiyya error
They affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of speech, but this is refuted because speech has sequential parts which cannot be eternal.
The author calls their view 'ignorance or obstinacy'.
3
Understand the Karamiyya error
They claim speech is originated (hadith) but subsists in Allah's essence, and they define it as the power to produce speech.
They call it 'qawl' (statement).
4
Understand the Mu'tazila error
They say speech is an accident created in some body, and Allah's speaking is His creating speech in that body.
5
Note the author's dismissive tone
The author uses strong language ('جهلاً أو عناداً') to dismiss the Hanabila/Hashwiyya, implying the refutation is obvious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس في أنه متكلم تواتر القول بذلك عن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام مع ثبوت صدقهم بالمعجزات من غير توقف على الكلام وقد يستدل بأن ضده في الحي نقص أو قصور في الكمال على ما قد مر ثم كلامه عندنا صفة أزلية منافية للسكوت والآفة يدل عليها بالعبارة أو الكتابة ليست من جنس الأصوات والحروف، وخالفنا في ذلك جميع الفرق ذهاباً إلى أن المعقول من الكلام هو الحي دون النفي ولم يقل بقدمه إلا الحنابلة والحشوية جهلاً منهم أو عناداً إذ لا خفاء في ترتب أجزائه وامتناع بقائه وزعم الكرامية أنه مع حدوثه قائم بذات الله تعالى وسموه قوله وجعلوا كلامه عبارة عن القدرة على إيجاد القول وعند المعتزلة هو حادث في جسم ما، ومعنى تكلم الباري تعالى به خلقه فيه.
The sixth investigation: concerning that He is a speaker (mutakallim). The statement of that has been transmitted continuously (tawatur) from the Prophets, peace be upon them, along with the confirmation of their truthfulness by miracles, without depending on speech. And it may be argued that its opposite in a living being is deficiency or imperfection in perfection, as has passed. Then His speech, in our view, is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) incompatible with silence and defect; it is indicated by expression (ibarah) or writing (kitabah), not being of the genus of sounds and letters. All sects have opposed us in this, holding that the rational (ma'qul) of speech is the living being (al-hayy) without negation. And none said its pre-eternity (qidam) except the Hanabila and Hashwiyya, out of ignorance or obstinacy, since there is no hiddenness in the sequence of its parts and the impossibility of its remaining. And the Karamiyya claimed that it is, while originated (hadith), subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High, and they called it His statement (qawl). And they made His speech an expression of the ability to produce speech. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is originated in some body, and the meaning of Allah Most High speaking is His creating it in that body.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a proof and a classification of theological positions regarding the divine attribute of speech (kalam). The author begins with the proof: the fact that God is 'speaking' (mutakallim) is known through the tawatur (massively transmitted reports) of the prophets, whose truthfulness is confirmed by miracles—so this belief does not require direct revelation of speech. Another proof is from the principle of perfection: silence (the opposite of speech) in a living being is a defect, so God must possess speech. The author then defines the Ash'ari position: God's speech is an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) that is incompatible with silence and defect. It is not composed of sounds or letters; rather, it is indicated (yadullu 'alayha) by expressions (ibarah) or writing (kitabah). This distinguishes the eternal meaning (ma'na) of speech from the created words that express it. Next, the author surveys the opposing sects: all other groups reject the Ash'ari definition. He specifies that only the Hanabila and Hashwiyya affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of speech, but they are ignorant or obstinate because speech has sequential parts (ḥurūf wa aṣwāt) which cannot be eternal. The Karamiyya hold that speech is originated (hadith) but subsists in God's essence, and they also identify it with the power to create speech. The Mu'tazila hold that speech is an accident created in some body (e.g., the speech of the tree or the angel), and God's 'speaking' means He creates that speech in another locus. The author implicitly refutes all these views by upholding the Ash'ari position that speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not composed of temporal elements.

Correction: Karamiyya say speech is originated but subsists in God's essence, while Mu'tazila say it is an accident created in a body.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view of an eternal, non-composite attribute of speech is defended by refuting the alternative positions of the Hanabila/Hashwiyya, Karamiyya, and Mu'tazila.

Two Proofs that God is Mutakallim: Prophetic Consensus and Reason
Two proofs for divine speech with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two proofs that God is mutakallim (speaking) — from prophetic consensus (tawātur) and from rational argument — and how the author answers the objection that silence is not necessarily a deficiency.

Core Idea
God's speech is proven by prophets and reason; the speaker is more perfect than the silent.

This passage from al-Sanusi presents the sixth topic: that God is mutakallim. He offers two distinct proofs: first, the unanimous reports of the prophets, verified by miracles; second, a rational argument based on the perfection of speech. He then addresses an objection concerning silence and power to speak. By the end, it becomes clear that divine speech is universally affirmed among religious traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the two distinct proofs and the objection.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate the proof from prophetic consensus (تواتر) and the rational proof (دليل عقلي). See how they are structured.
The first relies on tawatur and miracles; the second on analogy with hearing and sight.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Examine the objection that silence might not be a deficiency if one has the power to speak. Understand the author's response that the speaker is still more perfect.
The answer mirrors that given for hearing and sight.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the final sentence: there is no disagreement among religious groups that God is mutakallim.
This emphasizes the universal consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس : في أنه متكلم ، تواتر القول بذلك عن الأنبياء ، وقد ثبت صدقهم بدلالة المعجزات من غير توقف على إخبار الله تعالى عن صدقهم بطريق التكلم ليلزم الدور، وقد يستدل على ذلك بدليل عقلي على قياس ما مرّ في السمع والبصر، وهو أن عدم التكلم ممن يصح اتصافه بالكلام أعني الحي العالم القادر نقص، واتصاف بأضداد الكلام، وهو على الله تعالى محال . وإن نوقش في كونه نقصاً، سيما إذا كان مع قدرة على الكلام ، كما في السكوت فلا خفاء في أن المتكلم أكمل من غيره. ويمتنع أن يكون المخلوق أكمل من الخالق والاعتراض. والجواب : هنا كما مرّ في السمع والبصر وبالجملة لا خلاف لأرباب الملل والمذاهب في كون الباري تعالى متكلماً.
The sixth topic: concerning that He is mutakallim (speaking). The statement of that has been recurrently reported (tawātara) from the prophets, and their truthfulness has been established by the indication of miracles without depending on God, exalted be He, informing of their truthfulness by way of speech, lest circularity occur. And one may infer that by a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) analogously to what has preceded in [the topics of] hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar), which is that the absence of speech from one whom it is correct to describe with speech—I mean the living, knowing, powerful being—is a deficiency (nuqṣ), and his being described with the opposites of speech is impossible (muḥāl) for God, exalted be He. And if it is disputed that it is a deficiency, especially if it is accompanied by the power to speak, as in silence, then it is not hidden that the speaker is more perfect (akmal) than the non-speaker. And it is impossible that the creature be more perfect than the Creator; that is an objection. And the answer is here as has preceded in [the topics of] hearing and sight. In summary, there is no disagreement among the adherents of religions and schools concerning that the Creator, exalted be He, is mutakallim.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a proof passage containing two arguments: a proof from prophetic consensus and a rational proof, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by restating the sixth topic: that God is mutakallim. The first proof is the prophetic consensus (tawātur): the prophets unanimously reported this, and their truthfulness is established by miracles (muʿjizāt) without needing God's own speech to confirm them, thus avoiding circular reasoning (al-dawr). The second proof is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) similar to that used for God's attributes of hearing and sight. It runs as follows: the absence of speech (ʿadam al-takallum) from a being who is living (ḥayy), knowing (ʿālim), and powerful (qādir) is a deficiency (nuqṣ), and being described with opposites of speech is impossible for God. If one objects that silence is not a deficiency when one has the power to speak, the author responds that the speaker (mutakallim) is still more perfect than the non-speaker, and it is impossible for a creature to be more perfect than the Creator. The answer is the same as given previously for hearing and sight. The passage concludes that there is no disagreement among religious groups that God is mutakallim.

Key Terms

دليل عقلي
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from prophecy is circular because it relies on God's speech to confirm the prophets.
Correction: The author clarifies that the prophets' truthfulness is established by miracles, not by God's speech, thus no circularity.

Checkpoint

C) Because everyone already agrees God speaks.
D) Because the prophets are infallible by consensus.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is established by both prophetic consensus and rational necessity, and any objection based on the perfection of silence is answered by the greater perfection of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage argues that a speaker is more perfect than a non-speaker, especially when one has the power to speak. In your life, when you have the power to speak truth against injustice or to give advice, do you choose silence? What does your silence say about your pursuit of perfection?
?
The prophets' consensus that God speaks is verified by miracles. Do you recognize the 'miracles' of divine speech in your own life? Or do you demand more signs while ignoring the clear words of the Quran? How will you respond today to God's speech?

The Eternal Nature of God's Speech and Refutation of Anthropomorphism
Explains that God's speech is an eternal attribute expressed in scriptures, and refutes those who literalize it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq, God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, expressed in scriptures, and why the views of those who literalize it as sounds are untenable.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of sounds or letters.

In this passage, the author clarifies the true meaning of God's speech, distinguishing between the eternal attribute and its temporal expressions in scripture. He presents the orthodox Sunni position and then refutes the anthropomorphic views of the Hanabila and Hashwiyya who attribute literal sounds to God. Understanding this distinction is crucial to preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Notice the key terms like 'كلام نفسي' and how the author contrasts different views.
The passage begins by stating the disagreement is about the meaning and pre-eternity of God's speech.
2
Identify the Ahl al-Ḥaqq position
Find the description of God's speech as an eternal attribute not of sounds or letters, and how it is indicated by expression, writing, gesture.
The author uses the analogy of referring to God in different languages to show the expressions differ but the referent is the same.
3
Understand the opposing views
The passage mentions that all sects oppose this, claiming speech is only audible letters. Then the Hanabila and Hashwiyya go further, saying those letters and sounds are pre-eternal.
The passage ends with an example of their absurdity: some claim the book's binding is pre-eternal.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for tawhid. If God's speech were made of sounds, it would be temporal and contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما الخلاف في معنى كلامه، وفي قدمه وحدوثه ، فعند أهل الحق كلامه ليس من جنس الأصوات والحروف. بل صفة أزلية قائمة بذات الله تعالى منافيه للسكوت ، والآفة كما في الخرس والطفولة هو بها آمر ناه مخبر وغير ذلك، يدل عليها بالعبارة أو الكتابة أو الإشارة فإذا عبر عنها بالعربية ،فقرآن وبالسريانية ،فإنجيل، وبالعبرانية فتوراة والاختلاف على العبارات دون المسمى كما إذا ذكر الله تعالى بألسنة متعددة، ولغات مختلفة. وخالفنا في ذلك جميع الفرق. وزعموا أنه لا معنى للكلام إلا المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة الدال على المعاني المقصودة، وأن الكلام النفسي غير معقول. ثم قالت الحنابلة والحشوية: إن تلك الأصوات والحروف مع تواليها وترتب بعضها على البعض ، وكون الحرف الثاني من كل كلمة مسبوقاً بالحرف المتقدم عليه كانت ثابتة في الأزل، قائمة بذات الباري تعالى وتقدس، وأن المسموع من أصوات القراء والمرئي من أسطر الكتاب نفس كلام الله تعالى القديم، وكفى شاهداً على جهلهم ما نقل عن بعضهم أن الجلدة والغلاف أزليان، وعن بعضهم أن الجسم
And the disagreement is only concerning the meaning of His speech, and concerning its pre-eternity (qidam) and temporality (ḥudūth). For according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq (People of Truth), His speech is not of the genus of sounds and letters. Rather, it is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) subsisting in the essence of God, exalted be He, incompatible with silence and defect, as in muteness (kharas) and childhood (ṭufūla). By it He is commanding (āmir), forbidding (nāhin), informing (mukhbiʾ), and other than that. It is indicated by expression (ʿibāra), writing (kitāba), or gesture (ishāra). So if it is expressed in Arabic, it is the Qurʾān; in Syriac, it is the Injīl (Gospel); in Hebrew, it is the Tawrāt (Torah). The variation is in the expressions (ʿibārāt), not in the named thing (musammā), just as if God, exalted be He, is mentioned in multiple tongues and different languages. And in that we are opposed by all the sects. They claimed that there is no meaning of speech except that which is composed of audible letters indicating intended meanings, and that internal speech (kalām nafsī) is not rationally conceivable. Then the Ḥanābila and Ḥashwiyya said: that those sounds and letters, with their sequence, arrangement of some after others, and the fact that the second letter of every word is preceded by the preceding letter, were established in pre-eternity (azal), subsisting in the essence of the Creator, exalted and sanctified be He, and that what is heard from the voices of reciters and what is seen of the lines of the book is the very same pre-eternal speech of God, exalted be He. And sufficient as a witness to their ignorance is what is related from some of them that the binding and the cover are pre-eternal, and from some of them that the body...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation and refutation passage. The author states that the real disagreement among sects is about the meaning and nature (pre-eternity vs. temporality) of God's speech. He then presents the Ahl al-Ḥaqq position: God's speech is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) subsisting in His essence, incompatible with silence and defects like muteness and childhood. Through this attribute, God commands, forbids, informs, etc. This attribute is indicated (yadullu ʿalayhā) by expression, writing, or gesture. When expressed in Arabic, it is called the Qurʾān; in Syriac, the Injīl (Gospel); in Hebrew, the Tawrāt (Torah). The differences are only in the expressions, not in the underlying meaning (musammā), similar to how God is referred to in different languages. The author then notes that all other sects oppose this view. They claim that speech is only the orderly arrangement of audible letters indicating meanings, and reject the notion of internal speech (kalām nafsī) as unintelligible. Then he specifically refutes the Ḥanābila and Ḥashwiyya, who assert that those very sounds and letters, despite their sequence and dependency, are pre-eternal (thābita fī al-azal) and subsisting in God's essence, and that the actual sounds heard from reciters and the visible script in the book are identical to God's pre-eternal speech. The author concludes with an example of their absurdity: some of them claimed that the book's binding (jild) and cover (ghilāf) are pre-eternal, and some claimed that the body [is also pre-eternal]—the sentence is cut off but the argument is clear.

Correction: The passage clarifies that God's speech is an eternal attribute, not sounds or letters. The Quran is an expression of that attribute in Arabic, but the eternal speech itself is not composed of sounds.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not temporal sounds; scriptures are expressions of that attribute, not the attribute itself.

The Karramiyya Distinction Between Qawl and Kalam
Reports the Karramiyya distinction between qawl and kalam in response to a problematic view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya view that God's saying (qawl) is temporal and subsists in His essence, while His speech (kalam) is His eternal power to speak.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya split God's speech into a temporal saying and an eternal power.

This passage presents the Karramiyya's response to a problem: if the recited Qur'an is eternal, how do we account for its temporal letters? They resolve it by distinguishing between God's saying (qawl), which is temporal and inheres in His essence, and His speech (kalam), which is His eternal attribute of power to speak. This allows them to affirm both eternal divine speech and the temporal nature of the recited text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the initial problem
Start by reading the Arabic and translation. Notice the first sentence describes a view that the written Qur'an became eternal after being created. This is the problem the Karramiyya address.
2
Understand the Karramiyya's reasoning
The Karramiyya choose a 'lesser evil': contradicting a proof is less severe than denying what is self-evident (the temporality of sounds). Grasp this principle.
3
Grasp the distinction between qawl and kalam
They say the composed letters are temporal and subsist in God's essence, calling them 'qawl' (saying) but not 'kalam' (speech). God's kalam is His eternal power to speak, not a sequence of sounds.
Note that qawl is said to be 'temporal without a creator' (hādith lā muḥdith).
4
Examine the rule for temporal vs. created things
The passage ends with a distinction: things with a beginning, if they subsist in the essence, are temporal by power (not created). If separate, they are created by God's word 'Be'.
This rule reinforces their view that the temporal qawl is not created by a separate act but arises from the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي كتب به القرآن فانتظم حروفاً ورقوماً هو بعينه كلام الله تعالى، وقد صار قديماً، بعدما كان حادثاً، ولما رأت الكرامية أن بعض الشر أهون من البعض، وأن مخالفة الضرورة أشنع من مخالفة الدليل ذهبوا إلى أن المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة حدوثه قائم بذات الله تعالى، وأنه قول الله تعالى إلا كلامه، وإنما كلامه قدرته على التكلم وهو قديم، وقوله حادث لا محدث وفرقوا بينهما، بأن كل ما له ابتداء إن كان قائماً بالذات فهو حادث بالقدرة غير محدث، وإن كان مبايناً للذات فهو محدث بقوله كن لا بالقدرة
That by which the Qur'an was written, it arranged letters and script, is the very speech of God, and it became eternal after it was created. And when the Karramiyya saw that some evil is lighter than another, and that contradicting what is self-evident is more repugnant than contradicting a proof, they went to the view that the composed [letters] of the heard sounds, its temporality subsists in God's essence, and that it is God's saying (qawl) not His speech (kalam), but His speech is His power to speak and it is eternal, and His saying is temporal without a creator, and they distinguished between them, by [saying] that everything that has a beginning, if it subsists in the essence, then it is temporal by power (qudra) not created, and if it is separate from the essence, then it is created by His word 'Be' not by power.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting a problematic view that the written Qur'an (the composed letters) is the very speech of God and that it became eternal after being created. The author then reports the Karramiyya's response. The Karramiyya, recognizing that some evil is less severe than another, and that going against what is self-evident (like the temporality of sounds) is worse than going against a proof, proposed a distinction. They said that the composed letters heard in recitation are created and subsist in God's essence—meaning they inhere in God—and they are God's saying (qawl) but not His speech (kalam). For them, God's speech (kalam) is His eternal attribute of power to speak (qudra 'ala al-takallum). His saying (qawl) is temporal but has no creator (lā muḥdith), meaning it is not created by a separate act but rather arises from the essence. They further distinguish: anything that has a beginning, if it subsists in the essence (like an attribute), then it is temporal by power (qudra) but not created (lā muḥdith). If it is separate from the essence (i.e., external), then it is created by the word 'Be' (qawluhu kun), not by power.

Key Terms
الكرامية
القول
الكلام
القدرة على التكلم
حادث لا محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya believe the Qur'an is created in the usual sense (like other creatures).
Correction: They hold the recited letters are temporal (hādith) but subsist in God's essence and are 'without a creator' (lā muḥdith), meaning they are not created by an external act but arise from the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya, what is God's eternal speech (kalam)?
A) The recited Qur'an
B) His power to speak
C) The written text
D) The word 'Be'

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya resolve the tension between eternal divine speech and temporal recited letters by distinguishing qawl (temporal inhering attribute) from kalam (eternal power to speak).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Karramiyya chose a 'lesser evil' to avoid a greater one. Is there an area in your own life where you have knowingly settled for a lesser wrong rather than facing the full truth of God's commands? What would it cost you to embrace that truth today, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
They distinguished between qawl and kalam to preserve God's transcendence and avoid attributing temporality to His essence. In what subtle ways might you be limiting God's majesty—perhaps by reducing His attributes to fit your intellectual comfort, or by avoiding hard questions about His nature? What step can you take this week to humbly submit to His revelation even when it challenges your logic?
?
The passage states that contradicting what is self-evident is more repugnant than contradicting a proof. Have you ever ignored an obvious truth about your own sinfulness or need for repentance because it was more convenient than engaging with a doctrinal argument that would demand change? What self-evident truth about your relationship with God have you been avoiding, and how will you respond to it today?
