The Objection and Answer on the Nature of the Quran
Taftazani presents the objection and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the objection that the Quran must be created because its properties are those of temporal speech.

Core Idea
The Quran is called Allah's speech by common usage or metaphor, not literal identity with His eternal attribute.

This lesson presents a key objection to the Ash'ari doctrine of the uncreated Quran and the classical response. The objector argues that the Quran we recite is clearly created, but Taftazani answers that the name 'Quran' is applied to the created speech by common usage or famous metaphor, while the eternal speech remains an attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the objector presents two proofs and Taftazani gives a single concise answer.
2
Identify the two proofs of the objector
The objector gives two reasons why the Quran must be the created composed speech. Find them in the text and understand their logic.
The first proof is based on universal knowledge; the second on the properties of the Quran.
3
Understand the answer: common usage or metaphor
Taftazani answers that there is no dispute in calling the created composition 'Quran' by way of common usage (ishtirak) or famous metaphor (majaz mashhur). This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The answer does not deny that the Quran is created; it explains how it can still be called Allah's speech.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this distinction preserves both the eternity of Allah's attribute of speech and the temporality of the recited Quran.
This is a key move in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التنزيل ويقولون في أنفسهم وإذا ثبت أن الباري تعالى متكلم أنه ممتنع عليه قيام الكلام الحسي بذاته، تعين أن يكون هو النفس، ويكون قديماً لما مر. قوله: تمسكوا بوجوه: الأول: أنه علم بالضرورة من دين النبي الله حتى العوام والصبيان أن القرآن هو هذا الكلام المؤلف المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة المفتتح بالتحميد المختتم بالاستعاذة وعليها انعقد إجماع السلف وأكثر الخلف. الثاني: أن ما اشتهر وثبت بالنص والإجماع من خواص القرآن، إنما يصدق على هذا المؤلف الحادث لا المعنى القديم. وجوابهما أنه لا نزاع في إطلاق اسم القرآن وكلام الله تعالى بطريق الاشتراك أو المجاز المشهور شهرة الحقائق على هذا المؤلف الحادث وهو المتعارف عند العامة والقراء والأصوليين والفقهاء وإليه ترجع الخواص التي هي من الحروف وسمات الحدوث. قوله: صفات
قال: وذلك: صفات القرآن الكريم أن ما اشتهر من خواص القرآن إنما يصدق على اللفظ الحادث دون المعنى القديم، مثل كونه ذكراً عربياً منزلاً على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مقروءاً بالألسن مسموعاً بالآذان، مكتوباً في المصاحف مقروناً بالتحدي مفصلاً إلى السور والآيات قابلاً للنسخ واقعاً عقيب إرادة التكوين. قلنا كلامه تعالى ما بالاشتراك والمجاز المشهور على النظم المخصوص لا لمجرد أنه دال على كلامه القديم. وذلك إشارة إلى ما اشتهر من الخواص فالقرآن ذكر لقوله تعالى: (وهذا ذكر مبارك) وقوله (وإنه لذكر لك ولقومك) والذكر محدث لقوله تعالى: (وما يأتيهم من ذكر من الرحمن محدث) (وما يأتيهم من ذكر من ربهم محدث). وعربي لقوله تعالى: إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً. والعربي اللفظ لاشتراك اللغات في المعنى، ومنزل على النبي بشهادة النص والإجماع، ولا خفاء في امتناع نزول المعنى القديم القائم بذات الله تعالى بخلاف اللفظ، فإنه وإن كان عرضاً، لا يزول عن محله، لكن قد ينزل نزول الجسم
And in the Revelation: 'And they say within themselves' (Quran 58:8). And when it is established that al-Bari (the Creator), exalted is He, is speaking (mutakallim) and that it is impossible for sensory speech (al-kalam al-hissi) to subsist in His essence, it is determined that it is the speech of the soul (al-nafs) and it is eternal (qadim) as previously mentioned. He said: They argued with aspects: First: It is known by necessity from the religion of the Prophet of Allah—even the common people and children—that the Quran is this speech composed and arranged from audible letters, opening with praise and concluding with seeking refuge, and upon this the consensus of the salaf and most of the khalaf was established. Second: That what is famous and established by text (nass) and consensus (ijma') of the special properties (khawass) of the Quran applies only to this composed created (hadith) thing, not to the eternal meaning (al-ma'na al-qadim). And the answer to both is: There is no dispute in applying the name Quran and the speech of Allah, exalted is He, by way of common usage (ishtirak) or famous metaphor (majaz mashhur) famous like truths (haqa'iq), to this composed created thing, and it is the customary usage among the common people, reciters, usuliyyun, and fuqaha', and to it refer the special properties which are of letters and marks of temporality (huduth). He said: Attributes. He said: That is: the attributes of the Noble Quran—that what is famous of the special properties of the Quran applies only to the created utterance (lafz hadith) without the eternal meaning, such as its being a reminder (dhikr), Arabic, sent down (munazzal) upon the Prophet (peace be upon him), recited by tongues, heard by ears, written in the mushafs, coupled with challenge (tahaddi), detailed into surahs and verses, capable of abrogation (naskh), occurring after the will of creation (irada al-takwin). We said: His speech—may He be exalted—is by common usage and famous metaphor upon the specific composition (al-nazm al-makhsus), not merely because it indicates His eternal speech. And that is an indication to what is famous of the special properties: the Quran is a reminder (dhikr) as in His statement: 'And this is a blessed reminder' (Quran 21:50) and His statement: 'And indeed it is a reminder for you and your people' (Quran 43:44); and the reminder is created (muhdath) as in His statement: 'And there does not come to them a reminder from the Most Merciful that is new' (Quran 26:5) and 'And there does not come to them a reminder from their Lord that is new' (Quran 21:2). And it is Arabic as in His statement: 'Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Quran' (Quran 43:3); and the Arabic is the utterance because languages share meaning. And it is sent down upon the Prophet by witness of text and consensus; there is no concealment in the impossibility of the descent of the eternal meaning subsisting in the essence of Allah, exalted is He, unlike the utterance—for even though it is an accident (arad), it does not leave its locus (mahall), but it may descend like the descent of a body.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the nature of the Qur'an as the speech of Allah. The passage begins by recapitulating the earlier conclusion: since it is impossible for sensory speech (composed of letters and sounds) to subsist in Allah's essence, His speech must be an eternal meaning subsisting in His soul (kalam nafsi), not a temporal utterance. Then the objector ('qala: tamasaku') hangs on two proofs. First, he claims it is known by necessity from the religion of the Prophet—even to the common people and children—that the Qur'an is exactly this composed speech of letters, opening with praise (al-hamd) and ending with seeking refuge (al-isti'ada), and that the consensus of the early and later scholars supports this. Second, he argues that the famous properties of the Qur'an—such as being a reminder, Arabic, sent down, recited, etc.—apply only to this composed, created thing (al-mu'allaf al-hadith), not to an eternal meaning. The author then gives the answer to both proofs: there is no dispute in applying the name 'Qur'an' and 'speech of Allah' to this composed, created speech by way of common usage (ishtirak) or a famous metaphor (majaz mashhur) that is as well-known as literal truths. This usage is customary among the common people, Qur'an reciters, legal theorists (usuliyyun), and jurists (fuqaha'), and it is to this created speech that the famous properties—which are attributes of letters and signs of temporality—refer. The objector then says 'Attributes' (sifat), and the author responds with 'That is: the attributes of the Noble Qur'an...' explaining that the famous properties indeed apply only to the created utterance (lafz hadith), not the eternal meaning. He enumerates these properties: being a reminder (dhikr), being Arabic, being sent down, recited by tongues, heard by ears, written in mushafs, coupled with challenge (tahaddi), detailed into surahs and verses, subject to abrogation (naskh), and occurring after the will of creation. Then the author reiterates the answer: Allah's speech is called by common usage and famous metaphor upon the specific composition (al-nazm al-makhsus), not merely because it indicates His eternal speech. He then elaborates on the property of being a reminder by citing Quranic verses, showing that the reminder is described as 'muhdath' (created/new), which would not apply to an eternal meaning. He also cites the verse 'Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Quran' to argue that 'Arabic' refers to the utterance, because languages share meaning; it is the utterance that is Arabic, not the meaning. Finally, he argues that the Quran is described as 'sent down' (munazzal), and it is impossible for the eternal meaning subsisting in Allah's essence to descend, because that meaning is not subject to spatial movement. As for the utterance, even though it is an accident (arad) that does not leave its locus, it may still descend like the descent of a body. The passage thus defends the Ash'ari position that the eternal divine speech is an attribute of the essence, while the recited Qur'an is its temporal expression, and there is no contradiction in calling both 'the speech of Allah' by way of common usage or famous metaphor.

Correction: The physical Quran is a created expression; it is called 'speech of Allah' by common usage or metaphor, not literal identity with the eternal attribute.

The Properties of the Qur'an Belong to the Created Wording
Al-Taftazani states that properties apply to created wording and proves three of them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the famous properties of the Qur'an — such as being a reminder, Arabic, sent down, recited, written, challenged, divided into chapters, abrogable, and occurring after the divine will — apply only to the created wording (al-lafẓ al-ḥādith) and not to the eternal meaning (al-maʿnā al-qadīm), and you will see the proofs for three of these properties.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's temporal features belong to its created form, not its eternal essence.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a critical distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between the eternal divine speech and its temporal verbal expression. The author, al-Taftazani, asserts that the commonly known attributes of the Qur'an — such as being a reminder, Arabic, and sent down — are true only of the created words, not of the divine attribute of speech. He then provides scriptural and rational proofs to establish this claim. This lesson will walk you through his argument and help you grasp the reasoning behind this foundational doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the main thesis
Read the opening sentence carefully. Notice that the author lists a set of properties and then declares they apply to the created wording, not the eternal meaning.
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Examine the definition of 'Qur'an'
The author clarifies that the term 'Qur'an' is used by common usage and metaphor for the specific composition, not just because it indicates eternal speech.
This avoids confusion about the term's application.
3
Analyze the proof for 'dhikr'
The author cites verses showing the Qur'an is called a 'reminder' (dhikr) and that reminders are created (muhdath).
This proves dhikr applies to the created wording.
4
Analyze the proof for 'Arabic'
The author argues that 'Arabic' refers to the wording because languages differ in words but share meanings.
Thus Arabic cannot be an attribute of the eternal meaning.
5
Analyze the proof for being 'sent down'
The author distinguishes between the eternal meaning (impossible to descend) and the wording (can descend as an accident, and narrates the traditional account of the Quran's descent).
This shows nuzul applies only to the created form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وذلك : صفات القرآن الكريم أن ما اشتهر من خواص القرآن إنما يصدق على اللفظ الحادث دون المعنى القديم، مثل كونه ذكراً عربياً منزلاً على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مقروءاً بالألسن مسموعاً بالآذان، مكتوباً في المصاحف مقروناً بالتحدي مفصلاً إلى السور والآيات قابلاً للنسخ واقعاً عقيب إرادة التكوين . قلنا كلامه تعالى ما بالاشتراك والمجاز المشهور على النظم المخصوص لا لمجرد أنه دال على كلامه القديم وذلك إشارة إلى ما اشتهر من الخواص فالقرآن ذكر لقوله تعالى : {وهذا ذكر مبارك} وقوله { وإنه لذكر لك ولقومك} والذكر محدث لقوله تعالى: {وَمَا يَأْتِيهِمْ مِنْ ذِكْرِ مِنَ الرَّحْمَنِ مُحْدَثِ} {وَمَا يَأْتِيهِمْ مِنْ ذِكْرِ مِنْ رَبِّهِم مُحْدَث} . وعربي لقوله تعالى : {إنا جعلناه قرآنا عربياً} . والعربي اللفظ لاشتراك اللغات في المعنى، ومنزل على النبي بشهادة النص والإجماع، ولا خفاء في امتناع نزول المعنى القديم القائم بذات الله تعالى بخلاف اللفظ، فإنه وإن كان عرضاً، لا يزول عن محله، لكن قد ينزل نزول الجسم الحاصل له وقد روي أن الله تعالى أنزل القرآن دفعة إلى سماء الدنيا فحفظته الحفظة، أو كتبته الكتبة، ثم منزل منها بلسان جبريل على النبي عليه شيئاً فشيئاً بحسب المصالح.
He said: That is: the attributes of the Noble Qur'an: that what is famous of the special properties of the Qur'an applies only to the created wording (al-lafẓ al-ḥādith) and not to the eternal meaning (al-maʿnā al-qadīm), such as it being a reminder (dhikr), Arabic, sent down upon the Prophet, recited by tongues, heard by ears, written in the musḥafs, accompanied by challenge, divided into sūras and verses, subject to abrogation, occurring after the will of bringing into existence. We said: the speech of Allah Most High is by common usage and well-known metaphor (al-istirāk wa al-majāz al-mashhūr) with respect to the specific composition (al-naẓm al-makhṣūṣ), not merely because it indicates His eternal speech. And that is an indication to what is famous of the special properties: so the Qur'an is a reminder (dhikr) due to His saying: 'And this is a blessed reminder' and His saying: 'And indeed it is a reminder for you and your people', and the reminder is created due to His saying: 'And no reminder comes to them from the Most Merciful but it is created' and 'No reminder comes to them from their Lord but it is created'. And it is Arabic due to His saying: 'Indeed We have made it an Arabic Qur'an'. And the Arabic is the wording because languages share in the meaning. And it is sent down upon the Prophet by the testimony of the text and consensus. And there is no concealment of the impossibility of the descent of the eternal meaning subsisting in the essence of Allah Most High, unlike the wording, for even if it is an accident, it does not depart from its locus, but it may descend with the descent of a body. And it has been narrated that Allah Most High sent down the Qur'an all at once to the heaven of the world, so the guardian angels preserved it, or the scribes wrote it, then it was sent down from it by the tongue of Gabriel upon the Prophet gradually according to interests.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage defines the attributes of the Qur'an and argues that the famous properties (like being a reminder, Arabic, sent down, recited, written, challenged, divided into chapters, subject to abrogation, occurring after will) apply to the created wording (al-lafẓ al-ḥādith) and not to the eternal meaning (al-maʿnā al-qadīm) which is the divine attribute of speech. The author then explains that the term 'Qur'an' is used by common usage and metaphor for the specific composition, not because it is merely indicative of the eternal speech. He proves each property: the Qur'an is called a 'reminder' (dhikr) and cites verses showing that reminder is created ('muhdath'); it is Arabic, and he argues that 'Arabic' refers to the wording since languages differ in words but share meanings; it was sent down upon the Prophet, which cannot apply to the eternal attribute because that is inseparable from God; the descent is explained narratively as a literal sending down to the heaven of the world. The passage thus establishes a clear distinction between the temporal verbal expression and the eternal divine speech.

Key Terms

معنى قديم
اشتراك
مجاز
نظم مخصوص
ذكر
عربي
منزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Qur'an in its entirety, including its eternal meaning, is subject to temporal properties like descent and abrogation.
Correction: The author clarifies that all temporal properties (such as descent, abrogation, being Arabic, etc.) apply only to the created wording, not to the eternal attribute of divine speech.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following properties applies to the eternal meaning of the Qur'an?
A) Being written in musḥafs
B) Being a reminder
C) Being Arabic
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The famous properties of the Qur'an are all attributes of its created verbal form, not of the eternal divine speech.

The Objection of Concurrency and Its Answer
An objection that the eternal meaning would be concurrent with the challenge, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer to a key objection preserves the createdness of the Qur'an's wording.

Core Idea
The challenge applies to the created wording, not to the eternal meaning.

In this passage, the author faces an apparent problem: if the Qur'an's eternal meaning is everlasting, it would have been present at the time of the challenge, so the challenge cannot be a property of the created. The answer clarifies that the challenge—calling for imitation—is directed at the Arabic composition, not at the eternal attribute itself. Understanding this answer is crucial for defending the createdness of the recited Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the objector's assumption that challenge applies to the eternal meaning. Consider why that would be problematic for the doctrine of created speech.
The objector uses 'dā'im' to mean everlasting.
2
Examine the author's answer
The author distinguishes between the eternal attribute and the act of calling for opposition. Understand that the challenge is an action related to the created expression.
The phrase 'wa dhālika lā yutāṣawwar' is key.
3
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on how this answer preserves the unique properties of the created wording without affecting the eternity of the divine speech.
This is a classic Ashʿarī response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : القديم دائم فيكون مقارناً للتحدي ضرورة، فلا يكون ذلك من خواص الحادث. قلنا معناه : أن يدعو العرب إلى المعارضة والإتيان بالمثل وذلك لا يتصور، وذلك في الصفة القديمة.
For if it is said: the eternal is everlasting, so it is necessarily concurrent with the challenge, so that is not among the properties of the created. We said: Its meaning is that He calls the Arabs to opposition and bringing the like, and that is inconceivable, and that is in the eternal attribute.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if the Qur'an's eternal meaning (al-qadīm) is everlasting, it would have been present at the time of the challenge (al-taḥaddī), so the challenge cannot be a property unique to the created wording. The author responds that the challenge means God calls the Arabs to produce something similar (al-mithl), and this calling is inconceivable with respect to the eternal attribute itself (as it is not subject to imitation). The challenge is directed at the composed Arabic wording, which is created. Thus, the challenge is indeed a property of the created wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the challenge (taḥaddī) applies to the eternal meaning of the Qur'an, making the eternal meaning subject to imitation.
Correction: The challenge is directed at the created Arabic wording, not at the eternal attribute. The eternal meaning is not subject to imitation because it is beyond human capacity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The challenge of the Qur'an is a property of the created Arabic composition, not of the eternal divine attribute.

Abrogation and the Created vs. Eternal
Objection and answer regarding abrogation's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that abrogation applies to both wording and meaning is answered by distinguishing the created from the eternal.

Core Idea
Abrogation only affects the created, not the eternal.

This lesson examines a third objection against the argument that the Qur'an's abrogation proves its createdness. The objector claims that abrogation can affect both wording and meaning, so it cannot distinguish the created from the eternal. The answer clarifies that abrogation is specific to the created because the eternal does not change or cease.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: 'فإن قيل' (if it is said) introduces the objection, followed by 'قلنا' (we said) for the answer.
The objection concedes that abrogation can apply to meaning, not just wording.
2
Identify the key distinction
The answer accepts the premise but adds a crucial qualification: abrogation is specific to the created (الحادث), not the eternal (القديم). Understand why the eternal cannot be subject to abrogation.
The eternal does not cease (لا يرتفع) nor come to an end (لا ينتهي).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answer defends the Ash'ari position that the Qur'an as an attribute is eternal, while the revealed words and rulings are created. Abrogation pertains only to the latter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : النسخ كما يكون للفظ يكون للمعنى . قلنا : نعم لكن يخص الحادث. لأن القديم لا يرتفع ولا ينتهي .
For if it is said: abrogation just as it applies to the wording also applies to the meaning. We said: Yes, but it is specific to the created, because the eternal does not cease nor come to an end.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This is a third objection-and-answer. The objector claims that abrogation (al-naskh) applies to both wording and meaning, so it cannot be used to differentiate the created from the eternal. The author concedes that abrogation can apply to both, but immediately qualifies: it is specific to the created (al-ḥādith) because the eternal (al-qadīm) does not cease (lā yartaﬁʿ) nor come to an end (lā yantahī). Abrogation involves lifting or replacing a ruling, which implies temporality and change, incompatible with the eternal attribute. Thus, abrogation of a verse's wording or legal ruling pertains only to the created aspect of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
نسخ
لفظ
معنى
حادث
قديم
ارتفاع
انتهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think any abrogation (e.g., of a ruling) affects the eternal meaning.
Correction: The author shows abrogation only affects the created expressions or rulings, not the eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is abrogation specific to the created?
A) Because the eternal meaning is not written in musḥafs
B) Because the eternal does not cease or come to an end
C) Because abrogation only applies to Arabic words
D) Because abrogation was revealed after the Prophet's time

Key Takeaway
Abrogation pertains only to the created aspects of the Qur'an, not to the eternal divine attribute of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author distinguishes between the created and eternal to protect the divinity of Allah's attribute. In your own life, are you conflating temporary created things with what is eternal in your relationship with Allah? What specific step will you take today to prioritize the eternal over the created?

The Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Kun'
Argument that 'Kun' does not imply temporality of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine command 'Kun' cannot be taken literally and how it supports the pre-eternity of Allah's speech.

Core Idea
The command 'Kun' is a metaphor, not a temporal word.

The verse 'When He decrees a thing, He only says to it "Be" and it is' might seem to imply a temporal command. However, taking it literally leads to an infinite regress. In this passage, the author explains why 'Kun' must be understood metaphorically, and what it truly means in relation to Allah's creative power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the provided Arabic text carefully. Notice the opening phrase from the hadith and the logical structure that follows.
2
Understand the problem with a literal 'Be'
The author argues that if 'Kun' were a real word, it would itself be a created thing requiring another 'Kun', leading to an infinite chain. Identify this argument.
3
See the metaphorical interpretation
The author then offers an alternative: interpreting 'Kun' as a metaphor for instantaneous creation. This avoids the regress and does not imply that 'Kun' itself is temporal.
4
Analyze the analogy with teaching
The author provides an analogy: saying 'I only say to a person: Learn' means that if you say anything, it is that specific statement. Apply this to understand that the verse does not mean the same word 'Be' is used for every thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امرىء ما نوى ) يقتضي قدمها . إذ لو كانت حادثة لكانت واقعة بكلمة كن أخرى سابقة ويتسلسل. وإن جعلتم هذا الكلام لا على حقيقة، بل مجازاً عن سرعة الإيجاد، فلا دلالة فيه على حدوث «كن» - قوله - حقيقته إذ ليس قولنا لشيء من الأشياء عند تكوينه إلا هذا القول وهو لا يقتضي ثبوت هذا القول لكل شيء. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت ما قولي لأحد من الناس عند إرشاده إلا أن أقول له تعلم لم تدل على أنك تقول : تعلم لكل أحد بل على أنك لو قلت في حقه شيئا لم يكن إلا هذا القول
The hadith 'a person [gets] what he intended' implies its pre-eternity. For if it were originated, it would have occurred by another preceding word 'Be' and there would be an infinite regress. And if you interpret this speech not literally but metaphorically for the speed of creation, then there is no indication in it of the temporality of 'Be'. Its reality is that our saying to anything at its coming-into-being is only this saying, and it does not necessitate the existence of this saying for every thing. Do you not see that if you say 'My saying to any person when guiding him is only that I say to him: Learn', this does not indicate that you say 'Learn' to everyone, but rather that if you were to say something concerning him, it would be nothing but this saying.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the command 'Be'. It argues that if we take the command 'Be' as a real word, it would imply an infinite regress because each 'Be' would need a previous 'Be' to bring it into existence. But the correct understanding is that 'Be' is metaphorical for instantaneous creation. Therefore, it does not prove that the word 'Be' is created. The author then clarifies the meaning of the verse 'I only say to a thing "Be" and it is' — it means that God's creative command is single and specific to each thing; it does not mean that each thing receives the same word 'Be'. The analogy with teaching shows that 'I only say to him: Learn' means that if I say anything, it is that, not that I say it to everyone.

Key Terms
كن
الحدوث
القدم
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The command 'Be' in the Quran implies a temporal sequence of words, as if Allah says a real word 'Kun' each time He creates.
Correction: The command 'Kun' is a metaphor for instantaneous creation; it does not indicate a temporal word, and taking it literally leads to absurdities like infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that taking the command 'Be' literally leads to an infinite regress?
A) Because each 'Be' would be a created thing needing another 'Be'
B) Because God cannot speak more than one word
C) Because the Quran only mentions 'Be' once
D) Because the Prophet said so

Key Takeaway
The divine command 'Kun' is a metaphor for instantaneous creation, and understanding it correctly safeguards the pre-eternity of Allah's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken a verse of the Quran or a hadith literally and struggled with its apparent meaning? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your approach to understanding Allah's attributes? Will you seek deeper understanding before forming conclusions about His nature?

The infinite regress argument shows that even a single wrong assumption about divine speech leads to an impossible chain. In your own beliefs about Allah, are there any small assumptions you hold that, if followed to their logical end, would contradict His perfection? What will you do to examine and purify your understanding?
?
If the command 'Be' is only a metaphor for instantaneous creation, then Allah's power is not limited by words. Do you truly rely on His ability to create and command without delay, or do you sometimes feel He is slow to respond to your prayers? How can you train your heart to trust in His instantaneous decree?

The Quran as Originated by Allah
Taftazani's distinction of the Quran's origination by Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran has a special relation to Allah as His direct origination, not merely as an indicator of His eternal speech.

Core Idea
The Quran is Allah's direct creation in the Preserved Tablet, not just a sign.

This passage delves into the nature of the Quran's relationship with Allah. It rejects the common Ash'ari view that the Quran is called God's speech only because it indicates His eternal attribute. Instead, it affirms that Allah directly originated the Quranic script in the Preserved Tablet and the sounds in the angel, giving it a unique and special connection to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully once. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the verse citation at the end.
2
Identify the two views
The passage contrasts two positions: the common Ash'ari view (that the Quran is called God's speech only because it indicates His eternal speech) and the author's approved view (that it has an additional special relation because Allah originated it).
The author uses 'لكن المرضي عندنا' to introduce his view.
3
Understand the special relation
The special relation is explained by Allah originating the forms in the Preserved Tablet and the letters in the angel. This makes the Quran directly from Allah, not just a pointer.
The verse from Surah Al-Buruj is quoted as evidence.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why the author emphasizes 'خصوص التأليف' (particular composition) and that what we recite is the very Quran, not a likeness.
This guards against the misconception that the eternal attribute is in the physical words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لا لمجرد أنه دال على كلامه القديم بل لأنه إنشاءه لرقمه في اللوح أو الحروفه في الملك ويخص العربية منه باسم القرآن وهو المتعارف عند العامة، وفي علم الأصول، وإليه يرجع الخواص المذكورة ثم الصحيح أن المعتبر خصوص التأليف لا يغير المحل مما نقرؤه يكون نفس القرآن لا مثله ويثبت القول بقدر حصوله في اللسان أو المصحف للتأدب ودفع الوهم. هذا لا بمجرد أنه ذاك المشهور في كلام الأصحاب. أنه ليس إطلاق كلام الله تعالى على هذا المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة إلا بمعنى أنه دال على كلامه القديم حتى لو كان مخترع هذه الألفاظ غير الله تعالى لكان هذا الإطلاق بحاله، لكن المرضي عندنا أن له اختصاصاً آخر بالله تعالى، وهو أنه أخبر عنه بأن أوجد أولاً الأشكال في اللوح المحفوظ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ بل هو قرآن مجيد في لوح محفوظ )
His saying: [The Quran is called the speech of Allah] not merely because it is indicative of His eternal speech, but because He originated its writing in the Preserved Tablet or its letters in the angel, and the Arabic part of it is specifically named 'Quran', and this is well-known among the common people and in the science of usul, and to it refer the aforementioned special properties. Then the correct view is that the consideration is the particular arrangement [composition], not the change of locus, so what we recite is the very Quran, not its likeness, and this statement is affirmed to the extent of its occurrence in the tongue or the mushaf as a matter of reverence and to repel misconception. This is not merely the well-known statement of the companions: that the application of 'speech of Allah' to this organized collection of audible letters is only in the sense that it indicates His eternal speech, so that even if the creator of these words were other than Allah, this application would remain. But the approved view with us is that it has another specific relation to Allah, namely that He informed about it that He first originated the forms in the Preserved Tablet, as His saying: 'Nay, it is a glorious Quran in a Preserved Tablet.'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage clarifies the relationship between the recited Quran and the eternal attribute of speech. The author rejects the common Ash'ari position that the audible Quran is called the speech of Allah only because it indicates His eternal speech, and that it would still be so even if someone else created the words. Instead, the author (Taftazani) asserts that the Quran has a special relation to Allah because He directly originated it in the Preserved Tablet, the angel, and finally in the Prophet's tongue. The name 'Quran' is specifically for the Arabic expression. The correct view is that what we recite is the very Quran, not a copy or representation, and we affirm that it is speech of Allah to the extent it is uttered or written, as a form of reverence and to avoid the misconception that the eternal attribute is in the physical word.

الكلام القديم
اللوح المحفوظ
الألفاظ المسموعة

Correction: The Quran has a special relation to Allah because He directly originated its forms in the Preserved Tablet and its letters in the angel.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Quran is not just a sign of Allah's eternal speech; it is directly originated by Him in the Preserved Tablet, giving it a unique divine relationship.

The Identity of the Quran: Type-Unity Across Recitations
Discusses whether the Quran is the same individual utterance or one in kind across recitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari correct view holds that every recitation of the Quran is the very Quran itself, not a mere likeness.

Core Idea
Every recitation is the Quran itself, not just a copy.

In this passage, the text addresses a subtle question: When different people recite the Quran, are they producing the same Quran or merely similar copies? The answer has implications for the honor and unity of Allah's speech. We will see that the correct Ash'ari position affirms that each recitation is the very Quran, unified in type.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two views: one that the Quran is a specific composition in the first tongue, and each recitation is a likeness; the other (correct) that the Quran is one in type, and each recitation is the very Quran.
Notice the key difference: 'mithl' (likeness) vs. 'nafsuhu' (itself).
3
Understand 'type-unity'
The correct view relies on the concept of 'al-wahda al-naw'iyya' (type-unity). This means that the Quran is a universal that applies to every authentic recitation, not tied to a specific instance.
Type-unity preserves the Quran's divine status across all recitations.
4
Reflect on the theological importance
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that the Quran is the same in every recitation? How does this protect the Quran from being seen as multiple or created?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الأصوات في لسان الملك لقوله تعالى : إنه لقول رسول كريم ) (١) الآية أو لسان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لقوله تعالى : نزل به الروح الأمين على قلبك ) (٢). والمنزل على القلب هو المعنى دون اللفظ ثم اختلفوا . فقيل : هو اسم لهذا المؤلف المخصوص القائم بأول لسان اخترعه الله تعالى فيه حتى إن كل أحد بكسبه يكون مثله لا عينه، والأصح (٣) انها اسم له لا من حيث تعين المحل فيكون واحداً بالنوع ويكون ما يقرأه القارىء نفسه لا مثله
Or the sounds in the tongue of the angel as His saying: 'It is the speech of a noble messenger' [81:19] and in the tongue of the Prophet as His saying: 'The Trustworthy Spirit brought it down upon your heart' [26:193]. What is sent down upon the heart is the meaning, not the wording. Then they differed. It was said: It is a name for this particular composition that is present in the first tongue in which Allah invented it, so that everyone by his acquisition produces a likeness of it, not the very same. And the correct view is that it is a name for it not in terms of the specification of the locus, so it is one in type, and what the reader recites is the very Quran, not its likeness.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of how the Quran is related to Allah. It mentions that the Quran was revealed in sounds either from the angel or the Prophet's tongue. The meaning is what is sent down to the heart, not the wording. Then it presents two views: (1) Some say the Quran is the first specific composition that Allah created in the first tongue, and every subsequent recitation is a likeness (mithl) of it, not the same. (2) The correct view (al-ashah) is that the name 'Quran' applies to the composition regardless of the particular locus, so it is one in type (naw'iyya). This means that each recitation is the very Quran itself, not a mere representation. This is a key Ash'ari position to maintain the honor and unity of the Quran.

Key Terms
المؤلف المخصوص
النوعية
الوحدة النوعية
المثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that each recited Quran is a separate created thing rather than the same Quran.
Correction: The correct view affirms that every authentic recitation is the very Quran, unified in type, not a separate created entity.

Checkpoint

A) The reader produces a likeness of the original Quran
B) The reader's recitation is the very Quran itself
C) The reader's recitation is a different Quran
D) The Quran only exists in the Preserved Tablet

Key Takeaway
The Quran is one in type (naw'iyya), so every authentic recitation is the Quran itself, not a mere representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The correct view is that every recitation is the Quran itself. Does your heart register that each time you recite? If not, what is blocking that belief from transforming your recitation?
?
This lesson guards against the misconception that the Quran is a created thing. Have you ever, even momentarily, thought of the Quran as just a book like any other? How can you strengthen your conviction that it is Allah's eternal speech?

The Three Senses of 'Quran is the Speech of Allah'
Summarizing the different senses: by type-identity, by individual-identity, and as the eternal attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct theological senses in which the Quran is called the speech of Allah and why it is important to distinguish them.

Core Idea
The Quran is Allah's speech in three ways: as type, as individual, and as eternal attribute.

The phrase 'the Quran is the speech of Allah' is used in multiple senses, and confusing them leads to theological error. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies three distinct meanings: type-unity, individual-unity, and the actual eternal attribute. Let's unpack them and see how they protect the transcendence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses three 'what is said' phrases to introduce each sense.
2
Identify the three senses
Find the three distinct ways the Quran is called the speech of Allah: by type-unity, by individual-unity, and as the eternal attribute. Understand each one.
Type-unity means every copy shares the same type; individual-unity means a specific individual instance; eternal attribute is the uncreated speech.
3
Note the prohibition against inherence
The passage states that theologians forbid saying Allah's speech inheres in a tongue or mushaf, even if one means the verbal expression. Why? To avoid the misconception that the eternal attribute dwells in creation.
This is a matter of reverence (ta'dib) for the divine.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. How does it protect the transcendence of Allah and prevent anthropomorphism?
This connects to the broader goal of kalam: to affirm tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فما يقال إن المكتوب في كل مصحف والمقروء بكل لسان كلام الله تعالى. فباعتبار الوحدة النوعية. ومايقال إنه حكاية كلام الله ) ومماثل له ، إنما الكلام (4) المخترع في لسان الملك. فباعتبار الوحدة الشخصية ، وما يقال ان كلام الله تعالى ليس قائماً بلسان أو قلب ولا حالاً في مصحف أو لوح ، فيراد به الكلام الحقيقي الذي هو الصفة الأزلية، ومنعوا من القول بحلول كلامه في لسان أو قلب مصحف، وإن كان المراد هو اللفظي(ه) رعاية للتأديب، واحترازاً عن ذهاب الوهم إلى الحقيقي الأزلي.
In summary: As for what is said that 'what is written in every mushaf and recited by every tongue is the speech of Allah' — this is by consideration of type-unity. And what is said that 'it is a narration (hikaya) of the speech of Allah and similar to it' — that refers to the speech invented in the tongue of the angel, by consideration of individual-unity. And what is said that 'the speech of Allah does not subsist in a tongue or a heart, nor does it inhere in a mushaf or a tablet' — what is meant by it is the real speech that is the eternal attribute. And they forbade saying that His speech inheres in a tongue, heart, or mushaf, even if what is meant is the verbal speech, as a matter of reverence and to avoid the misconception that it refers to the eternal [attribute].
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a summary of three distinct ways in which the phrase 'Kalam Allah' is used in theological discourse. First, the Quran as written and recited is called the speech of Allah by virtue of type-unity (al-wihda al-naw'iyya), meaning every instance shares the same type. Second, when it is said that the Quran is a 'narration' or 'likeness' of Allah's speech, this refers to the speech invented by Allah in the tongue of the angel (Jibril), and this is by individual-unity (al-wihda al-shakhsiyya), meaning it is a specific, unique entity. Third, when it is said that Allah's speech does not reside in any created thing, this refers to the true eternal attribute of speech. The theologians forbade saying that Allah's speech inheres in a tongue or mushaf, even if one means the verbal expression, because it could lead to the misconception that the eternal attribute dwells in creation. This is a matter of reverence (ta'dib).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the eternal attribute of speech literally resides in physical copies of the Quran or in the reciter's voice.
Correction: The eternal attribute is uncreated and does not inhere in creation. The Quran we recite is a created expression that points to the eternal speech but is not identical with it.

Checkpoint

C) The Quran is a story told by Allah

Key Takeaway
The Quran is called the speech of Allah in three distinct senses—type-unity, individual-unity, and eternal attribute—and it is essential to keep them separate to avoid theological error and preserve divine transcendence.

?
The theologians forbade saying 'the Quran inheres in the heart' even as a convention, lest the mind slip into thinking the eternal attribute is literally inside you. Is there any casual phrase you use about your faith (e.g., 'I have Allah in my heart') that might, if unchecked, lead to theological carelessness? What precise language could you adopt instead?

Distinguishing Unity of Type and Individual Unity in Kalam Allah
Al-Taftazani explains different views on the meaning of 'kalam Allah' and distinguishes between unity of type and individual unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between unity of type (wahdat naw'iyya) and individual unity (wahdat shakhsiyya) as applied to the speech of Allah, and how different interpretations of 'kalam Allah' relate to these concepts.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is Allah's speech in type, but its recitations are not identical in individual existence.

Al-Taftazani delves into a nuanced debate about the nature of the Qur'an as the speech of Allah. He explains that the term 'kalam Allah' can be understood in multiple ways, leading to the distinction between unity of type (wahdat naw'iyya) and individual unity (wahdat shakhsiyya). This passage clarifies how the written and recited Qur'an relates to the eternal attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with the literal translation to get an overview of the different views presented.
2
Identify the two key perspectives
Notice how the passage first discusses the disagreement about whether kalam is the composition created in the first tongue or something shared in kind.
3
Understand the distinction
Focus on the summary at the end where the author explains the three statements and their perspectives (wahda naw'iyya and shakhsiyya).
4
Apply the concept
Reflect on how this distinction preserves both the divine origin of the Qur'an and the multiplicity of its recitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الأصوات في لسان الملك لقوله تعالى: إنه لقول رسول كريم الآية أو لسان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لقوله تعالى: نزل به الروح الأمين على قلبك. والمنزل على القلب هو المعنى دون اللفظ ثم اختلفوا. فقيل: هو اسم لهذا المؤلف المخصوص القائم بأول لسان اخترعه الله تعالى فيه حتى إن كل أحد بكسبه يكون مثله لا عينه، والأصح أنها اسم له لا من حيث تعين المحل فيكون واحداً بالنوع ويكون ما يقرأه القارئ نفسه لا مثله، وهكذا الحكم في كل شيء أو كتاب نسب إلى مؤلفه، وعلى التقديرين، فقد تجعل اسماً للمجموع بحيث لا يصدق على البعض، وقد يجعل اسماً لمعنى كلي صادق على المجموع، وعلى كل بعض من أبعاضه، ولهذا المقام زيادة توضيح في شرح التنقيح. وبالجملة: فما يقال إن المكتوب في كل مصحف والمقروء بكل لسان كلام الله تعالى. فباعتبار الوحدة النوعية. وما يقال إنه حكاية كلام الله ومماثل له، إنما الكلام المخترع في لسان الملك. فباعتبار الوحدة الشخصية، وما يقال إن كلام الله تعالى ليس قائماً بلسان أو قلب ولا حالاً في مصحف أو لوح، فيراد به الكلام الحقيقي الذي هو الصفة الأزلية، ومنعوا من القول بحلول كلامه في لسان أو قلب مصحف، وإن كان المراد هو اللفظي رعاية للتأديب، واحترازاً عن ذهاب الوهم إلى الحقيقي الأزلي.
Or the sounds on the tongue of the angel, according to His statement: 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger' (81:19) or the tongue of the Prophet, peace be upon him, according to His statement: 'The Trustworthy Spirit has brought it down upon your heart' (26:193). And what is sent down upon the heart is the meaning, not the wording. Then they disagreed. Some said it is a name for this particular composition established in the first tongue that Allah created, such that everyone by his acquisition produces something like it, not the same. The more correct [view] is that it is a name for it not by virtue of the specification of the locus, so it is one in kind, and what the reader recites is itself, not its like. And the same ruling applies to every thing or book attributed to its author. On both suppositions, it may be made a name for the whole such that it does not apply to the part, and it may be made a name for a universal meaning applying to the whole and to every part of its parts. There is further clarification of this topic in Sharh al-Tanqih. In sum: what is said is that what is written in every mushaf and recited in every tongue is the speech of Allah — from the perspective of unity of type; and what is said that it is a narration of the speech of Allah and similar to it is the speech invented in the tongue of the angel — from the perspective of individual unity; and what is said that the speech of Allah does not subsist in a tongue or heart nor inhere in a mushaf or tablet — what is meant by it is the real speech which is the eternal attribute. And they forbade saying that His speech inheres in a tongue or heart or mushaf, even if the intended meaning is the verbal [speech], out of respect and as a precaution against the imagination going to the eternal real [speech].
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'kalam Allah' (speech of Allah). The author begins by describing two possible interpretations based on Quranic verses: that the speech of Allah is the sounds on the tongue of the angel (Jibril) or the tongue of the Prophet. He notes that what is sent down upon the heart is the meaning (ma'na), not the wording (lafz). Then he presents the disagreement: some said 'kalam' is a name for the specific composition created in the first tongue, such that each person's recitation is like it (mithluhu) but not identical (la 'aynihi). The more correct view (al-ashah) is that it is a name for it not by virtue of the specification of the locus, so it is one in kind (wahid bil-naw'), and the reader recites the very same thing (nafsuhu), not its like. This is analogous to any book attributed to its author. On either view, 'kalam' may apply to the whole or to every part as a universal meaning. The author then summarizes three common statements: (1) that the written and recited Qur'an is Allah's speech — this is from the perspective of unity of type (wahda naw'iyya); (2) that it is a narration (hikaya) of Allah's speech — this refers to the speech invented in the angel's tongue, from the perspective of individual unity (wahda shakhsiyya); (3) that Allah's speech does not subsist in a tongue, heart, mushaf, or tablet — this refers to the real eternal speech (al-kalam al-haqiqi al-azali). The scholars forbade saying that His speech inheres in physical objects, even if one means the verbal speech (al-lafzi), as a precaution and out of respect, to prevent misunderstanding the eternal attribute as subject to inherence.

Key Terms
كلام الله
وحدة نوعية
وحدة شخصية
معنى
لفظ
صفة أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an as recited is either completely identical to the eternal speech or completely different and merely a creation.
Correction: The correct view distinguishes between unity of type: the recited Qur'an is the same in kind (true speech of Allah) but not identical in individual existence; it is not a separate creation nor is it the eternal attribute itself.

Checkpoint
According to the more correct view (الأصح), what is the relationship between the reader's recitation and the speech of Allah?
A) It is identical in every way.
B) It is a different creation each time.
C) It is the same in kind (one in type).
D) It is a narration of the eternal speech.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between unity of type and individual unity preserves both the divine origin of the Qur'an and the multiplicity of its recitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Ash'ari View: The Eternal Meaning and the Created Recitation
Al-Taftazani explains that the Arabic Qur'an is the eternal meaning described metaphorically by attributes of sounds and letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between the eternal meaning of the Qur'an and the created recitation and writing, and how revelation is perceived without spatial transfer.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's eternal meaning is heard through created sounds.

Al-Taftazani presents the Ash'ari defense that the Arabic Qur'an, while described as recited and heard, is actually the eternal meaning of Allah. He clarifies that the recitation and writing are created acts, while the meaning understood from them is eternal. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for how we understand the revelation of the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
The common linguistic metaphor
Read the opening quote and explanation that applying the attribute of the signifier to the signified is normal in speech, like saying 'I heard this meaning'.
This sets up the rhetorical justification for the Ash'ari position.
2
The Ash'ari answer defined
Understand that the Arabic Qur'an referred to in scripture is the eternal meaning described metaphorically by attributes of sounds and letters.
The eternal meaning is the true referent; the attributes are metaphorical.
3
Created recitation vs. eternal meaning
Note the distinction: recitation and writing are created acts, subject to commands and prohibitions, but the meaning understood from them is eternal and not physically located.
This resolves the apparent contradiction of an eternal Qur'an being recited in time.
4
Revelation without transfer
Grasp how Jibril perceived the eternal speech at the Lote-tree and communicated its meaning to the Prophet without moving the essence of the speech.
Revelation is about conveying meaning, not spatial movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله .. قال : وإجراء صفة الدال على المدلول شايع مثل : سمعت هذا المعنى وقرأته وكتبته واختصاص موسى عليه السلام بالمكالمة من حيث أنه سمع بلا صوت وحرف كما يرى في الآخرة بلا كم وكيف أو أنه سمع بصوت من جميع الجهات أو من جهة بلا اكتساب . هذا جواب آخر لأصحابنا تقريره أن المراد بالمذكور العربي المنزل المقروء المسموع المكتوب إلى آخر الخواص هو المعنى القديم، إلا أنه وصف بما هو من صفات الأصوات والحروف الدالة عليه مجازاً أو وصفاً للمدلول بصفة الدال عليه كما يقال : سمعت هذا المعنى من فلان وقرأته في بعض الكتب، وكتبته بيدي. وهذا ما قال أصحابنا : إن القراءة حادثة أعني أصوات القارىء التي هي من اكتسابه يؤمر بها تارة إيجاباً أو ندباً ومنهى عنها حيناً وكذا الكتابة أعني حركات الكاتب، والأحرف المرسومة، وأما المقروء بالقراءة المكتوب في المصاحف المحفوظ في الصدور، المسموع بالآذان فقديم ، ليس حالاً في لسان ولا قلب ولا مصحف، لأن المراد المعلوم بالقراءة المفهوم من الخطوط ومن الأصوات المسموعة ، وكذا المنزل. إذ معنى الإنزال أن جبريل عليه الصلاة والسلام أدرك كلام الله تعالى وهو في مقامه ثم نزل إلى الأرض وأفهم النبي ما فهمه عند سدرة المنتهى من غير نقل لذات الكلام.
His statement: He said: And applying the attribute of the signifier to the signified is common, such as saying: 'I heard this meaning', 'I read it', 'I wrote it', and the special distinction of Moses, peace be upon him, with the speaking, in that he heard without sound and letter, just as he will see in the Hereafter without quantity and quality, or that he heard with a sound from all directions, or from one direction without acquisition. This is another answer of our companions, the gist of which is that what is intended by the mentioned Arabic, revealed, recited, heard, written, etc. is the eternal meaning, except that it is described with what are attributes of the sounds and letters indicating it metaphorically, or describing the signified by the attribute of the signifier, as it is said: 'I heard this meaning from so-and-so', 'I read it in some books', 'I wrote it with my hand'. And this is what our companions said: that the recitation is created, meaning the sounds of the reader which are from his acquisition, sometimes commanded as obligatory or recommended, and sometimes forbidden; and likewise the writing, meaning the movements of the writer and the inscribed letters. As for what is recited through recitation, written in the mushafs, preserved in the chests, heard with the ears, it is eternal, not inhering in a tongue, heart, or mushaf, because what is intended is the known meaning from the recitation understood from the lines and from the heard sounds. And likewise the revelation. For the meaning of revelation is that Jibril, peace be upon him, perceived the speech of Allah while he was in his station, then he descended to the earth and made the Prophet understand what he understood at the Lote-tree of the Utmost Boundary, without transferring the essence of the speech.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the Ash'ari position on the nature of the Qur'an. It begins with a quote from the original text (al-Maqasid) and the author's comment: applying the attribute of the signifier (al-dal) to the signified (al-madlul) is common in language. For example, we say 'I heard this meaning' even though meaning is not literally heard; we attribute the action to the meaning because it is understood from the sounds. Similarly, the eternal meaning (al-ma'na al-qadim) that is the speech of Allah is described with attributes of sounds and letters metaphorically. The author then gives the Ash'ari answer: the referred Arabic Qur'an (revealed, recited, heard, written) is the eternal meaning, but it is described by attributes of sounds and letters metaphorically. The recitation (qira'a) itself is created (haditha), consisting of the sounds acquired by the reader, and is subject to commands and prohibitions. Likewise, writing (kitaba) consists of the writer's movements and inscribed letters and is created. However, what is recited through recitation – the meaning understood from the sounds and lines – is eternal (qadim). It does not inhere in any physical locus. The author then explains revelation (inzal): Jibril perceived the eternal speech of Allah in his station (the Lote-tree), then descended and made the Prophet understand what he understood, without transferring the essence (dhat) of the speech. Thus, the eternal speech is not spatially transferred, but its meaning is communicated.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the recitation (qira'a) itself is eternal.
Correction: The recitation is created; only the meaning understood from it is eternal.

C) It is a part of the eternal speech.
D) It is forbidden to recite it.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris teach that the Qur'an's eternal meaning is conveyed through created recitation and writing, but the essence of Allah's speech remains transcendent.

When you recite the Qur'an, do you pause to connect with the eternal meaning beyond the sounds, or does your mind wander to the letters and rules? What would your recitation look like if you focused entirely on the meaning as it comes from Allah?

The Ash'aris teach that the very essence of Allah's speech is beyond sounds and letters. Are you truly comfortable with the idea that the Qur'an you hold is not literally the words of Allah in their essence? Examine your heart—do you feel a subtle attachment to the physical book that rivals your attachment to its meaning?
?
If revelation is only the meaning conveyed by Jibril, not the speech itself, then how closely do you align your understanding of the Qur'an with the intent of the One who spoke it? Are you reading the Qur'an as a message from Allah to you personally, or as a historical document? Respond with a sincere intention to change.

Eternal Speech Described Metaphorically
The author presents the Ash'ari answer that eternal meaning is described by attributes of sounds and letters metaphorically.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school explains that terms like 'recited' and 'written' refer metaphorically to the eternal divine speech.

Core Idea
Eternal speech is described through temporal attributes metaphorically.

In this passage, the author presents a key Ash'ari response to the question of how eternal divine speech can be described as 'Arabic', 'sent down', 'recited', etc. He explains that these attributes belong to the sounds and letters that indicate the speech, not to the speech itself. This metaphorical use preserves the eternity of the speech while accounting for its temporal expressions.

Sound and letter as seen in the afterlife without quantity or quality, or that it is heard with sound from all directions or from a direction without acquisition. This is another answer of our companions: its exposition is that what is meant by the mentioned Arabic, sent down, recited, heard, written, and the rest of the specific attributes is the eternal meaning, except that it is described by what is among the attributes of the sounds and letters that indicate it metaphorically, or a description of the indicated by the attribute of the indicator, as it is said: 'I heard this meaning from so-and-so, and I read it in some books, and I wrote it with my hand.' And this is what our companions said: that recitation is created, I mean the sounds of the reciter which are from his acquisition, commanded at times obligatorily or recommendatorily and forbidden at other times, and likewise writing, I mean the movements of the writer and the drawn letters. As for what is recited by recitation, written in the manuscripts, preserved in the breasts, heard by the ears, it is eternal, not subsisting in a tongue, nor a heart, nor a manuscript, because what is meant is the known thing by recitation, understood from the lines and from the audible sounds, and likewise the sent down. For the meaning of sending down is that Gabriel (peace be upon him) perceived the speech of God Most High while he was in his station, then descended to the earth and made the Prophet understand what he understood at the Lote Tree of the Utmost Boundary without transferring the essence of the speech.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation by the author's school (the Ash'aris) of how the eternal divine speech relates to its temporal expressions. It begins by referencing a previous discussion about whether God's speech is heard as sound and letter or seen without quantity/quality, then presents the school's answer. The key point is that terms like 'Arabic,' 'sent down,' 'recited,' 'heard,' 'written' refer to the eternal meaning (al-ma'na al-qadim), but that meaning is described metaphorically using attributes of sounds and letters that indicate it. The author gives the analogy of saying 'I heard this meaning from so-and-so,' where the meaning (ma'na) is attributed to the sound that conveys it. Then the author distinguishes between creation (hadith) and eternal (qadim): the sounds of the reciter (qira'a) are created, as are the movements of the writer and the letters; but what is recited (al-maqru'), written in the mushafs, preserved in hearts, and heard by ears is the eternal meaning, which does not inhere in any physical substrate. Finally, the author explains the concept of sending down (inzal): Gabriel perceived the eternal speech in his station and then conveyed its understanding (fahm) to the Prophet without transferring the essence (dhat) of the speech itself. This preserves the eternity of the Qur'an while accounting for temporal manifestations.

Key Terms
المعنى القديم
مجاز
صفات الأصوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Qur'an as recited is entirely created or that the Arabic words are the eternal speech itself.
Correction: The Ash'aris distinguish between the eternal meaning and the created sounds/letters that indicate it. The attributes like 'recited' apply metaphorically to the meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the author's companions, what is the relationship between the eternal meaning and the attributes of sounds and letters?
A) The eternal meaning is literally the sounds and letters.
B) The eternal meaning is described by the attributes of sounds and letters metaphorically.
C) The sounds and letters are eternal.
D) The eternal meaning is created when recited.

Key Takeaway
Eternal speech is described through temporal attributes metaphorically to maintain its eternity while explaining its temporal expression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite the Qur'an daily, forming sounds and letters with your tongue. How does the knowledge that these sounds are a metaphorical expression of God's eternal speech transform the way you approach each verse? Will you now seek to connect more deeply with the eternal meaning beyond the words?
?
The passage compares saying 'I heard this meaning from so-and-so' to attributing eternal meaning to temporal sounds. When you listen to the Qur'an, do you actually feel you are hearing God's eternal speech, or does the metaphor create a distance? How would you explain this to someone who struggles with the concept?
?
If the recited Qur'an is described metaphorically by temporal attributes, then your experience of the Qur'an is mediated by metaphor. Does this unsettle you, or does it inspire you to reflect more on the reality behind the metaphor? What concrete step can you take this week to connect with the eternal meaning rather than just the sounds?

Created Recitation and Eternal Meaning
The author distinguishes created recitation/writing from eternal meaning and explains inzal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between created recitation/writing and the eternal meaning, and the concept of sending down (inzal) of the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Recitation is created, but what is recited is eternal; sending down conveys understanding, not essence.

In this continuation, the author specifies that the sounds of recitation (qira'a) and the movements of writing are created and from human acquisition, while what is recited and written—the meaning—is eternal and does not reside in any physical medium. He then explains the sending down (inzal) of the Qur'an as Gabriel's perception and conveyance of understanding, not the transport of the speech essence.

The author explains that sending down means Gabriel perceived the eternal speech and conveyed its understanding to the Prophet without transferring the essence.

Why Moses is the Speech of God: Objection and Three Answers
Objection and three scholarly answers on Moses' distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection against Moses' unique title is raised and how three different scholarly responses preserve his special status through miraculous hearing.

Core Idea
Moses heard divine speech in a way that breaks natural custom.

The title 'Kalim Allah' (the one to whom God spoke) might seem unremarkable if every believer hears God's words in the Qur'an. This passage addresses that objection by presenting three models of how Moses heard God's speech—each involving a miraculous mode beyond ordinary hearing. As you read, notice how each answer maintains the uniqueness of Moses' encounter while upholding the nature of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the premise: if divine speech is either audible letters or understood meaning, then everyone hears it. So why is Moses special?
The objection targets the very basis of Moses' title.
2
Study the first answer (al-Ghazali)
Identify the claim: Moses heard eternal speech without sound or letter, by breaking custom. Compare it to seeing Allah in the afterlife.
This answer relies on the possibility of direct perception of the attribute.
3
Study the second answer
Notice that this answer says Moses heard sound from all directions, also contrary to custom.
It still involves a miraculous mode.
4
Study the third answer (al-Maturidi and al-Isfarayini)
Understand that Moses heard sound from one direction but the sound was directly created by Allah, not acquired by any creature.
This emphasizes the honor given to Moses.
5
Compare the three answers
Ask yourself: what do they have in common? All involve khariq al-'ada (breaking of custom). How do they differ?
The common element preserves Moses' unique status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا أريد بكلام الله تعالى المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة من غير اعتبار تعين المحل فكل أحد منا يسمع كلام الله تعالى، وكذا إذا أريد به المعنى الأزلي، وأريد بسماعه فهمه من الأصوات المسموعة ! فما وجه اختصاص موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام بأنه كليم الله تعالى. فإن قلنا فيه أوجه : أحدها وهو اختيار الإمام حجة الإسلام رحمه الله ، أنه كلامه الأزلي بلا صوت ولا حرف، كما ترى ذاته في الآخرة بلا كم، ولا كيف وهذا على مذهب من يجوز تعلق الرؤية والسماع بكل موجود حتى الذات والصفات، لكن سماع غير الصوت والحرف لا يكون إلا بطريق خرق العادة. وثانيها : أنه سمعه بصوت من جميع الجهات على خلاف ما هو العادة . وثالثها : أنه سمع من جهة لكن بصوت غير مكتسب للعباد على ما هو شأن سماعنا، وحاصله أنه أكرم موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام، فأفهمه كلامه بصوت تولى بخلقه من غير كسب لأحد من خلقه، وإلى هذا ذهب الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي ، والأستاذ أبو إسحق الاسفرايني .
For if it is said: If by the speech of God Most High is meant the composition of audible letters without considering determination of place, then every one of us hears the speech of God Most High; and likewise if by it is meant the eternal meaning, and by hearing it is meant understanding it from audible sounds, then what is the specific distinction of Moses (peace be upon him) in being the interlocutor of God (Kalim Allah)? Then we say concerning it there are aspects: One of them—and it is the choice of Imam Hujjat al-Islam (may God have mercy on him)—that it is His eternal speech without sound or letter, as you see His essence in the afterlife without quantity or quality, and this is according to the school of those who allow sight and hearing to attach to every existent, even the essence and attributes, but hearing other than sound and letter only occurs by way of breaking the custom. The second: that he heard it with sound from all directions contrary to what is customary. The third: that he heard from a direction but with a sound not acquired by the servants, unlike the manner of our hearing, and its conclusion is that He honored Moses (peace be upon him) and made him understand His speech with a sound that He undertook to create without acquisition by any of His creatures. To this went the Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its three answers. The objection is raised against the view that divine speech is either the heard letters or the eternal meaning understood from sounds: if that is so, then every believer hears God's speech in the Qur'an, so why is Moses uniquely called Kalim Allah (the one to whom God spoke)? The objection presupposes that Moses' distinction requires a special mode of hearing. Then the author gives three answers (wujuh). The first, from Imam al-Ghazali, is that Moses heard the eternal speech without sound or letter, a direct apprehension akin to seeing God in the afterlife—this occurs by breaking the natural custom (khariq al-'ada). The second answer is that he heard it with sound but from all directions, also contrary to custom. The third answer, attributed to al-Maturidi and al-Isfarayini, is that he heard it from one direction with a sound directly created by God, not acquired by any creature, thus a special honor to Moses. Each answer maintains that Moses' hearing was miraculous, preserving his unique status.

Key Terms
كليم الله
كلام الأزلي
خرق العادة
سماع
موسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if divine speech is heard in the Qur'an, then Moses had no unique privilege.
Correction: The answers show that the mode of hearing—miraculous and beyond ordinary custom—is what distinguishes Moses.

Checkpoint
What is common to all three answers regarding how Moses heard God's speech?
A) He heard it with sound and letters.
B) He heard it in a way that breaks the natural custom.
C) He heard it from all directions.
D) He heard it without understanding.

Key Takeaway
Moses' title 'Kalim Allah' is preserved because his hearing of divine speech occurred through a miraculous break from natural custom, regardless of whether sound or direction was involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God's speech can be heard in ways that break all natural laws, have you ever silenced your heart to the possibility that He might speak to you in a manner beyond your expectations? What would it take for you to truly listen for a divine address that shatters your categories?

Are you living as if God's grace can single you out for a privileged encounter, or do you assume His gifts are always evenly distributed? Confess the pride that makes you resent His sovereign choices.
?
The third answer says the sound Moses heard was 'not acquired by any creature'. When you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as a human recitation or as the very speech of God that comes to you without any creature's acquisition? Make a concrete change in how you listen to the Qur'an this week, acknowledging its divine origin beyond human voices.

The Verbal Nature of Disagreement on Hearing Non-Sound
Al-Ustadh notes the agreement and verbal difference on hearing non-sound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent disagreement on hearing non-sound is merely verbal, not a real theological conflict.

Core Idea
The dispute over hearing non-sound is verbal, not doctrinal.

In this short but crucial passage, al-Ustadh clarifies a point that could otherwise cause confusion. He reveals that what seems like a disagreement among scholars about whether non-sound can be heard is actually a verbal difference—both sides agree on the reality, but one group extends the term 'heard' by analogy. This lesson unpacks that reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the key terms and structure sink in without over-analysing.
Notice the phrase 'اختلاف لفظي' — verbal difference — is the climax.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage mentions two groups: those who definitively say non-sound cannot be heard, and those who say it can be called 'heard' because the meaning is known through sound. Identify each.
3
Understand the verbal vs. real distinction
Al-Ustadh says the difference is 'lafzi' (verbal) not 'ma'nawi' (real). This means both groups agree on the underlying fact—non-sound is not directly heard—but they differ on whether to call it 'heard' by extension.
This is a key methodological principle in kalam: not every apparent dispute is a real one.
4
Reflect on the reconciliation
Consider how this passage resolves a potential conflict. By labeling it a verbal difference, al-Ustadh preserves agreement on core doctrine while acknowledging alternative terminology.
This shows the importance of precise language in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الأستاذ : اتفقوا على أنه لا يمكن سماع غير الصوت إلا أن منهم من بَتَّ القول بذلك ، ومنهم من قال لما كان المعنى القائم بالنفس معلوماً بواسطة سماع الصوت كان مسموعاً ، فالاختلاف لفظي لا معنوي - قوله -.
Al-Ustadh said: They agreed that it is impossible to hear other than sound, except that among them are those who definitively stated that, and among them are those who said: since the meaning subsisting in the soul is known by means of hearing the sound, it is [considered] heard. So the difference is verbal, not real. — end of his statement.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark from al-Ustadh (probably Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) about the previous discussion on whether non-sound (i.e., the eternal meaning) can be heard. He states that all scholars agree that it is impossible to hear anything other than sound (la yumkinu sama'u ghayr al-sawt). However, some scholars (like the author's school) take that as a definitive ruling—non-sound simply cannot be heard. Others (like al-Isfarayini's own view?) say that since the meaning (al-ma'na al-qa'im bi al-nafs) is known by means of hearing the sound, it can be called 'heard' metaphorically or derivatively. Therefore, the difference of opinion is merely verbal (lafzi) and not real (ma'nawi). This reconciles the positions: both agree in practice, but differ in terminology about whether the meaning is 'heard' directly or indirectly.

Key Terms
سماع
الصوت
المعنى القائم بالنفس
اختلاف لفظي
اختلاف معنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there is a real theological disagreement between scholars about whether non-sound can be heard.
Correction: Al-Ustadh clarifies it is only a verbal difference: one group simply refuses to call it 'heard', the other allows it by extension, but both agree non-sound is not heard directly.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ustadh, what is the nature of the disagreement about hearing non-sound?
A) It is a real disagreement about whether God's speech can be heard.
B) It is a verbal disagreement because he who calls it 'heard' does so by extension.
C) It is a disagreement about the possibility of hearing without ears.
D) It is a disagreement about the meaning of 'agreement'.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement over hearing non-sound is merely verbal — both sides agree on the reality but differ in terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you debate matters of faith, how often do you find yourself arguing over words while actually agreeing on the substance? Can you recall a specific recent example? Commit to checking whether a disagreement is merely verbal before it escalates.
?
This lesson shows that even great scholars avoided unnecessary conflict by clarifying terminology. Is your heart willing to let go of being 'right' about words when the meaning is agreed? What pride might you need to set aside?
?
If God’s attribute of hearing is understood by all to be perfect and without flaw, why do we sometimes insist on our limited human analogies? How can you train yourself to submit to the agreed meanings rather than personal formulations?
